Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Slave's Inherent Capacity: Ahliyyat al-Takallum and al-Dhimmah
Defines the slave's legal capacity through speech and dhimmah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two bases of the slave's legal capacity (ahliyyat al-takallum and dhimmah) and explain how they establish eligibility for disposal (tasarruf) and hand-ownership (milk al-yad) in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The slave's intellect and dhimmah make him eligible for transactions; the master's right only restricts, not nullifies capacity.

In Hanafi usul, a slave is not devoid of legal capacity. This lesson examines the two foundations—capacity for speech (ahliyyat al-takallum) and the dhimmah—that ground the slave's ability to engage in disposal and hold hand-ownership. Understanding these concepts clarifies why the slave can transact with permission and how his capacity differs from full ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases
Locate in the passage the two elements that the Hanafi school cites as proof of the slave's capacity: ahliyyat al-takallum (capacity for speech) and al-dhimmah (legal personality).
These are the core of the Hanafi argument.
2
Define ahliyyat al-takallum
Understand that this capacity arises from intellect (aqil), which slavery does not impair. The text uses the example that a slave's narration is binding, proving his speech is valid.
Intellect remains intact regardless of slave status.
3
Define al-dhimmah
Al-dhimmah is the legal personality that makes a person fit to be addressed with rulings (khitab). The slave has a dhimmah because he is commanded with prayer, fasting, etc.
Even though diminished, the dhimmah exists.
4
Explain how these lead to eligibility
From the presence of valid speech capacity and dhimmah, the text concludes that the slave is eligible (ahl) for disposal and hand-ownership. These are not created by the master's permission; they are inherent.
The master's permission only removes the restriction (hajr).
5
Distinguish eligibility from permission
The text emphasizes that the master's permission does not establish eligibility; it only lifts the preventer (mani) caused by the master's right over the slave's neck and benefits.
This distinction is crucial for the Shafi'i dispute.

فالرق في معتق البعض كامل فهو كالمكاتب عنده وحر عندهما، ثم الرق ينصف الكرامات، ولهذا يحل له نكاح اثنتين ويكون طلاق الأمة تطليقتين وعدتها حيضتين بالنص الذي رواه الدارقطني : طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان ويكون قسمها نصف قسم الحرة، ويكون حلها قبل الحرة ومنفردة دون بعدها ومعها وكذا ينصف الحدود، فحد العبد نصف حد الحر، لأن الغرم بالغنم والرق يمنع مالكية المال لأنه مملوك نفسه ذليل مهان، فكيف يكون مالكاً، بخلاف مالكية النفس فإنها تبقى على أصل الحرية فتصح أقاريره بالحدود والقصاص والسرقة هالكاً وقائماً في المأذون وفي المحجور عند الإمام فيقطع ويرد المال، وعند أبي يوسف : يقطع ولا يردّ، وعند الإمام محمد لا يقطع ولا يرد هذا كله إذا كذبه المولى، ولا يصح إقرار المولى في حقه بحد أو قصاص، وكذا يملك هو ملك النكاح، وإنما يحتاج إلى الإذن لأنه يجب به المال في الذمة والرقبة هي ملك المولى، ولذا لا يملك طلاق امرأته، وكذا يملك دم نفسه فلا يحل للسيد قتله ولا إتلاف عضو من أعضائه، ولذا يقتل الحرّ به عندنا، وأما عند غيرنا فإنما لا يقتل لأجل كرامة الحر، والرق أيضاً يمنع مالكية المنافع، بل المنافع كلها للسيد إلا ما استثنى منها كالصوم والصلاة، فلا يخرج للجمعة والعيدين والحج إلا بإذن السيد وكذا الجهاد لا يخرج له إلا بإذنه أو بإذن الشرع عند النفير العام، وإنما يصح أمانة مأذوناً، لأنه مالك للغنيمة، فينفذ عليه بالذات وعلى غيره بالتبع، والرق يمنع الولايات فلا تصح الشهادة على أحد ولا قضاؤه ولا حكومته، وكذا إمارته، والرق ينقص الذمة فلا يجب على ذمته شيء إلا بضم مالية رقبته إليه فلا يجب بإقراره المال في الحال، إلا في المأذون للضرورة، وتجب الغرامات لأجل الجنايات في الذمة، فتؤدى فتباع رقبته إلا أن يفدي المولى، وكذا تباع رقبة المأذون فيما بقي من الدين بعد الأداء من الكسب أو لم يكن له كسب ولا تجوز تبرعاته من أكسابه لأنه ملك المولى أو الدائنين، ولا تقبل هديته إلا اليسير بالنص، ولا يجوز له التسري من إمائه وإن كان مكاتباً .

(العبد أهل للتصرف وملك اليد عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله ، فإنه عنده ليس أهلاً لهما، وإنما له التصرف وملك اليد خلافة من السيد لنا أنهما أي ملك التصرف وملك اليد إنما يكونان (بأهلية التكلم) وصحته (والذمة) وهي كون الإنسان صالحاً لأن يخاطب بالأحكام ( والأولى) أي أهلية التكلم إنما تكون بالعقل وهو لا يختل بالرق) بالضرورة (ولذا كانت روايته ملزمة للعمل للخلق) ولو لم يكن كلامه معتبراً بكون عقله رقبته وفي منافعه ولو جاز له التصرف من غير إذنه صارت الرقبة هالكة في الدين ولا يقدر على الاستخدام فيستضر به (فإذنه فك الحجر ورفع المانع عن صحة التصرف (لا إثبات الأهلية كما هو مزعوم الشافعي رحمه الله، الشافعية (قالوا: لو كان العبد أهلاً للتصرف في شيء لكان أهلاً للملك) فيه (لأن التصرف سبب له) فإن الشيء يملك بالبيع والشراء ومسبب عنه فإن الملك يبيح التصرف، ولذا لا يباح في ملك غيره، ووجود الشيء يستلزم وجود سببه ومسببه ، فالتصرف أيضاً يستلزم الملك استلزام السبب والمسبب (واللازم باطل إجماعاً) فالملزوم مثله، فليس أهلاً للتصرف (وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف لم يكن أهلاً لليد، لأن اليد إنما تستفاد بملك الرقبة أو التصرف. وقد انتفيا، قلنا) لا نسلم الملازمة من كون التصرف سبباً للملك لا يمتنع انفكاكه حتى تجب الملازمة، إذ (التخلف) فيه (لمانع) وهو كون رقبته مملوكة للسيد (لا لعدم المقتضى) وهو التصرف، وكذا لا يلزم من كون التصرف مسبباً عن الملك لزومه له بل قد ينفك السبب عن مسببه إذا وجد هذا المسبب بسبب آخر (ويجوز تعدد الأسباب الأهلية التصرف والحاصل منع اللزوم بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك بإبداء المانع مع سببية أهلية التصرف لأهلية الملك وبإبداء سبب آخر لأهلية التصرف غير أهلية فيما يكون مسبباً، وإذا لم يثبت الاستلزام بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك لم يثبت ما يبتني عليه من قوله، وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف الخ ... فالكل جواب واحد، وزعم أن حديث تعدد الأسباب جواب آخر ومنع لقوله لأن اليد إنما يستفاد الخ ... فحينئذ يرد عليه أن حديث تعدد الأسباب لا يضرنا فإن المقصود بيان اللزوم بين أهلية اليد وأهلية التصرف، وهو حاصل، وإنما يضر هذا الحديث بعض المشايخ حيث أوردوا في تقرير كلام الشافعي أن ملك اليد يستفاد بملك الرقبة وعلى ما قررنا لا ورود لهذا فافهم ثم إذا ثبت له ملك اليد دون ملك الرقبة وهو أعلى من ملك الرقبة فإنه المقصود من ملك الرقبة، وقد كان مالكيته للنكاح بكلمة، فمالكيته أنقص من مالكية الحر انتقص ما يبتني على مالكيته وهو الدية، ولا تنقص عن النصف كما في المرأة لأن مالكيته ليست نصف مالكية الحر بل أزيد، ولا ينقص قدر الربع، لأن مالكيته أكثر يداً من ربع مالكية الحرة، فالنقصان غير مقدر، فنقصنا بقدر نصاب السرقة، فإن له اعتباراً في الشرع في مقابلة أعضاء الإنسان بخلاف سائر التنقيصات، فإنها لم تكن لأجل نقصان المالكية، بل لنقصان الكرامة، وأما الدية فباعتبار المالكية، ألا ترى أن دية المرأة نصف دية الرجل لكونها مالكة للمال فقط دون النكاح، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يرد ما أورد صدر الشريعة أنه يلزم حينئذٍ أن لا يتصف بشيء من النعم لأجل كون المالكية زائدة على النصف من الحر فتدبر، ثم أورد من عند نفسه دليلاً آخر هو أن المعتبر فيه المالية دون الآدمية فتعتبر في الضمان قيمته، إلا أنه نقص عن قيمة الحر لئلا يلزم شبهة المساواة بين الحر والعبد فتدبر فيه فإنه موضع تأمل.
(مطلقاً)
،
(فرع) لو أذن له المولى في نوع من التجارة كان له (التصرف) في أنواع التجارات الأنواع لأنه لما أذن فوت حقه في الخدمة، وفي براءة رقبته من الدين وأهلية في جميع الأنواع
Slavery in the case of a part-free [slave] is complete, so he is like a mukatab according to him (Abu Hanifa) and free according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Then slavery halves honors; hence it is permissible for him to marry two [women], and the divorce of a female slave is two divorces and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles by the text narrated by al-Daraqutni: 'The divorce of a female slave is two divorces and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' And her share [of the husband's time] is half the share of a free woman, and her [marriage] dissolution comes before a free woman and alone, not after her or with her. Similarly, it halves penalties: the penalty of a male slave is half the penalty of a free man, because gain is [proportional to] loss. Slavery prevents ownership of property, because he is owned, himself, lowly and contemptible; so how can he be an owner? Unlike ownership of self, which remains on the original freedom, so his confessions regarding hudud, retaliation, and theft are valid, whether he is perishing or standing, in the case of a permitted [slave] and a restricted one: according to al-Imam [Abu Hanifa], he is cut [for theft] and must return the property; according to Abu Yusuf, he is cut and does not return it; according to al-Imam Muhammad, he is not cut and does not return it. All this if the master denies him. And the master's confession regarding hudud or retaliation is not valid against him. Similarly, he owns the ownership of marriage, but he needs permission because it entails wealth in the dhimmah while the neck [person] is the master's property; hence he does not own the divorce of his wife. And he owns [the right to] his own life, so it is not permissible for the master to kill him or destroy any of his limbs; hence a free man is killed for him according to us. According to others, he is not killed only because of the honor of the free man. Slavery also prevents ownership of benefits; rather all benefits belong to the master except what is exempted, like fasting and prayer. So he does not go out for Friday, the two Eids, or Hajj except with the master's permission; similarly, jihad: he does not go out for it except with his permission or with permission of the Law at the general mobilization. And his guarantee is valid only if permitted, because he owns the spoils; it takes effect on him directly and on others derivatively. Slavery prevents [holding] authorities: so his testimony is not valid against anyone, nor his judgeship, nor his governance, nor his leadership. And slavery diminishes the dhimmah: nothing is obligatory on his dhimmah except by adding the financial value of his neck to it; hence wealth is not obligatory due to his confession immediately, except in the case of the permitted slave due to necessity. And penalties due to injuries become obligatory on his dhimmah, so they are paid and then his neck is sold unless the master ransoms him. Similarly, the neck of the permitted slave is sold for the remainder of the debt after payment from earnings or if he has no earnings. His voluntary donations from his earnings are not valid because they belong to the master or the creditors. And his gift is not accepted except a trifle by text. And it is not permissible for him to take a concubine from his female slaves, even if he is a mukatab.
Issue: The slave is eligible for disposal and ownership of hand according to us, contrary to al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, for according to him he is not eligible for them; rather he has disposal and hand-ownership as deputyship from the master. Our proof: both, namely ownership of disposal and hand-ownership, arise only from (capacity for speech) and its soundness, and (the dhimmah) which is that the person is fit to be addressed with rulings. (The first), i.e., capacity for speech, arises only from intellect, which is not disturbed by slavery necessarily; hence his narration is binding for action for the people. If his speech were not considered due to his intellect being disturbed, his narration would not be considered; rather he would become like an insane person. (The second), i.e., the dhimmah, arises only from capacity for obligation upon him and entitlement for him, and for their realization he is addressed with his rights, i.e., the rights of Allah Most High, like prayer, fasting, refraining from prohibitions, except what would miss the master's service like Friday and the like. And his confession regarding hudud and retaliation is valid, and his maintenance is obligatory on the master. Since the valid capacity for speech and the sound dhimmah are established for him, he becomes eligible for ownership of disposal and hand-ownership. (And the restriction) on disposal is due to the master's right in his neck and his benefits; if disposal were permitted without his permission, the neck would be ruined in debt and the master would not be able to use him and would be harmed. So his permission is the dissolution of the restriction and removal of the preventer from the validity of disposal, not establishing eligibility as claimed by al-Shafi‘i. The Shafi‘is said: If the slave were eligible for disposal in anything, he would be eligible for ownership in it, because disposal is a cause for it; for a thing is owned by sale and purchase, and it is a consequence of it, for ownership permits disposal; hence it is not permitted in the property of another. And the existence of a thing necessitates the existence of its cause and its consequence; so disposal also necessitates ownership as the cause and consequence necessitate. And the necessary is false by consensus, so the same for the necessitator; thus he is not eligible for disposal. And if he is not eligible for disposal, he is not eligible for hand, because hand is only obtained by ownership of the neck or disposal, and both are absent. We say: We do not accept the inevitability from the fact that disposal is a cause for ownership is not prevented from being separate such that the inevitability is necessary because (the lack) in it is due to a preventer, namely that his neck is owned by the master, not due to lack of the efficient cause, which is disposal. Similarly, it does not necessarily follow that disposal is a consequence of ownership such that ownership is necessary for it; rather the cause may separate from its effect if this effect arises from another cause. And multiple causes are permissible. The eligibility for disposal and the result is prevention of the necessity between eligibility for disposal and eligibility for ownership by presenting the preventer with the causality of eligibility for disposal for eligibility for ownership, and by presenting another cause for eligibility for disposal besides eligibility in what is an effect. And if the necessity is not established between eligibility for disposal and eligibility for ownership, what is built upon his statement is not established: 'And if he is not eligible for disposal...' so all is one answer. And the claim that the discussion of multiple causes is another answer and prevention of his statement because the hand is only obtained... then it is objected that the discussion of multiple causes does not harm us, for the purpose is to establish the necessity between eligibility for hand and eligibility for disposal, and it is established. This discussion only harms some shaykhs where they introduced in the articulation of al-Shafi‘i's statement that hand-ownership is obtained by ownership of the neck, and based on what we have established there is no objection to this, so understand. Then when hand-ownership is established for him without ownership of the neck—and it is higher than ownership of the neck because it is the purpose of ownership of the neck—and his ownership for marriage is by [some] word, so his ownership is deficient compared to the ownership of a free man; what is built on his ownership, namely blood-money, is deficient, but not deficient by half as for a woman because his ownership is not half of a free man's ownership but more; and it is not deficient by a quarter because his hand-ownership is more than a quarter of a free woman's hand-ownership; so the deficiency is not specified. So we diminished it by the extent of the nisab of theft, for it has a consideration in the Law in exchange for human limbs, unlike other reductions which were not due to deficiency of ownership but to deficiency of honor. As for blood-money, it is by consideration of ownership; do you not see that the blood-money of a woman is half of a man's because she owns property only, not marriage. On this estimate, what Sadr al-Shari‘ah objected does not occur, that then he would not be described with any of the blessings because his ownership exceeds half of a free man, so reflect. Then he introduced from himself another proof: that the consideration is financial value, not humanity, so his value is considered in guarantee, but it is less than the value of a free man to avoid the semblance of equality between free and slave; so reflect on it, for it is a place of contemplation.
(Absolutely)
(Branch) If the master permits him to engage in a type of trade, he has (disposal) in all types of trades because when he permitted, he forfeited his right in service and in the freedom of his neck from debt, and eligibility in all types.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut defines the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of a slave in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by explaining that slavery halves honors: a slave can marry two wives, but his wife's divorce is two divorces, waiting period two cycles, and his share of time is half. Penalties are halved. Then he explains why a slave cannot own property: he himself is owned, so he cannot be a property owner. However, he does own his self (ownership of self) and his statements regarding hudud, retaliation, and theft are valid (subject to master's denial). The slave needs permission for marriage because it incurs financial obligations on his dhimmah, but his neck is owned by the master. He cannot divorce his wife. A free man is killed for killing a slave (according to Hanafis). Benefits belong to the master, except essential religious duties. The slave cannot attend communal prayers without permission. His guarantee (aman) is valid if permitted because he can own spoils. He cannot hold judicial or testimonial authority. His dhimmah is weak: liabilities are only incurred by including his value. Confessions of debt are not immediately binding except for the permitted slave. Penalties for injuries are due from his dhimmah; if unpaid, he is sold. Donations and gifts are restricted. The main issue (mas'ala) is that the slave is eligible for disposal (tasarruf) and hand-ownership (milk al-yad) in Hanafi view, contrary to Shafi'i. The author provides proofs: the slave has capacity for speech (ahliyyat al-takallum) due to intellect, which remains intact in slavery; his narrations are thus binding. He also has a dhimmah, making him addressable with duties (prayer, fasting, avoiding prohibitions). Therefore he has capacity for disposal and hand-ownership. The restriction (hajr) is only due to the master's rights; the master's permission removes the obstacle, not grants capacity. The Shafi'i argument that disposal necessitates ownership (and since the slave cannot own property, he cannot have disposal) is refuted: the lack of ownership is due to a preventer (master's ownership), not absence of capacity. Hand-ownership can be obtained without full ownership of the neck. The author then discusses the implications for blood-money (diyah) and the nisab of theft, explaining why the slave's diyah is reduced but not exactly half. Finally, a branch: if the master permits one type of trade, the slave can engage in all types because the permission exempts the master's rights in service and debt.

Key Terms
أهلية التكلم (ahliyyat al-takallum): Capacity for speech, which arises from intellect; the slave retains this capacity because intellect is unaffected by slavery.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal personality that makes a person fit to be addressed with rulings and to bear obligations and rights; the slave possesses a dhimmah, though it is diminished.
ملك اليد (milk al-yad): Hand-ownership or possessory ownership; it is the actual control and benefit from property, distinct from full ownership of the neck (raqabah). The slave can have milk al-yad without owning the raqabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of the master's permission on a slave's ability to engage in transactions?
A) Permission establishes the slave's eligibility for disposal (ahliyyat al-tasarruf).
B) Permission removes the restriction (hajr) but does not create eligibility, as eligibility already exists.
C) Permission allows the slave to own property fully.
D) Permission is required for every transaction but does not affect the slave's capacity.

Key Takeaway
The slave has inherent legal capacity for disposal and hand-ownership based on his intellect and dhimmah; the master's permission only removes the restriction.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
The Shafi'i Objection and Hanafi Refutation: Causal Necessity vs. Preventer
Analyses the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi rebuttal on slave's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Shafi'i argument that disposal necessitates ownership and evaluate the Hanafi refutation using the concepts of preventer (mani) and multiple causation (ta'addud al-asbab).

Core Idea
Disposal is a cause for ownership, but its effect can be blocked by a preventer—the master's ownership of the slave's neck.

In this lesson, we examine the Shafi'i claim that if a slave were eligible for disposal, he would be eligible for ownership, which is false by consensus. The Hanafi response introduces the critical usul concept of a 'preventer' (mani) that blocks the causal link between capacity and actual ownership. We also explore the idea of multiple causes for hand-ownership.

Guided Path
1
Restate the Shafi'i syllogism
The Shafi'is argue: disposal is a cause for ownership; since the slave cannot own property (by consensus), he cannot have disposal. This is based on the rule that the existence of a cause necessitates the existence of its effect.
This is a strict causal entailment.
2
Identify the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis deny the necessity: a cause may fail to produce its effect due to a preventer (mani). Here, the preventer is the master's ownership of the slave's neck.
The effect is blocked, not absent due to lack of cause.
3
Understand multiple causation
The Hanafis also argue that hand-ownership (milk al-yad) can arise from another cause besides disposal. Thus, even if disposal were absent, hand-ownership could exist through another route.
This weakens the Shafi'i claim that hand-ownership requires disposal.
4
Apply to the dispute
The permission of the master removes the preventer, allowing the slave's disposal to take effect. It does not create the capacity, which is inherent.
Thus the Shafi'i fear that disposal would lead to ownership is unfounded because the preventer remains until permission.
5
Conclude the analytical outcome
The Hanafi response preserves the slave's inherent capacity while acknowledging the master's rights. The dispute is about the logical structure of legal causation.
This is a classic example of applying usul principles to a fiqh dispute.

فالرق في معتق البعض كامل فهو كالمكاتب عنده وحر عندهما، ثم الرق ينصف الكرامات، ولهذا يحل له نكاح اثنتين ويكون طلاق الأمة تطليقتين وعدتها حيضتين بالنص الذي رواه الدارقطني : طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وعدتها حيضتان ويكون قسمها نصف قسم الحرة، ويكون حلها قبل الحرة ومنفردة دون بعدها ومعها وكذا ينصف الحدود، فحد العبد نصف حد الحر، لأن الغرم بالغنم والرق يمنع مالكية المال لأنه مملوك نفسه ذليل مهان، فكيف يكون مالكاً، بخلاف مالكية النفس فإنها تبقى على أصل الحرية فتصح أقاريره بالحدود والقصاص والسرقة هالكاً وقائماً في المأذون وفي المحجور عند الإمام فيقطع ويرد المال، وعند أبي يوسف : يقطع ولا يردّ، وعند الإمام محمد لا يقطع ولا يرد هذا كله إذا كذبه المولى، ولا يصح إقرار المولى في حقه بحد أو قصاص، وكذا يملك هو ملك النكاح، وإنما يحتاج إلى الإذن لأنه يجب به المال في الذمة والرقبة هي ملك المولى، ولذا لا يملك طلاق امرأته، وكذا يملك دم نفسه فلا يحل للسيد قتله ولا إتلاف عضو من أعضائه، ولذا يقتل الحرّ به عندنا، وأما عند غيرنا فإنما لا يقتل لأجل كرامة الحر، والرق أيضاً يمنع مالكية المنافع، بل المنافع كلها للسيد إلا ما استثنى منها كالصوم والصلاة، فلا يخرج للجمعة والعيدين والحج إلا بإذن السيد وكذا الجهاد لا يخرج له إلا بإذنه أو بإذن الشرع عند النفير العام، وإنما يصح أمانة مأذوناً، لأنه مالك للغنيمة، فينفذ عليه بالذات وعلى غيره بالتبع، والرق يمنع الولايات فلا تصح الشهادة على أحد ولا قضاؤه ولا حكومته، وكذا إمارته، والرق ينقص الذمة فلا يجب على ذمته شيء إلا بضم مالية رقبته إليه فلا يجب بإقراره المال في الحال، إلا في المأذون للضرورة، وتجب الغرامات لأجل الجنايات في الذمة، فتؤدى فتباع رقبته إلا أن يفدي المولى، وكذا تباع رقبة المأذون فيما بقي من الدين بعد الأداء من الكسب أو لم يكن له كسب ولا تجوز تبرعاته من أكسابه لأنه ملك المولى أو الدائنين، ولا تقبل هديته إلا اليسير بالنص، ولا يجوز له التسري من إمائه وإن كان مكاتباً .
مسألة
(العبد أهل للتصرف وملك اليد عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله ، فإنه عنده ليس أهلاً لهما، وإنما له التصرف وملك اليد خلافة من السيد لنا أنهما أي ملك التصرف وملك اليد إنما يكونان (بأهلية التكلم) وصحته (والذمة) وهي كون الإنسان صالحاً لأن يخاطب بالأحكام ( والأولى) أي أهلية التكلم إنما تكون بالعقل وهو لا يختل بالرق) بالضرورة (ولذا كانت روايته ملزمة للعمل للخلق) ولو لم يكن كلامه معتبراً بكون عقله مختلاً لم تعتبر روايته، بل يصير كالمعتوه (والثانية) أي الذمة إنما تكون بأهلية) الإيجاب عليه والاستيجاب له، ولتحققهما خوطب بحقوقه أي حقوق الله (تعالى) من الصلاة والصوم والكف عن المحرمات، إلا ما يفوت به خدمة السيد كالجمعة ونحوها ويصح إقراره بالحدود والقصاص) وتجب نفقته على السيد، وإذ قد ثبت أهلية التكلم المعتبر والذمة الصحيحة له صار أهلاً لملك التصرف وملك اليد (وإنما الحجر) عن التصرف لحق المولى في رقبته وفي منافعه ولو جاز له التصرف من غير إذنه صارت الرقبة هالكة في الدين ولا يقدر على الاستخدام فيستضر به (فإذنه فك الحجر ورفع المانع عن صحة التصرف (لا إثبات الأهلية كما هو مزعوم الشافعي رحمه الله، الشافعية (قالوا: لو كان العبد أهلاً للتصرف في شيء لكان أهلاً للملك) فيه (لأن التصرف سبب له) فإن الشيء يملك بالبيع والشراء ومسبب عنه فإن الملك يبيح التصرف، ولذا لا يباح في ملك غيره، ووجود الشيء يستلزم وجود سببه ومسببه ، فالتصرف أيضاً يستلزم الملك استلزام السبب والمسبب (واللازم باطل إجماعاً) فالملزوم مثله، فليس أهلاً للتصرف (وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف لم يكن أهلاً لليد، لأن اليد إنما تستفاد بملك الرقبة أو التصرف. وقد انتفيا، قلنا) لا نسلم الملازمة من كون التصرف سبباً للملك لا يمتنع انفكاكه حتى تجب الملازمة، إذ (التخلف) فيه (لمانع) وهو كون رقبته مملوكة للسيد (لا لعدم المقتضى) وهو التصرف، وكذا لا يلزم من كون التصرف مسبباً عن الملك لزومه له بل قد ينفك السبب عن مسببه إذا وجد هذا المسبب بسبب آخر (ويجوز تعدد الأسباب الأهلية التصرف والحاصل منع اللزوم بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك بإبداء المانع مع سببية أهلية التصرف لأهلية الملك وبإبداء سبب آخر لأهلية التصرف غير أهلية فيما يكون مسبباً، وإذا لم يثبت الاستلزام بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك لم يثبت ما يبتني عليه من قوله، وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف الخ ... فالكل جواب واحد، وزعم أن حديث تعدد الأسباب جواب آخر ومنع لقوله لأن اليد إنما يستفاد الخ ... فحينئذ يرد عليه أن حديث تعدد الأسباب لا يضرنا فإن المقصود بيان اللزوم بين أهلية اليد وأهلية التصرف، وهو حاصل، وإنما يضر هذا الحديث بعض المشايخ حيث أوردوا في تقرير كلام الشافعي أن ملك اليد يستفاد بملك الرقبة وعلى ما قررنا لا ورود لهذا فافهم ثم إذا ثبت له ملك اليد دون ملك الرقبة وهو أعلى من ملك الرقبة فإنه المقصود من ملك الرقبة، وقد كان مالكيته للنكاح بكلمة، فمالكيته أنقص من مالكية الحر انتقص ما يبتني على مالكيته وهو الدية، ولا تنقص عن النصف كما في المرأة لأن مالكيته ليست نصف مالكية الحر بل أزيد، ولا ينقص قدر الربع، لأن مالكيته أكثر يداً من ربع مالكية الحرة، فالنقصان غير مقدر، فنقصنا بقدر نصاب السرقة، فإن له اعتباراً في الشرع في مقابلة أعضاء الإنسان بخلاف سائر التنقيصات، فإنها لم تكن لأجل نقصان المالكية، بل لنقصان الكرامة، وأما الدية فباعتبار المالكية، ألا ترى أن دية المرأة نصف دية الرجل لكونها مالكة للمال فقط دون النكاح، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يرد ما أورد صدر الشريعة أنه يلزم حينئذٍ أن لا يتصف بشيء من النعم لأجل كون المالكية زائدة على النصف من الحر فتدبر، ثم أورد من عند نفسه دليلاً آخر هو أن المعتبر فيه المالية دون الآدمية فتعتبر في الضمان قيمته، إلا أنه نقص عن قيمة الحر لئلا يلزم شبهة المساواة بين الحر والعبد فتدبر فيه فإنه موضع تأمل.
(مطلقاً)
،
(فرع) لو أذن له المولى في نوع من التجارة كان له (التصرف) في أنواع التجارات الأنواع لأنه لما أذن فوت حقه في الخدمة، وفي براءة رقبته من الدين وأهلية في جميع الأنواع
Slavery in the case of a part-free [slave] is complete, so he is like a mukatab according to him (Abu Hanifa) and free according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Then slavery halves honors; hence it is permissible for him to marry two [women], and the divorce of a female slave is two divorces and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles by the text narrated by al-Daraqutni: 'The divorce of a female slave is two divorces and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' And her share [of the husband's time] is half the share of a free woman, and her [marriage] dissolution comes before a free woman and alone, not after her or with her. Similarly, it halves penalties: the penalty of a male slave is half the penalty of a free man, because gain is [proportional to] loss. Slavery prevents ownership of property, because he is owned, himself, lowly and contemptible; so how can he be an owner? Unlike ownership of self, which remains on the original freedom, so his confessions regarding hudud, retaliation, and theft are valid, whether he is perishing or standing, in the case of a permitted [slave] and a restricted one: according to al-Imam [Abu Hanifa], he is cut [for theft] and must return the property; according to Abu Yusuf, he is cut and does not return it; according to al-Imam Muhammad, he is not cut and does not return it. All this if the master denies him. And the master's confession regarding hudud or retaliation is not valid against him. Similarly, he owns the ownership of marriage, but he needs permission because it entails wealth in the dhimmah while the neck [person] is the master's property; hence he does not own the divorce of his wife. And he owns [the right to] his own life, so it is not permissible for the master to kill him or destroy any of his limbs; hence a free man is killed for him according to us. According to others, he is not killed only because of the honor of the free man. Slavery also prevents ownership of benefits; rather all benefits belong to the master except what is exempted, like fasting and prayer. So he does not go out for Friday, the two Eids, or Hajj except with the master's permission; similarly, jihad: he does not go out for it except with his permission or with permission of the Law at the general mobilization. And his guarantee is valid only if permitted, because he owns the spoils; it takes effect on him directly and on others derivatively. Slavery prevents [holding] authorities: so his testimony is not valid against anyone, nor his judgeship, nor his governance, nor his leadership. And slavery diminishes the dhimmah: nothing is obligatory on his dhimmah except by adding the financial value of his neck to it; hence wealth is not obligatory due to his confession immediately, except in the case of the permitted slave due to necessity. And penalties due to injuries become obligatory on his dhimmah, so they are paid and then his neck is sold unless the master ransoms him. Similarly, the neck of the permitted slave is sold for the remainder of the debt after payment from earnings or if he has no earnings. His voluntary donations from his earnings are not valid because they belong to the master or the creditors. And his gift is not accepted except a trifle by text. And it is not permissible for him to take a concubine from his female slaves, even if he is a mukatab.
Issue: The slave is eligible for disposal and ownership of hand according to us, contrary to al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, for according to him he is not eligible for them; rather he has disposal and hand-ownership as deputyship from the master. Our proof: both, namely ownership of disposal and hand-ownership, arise only from (capacity for speech) and its soundness, and (the dhimmah) which is that the person is fit to be addressed with rulings. (The first), i.e., capacity for speech, arises only from intellect, which is not disturbed by slavery necessarily; hence his narration is binding for action for the people. If his speech were not considered due to his intellect being disturbed, his narration would not be considered; rather he would become like an insane person. (The second), i.e., the dhimmah, arises only from capacity for obligation upon him and entitlement for him, and for their realization he is addressed with his rights, i.e., the rights of Allah Most High, like prayer, fasting, refraining from prohibitions, except what would miss the master's service like Friday and the like. And his confession regarding hudud and retaliation is valid, and his maintenance is obligatory on the master. Since the valid capacity for speech and the sound dhimmah are established for him, he becomes eligible for ownership of disposal and hand-ownership. (And the restriction) on disposal is due to the master's right in his neck and his benefits; if disposal were permitted without his permission, the neck would be ruined in debt and the master would not be able to use him and would be harmed. So his permission is the dissolution of the restriction and removal of the preventer from the validity of disposal, not establishing eligibility as claimed by al-Shafi‘i. The Shafi‘is said: If the slave were eligible for disposal in anything, he would be eligible for ownership in it, because disposal is a cause for it; for a thing is owned by sale and purchase, and it is a consequence of it, for ownership permits disposal; hence it is not permitted in the property of another. And the existence of a thing necessitates the existence of its cause and its consequence; so disposal also necessitates ownership as the cause and consequence necessitate. And the necessary is false by consensus, so the same for the necessitator; thus he is not eligible for disposal. And if he is not eligible for disposal, he is not eligible for hand, because hand is only obtained by ownership of the neck or disposal, and both are absent. We say: We do not accept the inevitability from the fact that disposal is a cause for ownership is not prevented from being separate such that the inevitability is necessary because (the lack) in it is due to a preventer, namely that his neck is owned by the master, not due to lack of the efficient cause, which is disposal. Similarly, it does not necessarily follow that disposal is a consequence of ownership such that ownership is necessary for it; rather the cause may separate from its effect if this effect arises from another cause. And multiple causes are permissible. The eligibility for disposal and the result is prevention of the necessity between eligibility for disposal and eligibility for ownership by presenting the preventer with the causality of eligibility for disposal for eligibility for ownership, and by presenting another cause for eligibility for disposal besides eligibility in what is an effect. And if the necessity is not established between eligibility for disposal and eligibility for ownership, what is built upon his statement is not established: 'And if he is not eligible for disposal...' so all is one answer. And the claim that the discussion of multiple causes is another answer and prevention of his statement because the hand is only obtained... then it is objected that the discussion of multiple causes does not harm us, for the purpose is to establish the necessity between eligibility for hand and eligibility for disposal, and it is established. This discussion only harms some shaykhs where they introduced in the articulation of al-Shafi‘i's statement that hand-ownership is obtained by ownership of the neck, and based on what we have established there is no objection to this, so understand. Then when hand-ownership is established for him without ownership of the neck—and it is higher than ownership of the neck because it is the purpose of ownership of the neck—and his ownership for marriage is by [some] word, so his ownership is deficient compared to the ownership of a free man; what is built on his ownership, namely blood-money, is deficient, but not deficient by half as for a woman because his ownership is not half of a free man's ownership but more; and it is not deficient by a quarter because his hand-ownership is more than a quarter of a free woman's hand-ownership; so the deficiency is not specified. So we diminished it by the extent of the nisab of theft, for it has a consideration in the Law in exchange for human limbs, unlike other reductions which were not due to deficiency of ownership but to deficiency of honor. As for blood-money, it is by consideration of ownership; do you not see that the blood-money of a woman is half of a man's because she owns property only, not marriage. On this estimate, what Sadr al-Shari‘ah objected does not occur, that then he would not be described with any of the blessings because his ownership exceeds half of a free man, so reflect. Then he introduced from himself another proof: that the consideration is financial value, not humanity, so his value is considered in guarantee, but it is less than the value of a free man to avoid the semblance of equality between free and slave; so reflect on it, for it is a place of contemplation.
(Absolutely)
(Branch) If the master permits him to engage in a type of trade, he has (disposal) in all types of trades because when he permitted, he forfeited his right in service and in the freedom of his neck from debt, and eligibility in all types.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut defines the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of a slave in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by explaining that slavery halves honors: a slave can marry two wives, but his wife's divorce is two divorces, waiting period two cycles, and his share of time is half. Penalties are halved. Then he explains why a slave cannot own property: he himself is owned, so he cannot be a property owner. However, he does own his self (ownership of self) and his statements regarding hudud, retaliation, and theft are valid (subject to master's denial). The slave needs permission for marriage because it incurs financial obligations on his dhimmah, but his neck is owned by the master. He cannot divorce his wife. A free man is killed for killing a slave (according to Hanafis). Benefits belong to the master, except essential religious duties. The slave cannot attend communal prayers without permission. His guarantee (aman) is valid if permitted because he can own spoils. He cannot hold judicial or testimonial authority. His dhimmah is weak: liabilities are only incurred by including his value. Confessions of debt are not immediately binding except for the permitted slave. Penalties for injuries are due from his dhimmah; if unpaid, he is sold. Donations and gifts are restricted. The main issue (mas'ala) is that the slave is eligible for disposal (tasarruf) and hand-ownership (milk al-yad) in Hanafi view, contrary to Shafi'i. The author provides proofs: the slave has capacity for speech (ahliyyat al-takallum) due to intellect, which remains intact in slavery; his narrations are thus binding. He also has a dhimmah, making him addressable with duties (prayer, fasting, avoiding prohibitions). Therefore he has capacity for disposal and hand-ownership. The restriction (hajr) is only due to the master's rights; the master's permission removes the obstacle, not grants capacity. The Shafi'i argument that disposal necessitates ownership (and since the slave cannot own property, he cannot have disposal) is refuted: the lack of ownership is due to a preventer (master's ownership), not absence of capacity. Hand-ownership can be obtained without full ownership of the neck. The author then discusses the implications for blood-money (diyah) and the nisab of theft, explaining why the slave's diyah is reduced but not exactly half. Finally, a branch: if the master permits one type of trade, the slave can engage in all types because the permission exempts the master's rights in service and debt.

Key Terms
مانع (mani'): A preventer that blocks the legal effect of a cause while the cause itself remains present.
تعدد الأسباب (ta'addud al-asbab): The principle that a single legal effect can arise from multiple different causes; used here to argue that hand-ownership can be obtained through other means besides disposal.
أهلية التكلم (ahliyyat al-takallum): Capacity for speech, which arises from intellect; the slave retains this capacity because intellect is unaffected by slavery.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal personality that makes a person fit to be addressed with rulings and to bear obligations and rights; the slave possesses a dhimmah, though it is diminished.
ملك اليد (milk al-yad): Hand-ownership or possessory ownership; it is the actual control and benefit from property, distinct from full ownership of the neck (raqabah). The slave can have milk al-yad without owning the raqabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key usul concept that Hanafis use to refute the Shafi'i argument that disposal necessitates ownership?
A) Ijma' (consensus)
B) Istihsan (juristic preference)
C) The concept of a preventer (mani) that can block the effect of a cause
D) The concept of qiyas (analogy)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rebuttal uses the concept of a preventer (mani) to break the Shafi'i causal chain, preserving the slave's inherent capacity while acknowledging the master's right as a blocking condition.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
المانع يكسر الاستلزام: رد الحنفية على الشافعية في أهلية العبد للتصرف
Hanafi rebuttal to Shafi'i entailment argument on slave capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Shafi'i argument for necessary entailment between transaction capacity and ownership capacity, and apply the Hanafi concept of mania (preventing factor) to refute it.

Core Idea
A cause can exist without its effect if a preventing factor blocks it — the slave's capacity for transaction is valid despite blocked ownership.

In this passage, the Hanafi school confronts a key Shafi'i argument: if a slave can transact, he must be able to own. The Hanafi response introduces a critical usul tool — the concept of al-mani', which shows that an effect can be absent not because the cause is missing, but because a preventing factor intervenes. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping how Hanafi usul handles conflicts between textual implications and practical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i claim
The Shafi'i argue that transaction capacity necessarily entails ownership capacity because transaction is a cause of ownership. Since the slave lacks ownership by consensus, he must lack transaction capacity.
This is based on the principle that the existence of a cause necessitates the existence of its effect.
2
Locate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response begins with 'لا نسلم الملازمة' (we do not accept the entailment). They deny the necessary implication.
The key is that the absence of the effect does not prove the absence of the cause.
3
Understand the mania argument
The effect (ownership) is blocked by a mania: the slave's person is owned by the master. So the cause (transaction) is present, but the effect is prevented.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between 'adam al-muqtadi' (absence of cause) and wujud al-mani' (presence of preventer).
4
Consider the multiple causes argument
Ownership can arise from another cause besides transaction, so the entailment is not one-to-one. Transaction does not necessitate ownership if ownership can come from elsewhere.
This supports the separation of cause and effect.
5
Summarize the thamarah
Practical difference: In Hanafi, a slave with permission can transact and possess (yad), while in Shafi'i, a slave has no transaction capacity.
This illustrates how usul principles affect fiqh rulings.

قالوا: لو كان العبد أهلاً للتصرف في شيء لكان أهلاً للملك فيه (لأن التصرف سبب له) فإن الشيء يملك بالبيع والشراء ومسبب عنه فإن الملك يبيح التصرف، ولذا لا يباح في ملك غيره، ووجود الشيء يستلزم وجود سببه ومسببه ، فالتصرف أيضاً يستلزم الملك استلزام السبب والمسبب (واللازم باطل إجماعاً) فالملزوم مثله، فليس أهلاً للتصرف (وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف لم يكن أهلاً لليد، لأن اليد إنما تستفاد بملك الرقبة أو التصرف. وقد انتفيا، قلنا) لا نسلم الملازمة من كون التصرف سبباً للملك لا يمتنع انفكاكه حتى تجب الملازمة، إذ (التخلف) فيه (لمانع) وهو كون رقبته مملوكة للسيد (لا لعدم المقتضى) وهو التصرف، وكذا لا يلزم من كون التصرف مسبباً عن الملك لزومه له بل قد ينفك السبب عن مسببه إذا وجد هذا المسبب بسبب آخر (ويجوز تعدد الأسباب الأهلية التصرف والحاصل منع اللزوم بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك بإبداء المانع مع سببية أهلية التصرف لأهلية الملك وبإبداء سبب آخر لأهلية التصرف غير أهلية فيما يكون مسبباً، وإذا لم يثبت الاستلزام بين أهلية التصرف وأهلية الملك لم يثبت ما يبتني عليه من قوله، وإذا لم يكن أهلاً للتصرف الخ ... فالكل جواب واحد، وزعم أن حديث تعدد الأسباب جواب آخر ومنع لقوله لأن اليد إنما يستفاد الخ ... فحينئذ يرد عليه أن حديث تعدد الأسباب لا يضرنا فإن المقصود بيان اللزوم بين أهلية اليد وأهلية التصرف، وهو حاصل، وإنما يضر هذا الحديث بعض المشايخ حيث أوردوا في تقرير كلام الشافعي أن ملك اليد يستفاد بملك الرقبة وعلى ما قررنا لا ورود لهذا فافهم ثم إذا ثبت له ملك اليد دون ملك الرقبة وهو أعلى من ملك الرقبة فإنه المقصود من ملك الرقبة، وقد كان مالكيته للنكاح بكلمة، فمالكيته أنقص من مالكية الحر انتقص ما يبتني على مالكيته وهو الدية، ولا تنقص عن النصف كما في المرأة لأن مالكيته ليست نصف مالكية الحر بل أزيد، ولا ينقص قدر الربع، لأن مالكيته أكثر يداً من ربع مالكية الحرة، فالنقصان غير مقدر، فنقصنا بقدر نصاب السرقة، فإن له اعتباراً في الشرع في مقابلة أعضاء الإنسان بخلاف سائر التنقيصات، فإنها لم تكن لأجل نقصان المالكية، بل لنقصان الكرامة، وأما الدية فباعتبار المالكية، ألا ترى أن دية المرأة نصف دية الرجل لكونها مالكة للمال فقط دون النكاح، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يرد ما أورد صدر الشريعة أنه يلزم حينئذٍ أن لا يتصف بشيء من النعم لأجل كون المالكية زائدة على النصف من الحر فتدبر، ثم أورد من عند نفسه دليلاً آخر هو أن المعتبر فيه المالية دون الآدمية فتعتبر في الضمان قيمته، إلا أنه نقص عن قيمة الحر لئلا يلزم شبهة المساواة بين الحر والعبد فتدبر فيه فإنه موضع تأمل.
They said: If the slave were legally capable of transacting in anything, he would be legally capable of ownership in it, because transaction is a cause of ownership. For a thing is owned through sale and purchase, and is an effect of it; indeed ownership permits transaction, and therefore it is not permitted in the property of another. The existence of a thing necessitates the existence of its cause and its effect, so transaction also necessitates ownership as cause and effect necessitate. And the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise false. Thus he is not legally capable of transaction. And if he is not legally capable of transaction, he is not legally capable of possession (yad), because possession is only obtained through ownership of the slave's person (raqabah) or through transaction, and both are absent. We said: We do not accept the implication that transaction being a cause of ownership cannot be separated [from it] such that implication is necessary. For the failure of the effect to occur is due to a preventing factor—namely that his person is owned by the master—not due to the absence of the efficient cause, which is transaction. Likewise, it does not follow from transaction being an effect of ownership that it necessarily accompanies it; rather, the cause may be separated from its effect if that effect occurs due to another cause, and multiple causes are permissible. The gist is the prevention of entailment between capacity for transaction and capacity for ownership by presenting a preventing factor, with the causality of capacity for transaction for capacity for ownership, and by presenting another cause for capacity for transaction other than capacity for ownership in what is an effect. And if the entailment between capacity for transaction and capacity for ownership is not established, then what is based on his statement is not established—that if he is not capable of transacting, etc. So the whole is one answer. And he claimed that the mention of multiple causes is another answer and a prevention of his statement because yad is only obtained... Then it is objected that the mention of multiple causes does not harm us, for the purpose is to state the entailment between capacity for yad and capacity for transaction, which is established. This mention only harms some mashayikh who, in explaining al-Shafi'i's words, said that yad of ownership is obtained through ownership of the raqabah. On our formulation, there is no objection. Understand. Then, if yad of ownership is established for him without ownership of the raqabah—and it is higher than ownership of the raqabah, for it is the purpose of ownership of the raqabah—and his ownership (malikiyyah) for marriage is with a word, so his malikiyyah is less than the malikiyyah of a free man. So what is based on his malikiyyah—namely blood money (diyah)—is reduced, but it is not reduced to half as in the case of a woman, because his malikiyyah is not half of the free man's malikiyyah but more. Nor is it reduced by a quarter, because his malikiyyah is more by yad than a quarter of a free woman's malikiyyah. So the reduction is not fixed; we reduced it by the amount of the minimum for theft (nisaab al-sariqah), because it has consideration in the law in the case of body parts of a human, unlike other reductions which were not due to deficiency in malikiyyah but due to deficiency in dignity. As for diyah, it is based on malikiyyah; do you not see that the diyah of a woman is half the diyah of a man because she owns property only, not marriage. On this estimation, the objection of Sadr al-Shariah that then he would not be described with any grace due to his malikiyyah being more than half of a free man does not apply; so reflect. Then he (the author) adduced on his own another proof: that the consideration is for monetary value, not humanity, so his value is considered in liability, except that it is less than the value of a free man to avoid the semblance of equality between free and slave. Reflect on this, for it is a point for contemplation.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school over the legal capacity of a slave. The Shafi'i argument, as presented, is that if a slave could transact, he would also be capable of ownership, because transaction is a cause of ownership. Since the slave is incapable of ownership by consensus, he must be incapable of transaction. Furthermore, possession (yad) is derived from ownership or transaction, so if both are absent, the slave also lacks the capacity for yad. The Hanafi response, introduced with 'قلنا' (we said), denies the necessary implication. They argue that the absence of the effect (ownership) does not negate the capacity for the cause (transaction) if a preventing factor (the slave being owned by the master) is present. Similarly, transaction can occur without ownership if ownership arises from another source. Therefore, a slave can have capacity for transaction and for yad without full ownership. The discussion then extends to the partial ownership of a slave (malikiyyah) and how it affects blood money (diyah) and other rulings. The author concludes with a separate proof that the slave's value in liability is based on his monetary value, not his humanity, adjusted to avoid equality with free men. This section is crucial for understanding the Hanafi position on the partial capacity of slaves and the use of legal fictions (hila) in permitting transactions.

Key Terms
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): Capacity to perform legal transactions (e.g., sale, purchase) independently.
أهلية الملك (ahliyyat al-milk): Capacity to own property in full, including the right to transfer ownership without restriction.
المانع (al-mani'): A preventing factor that blocks the legal effect of a cause. Here, the master's ownership of the slave's person prevents the slave from owning the raqabah despite having capacity for transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does a slave not become fully capable of ownership (ahliyyat al-milk) despite having capacity for transaction (ahliyyat al-tasarruf)?
A) Because the slave lacks rational capacity.
B) Because the master's ownership of his person (raqabah) acts as a preventing factor (mani').
C) Because the Shafi'i school proved it impossible.
D) Because the slave is not a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul concept of mania allows a cause to exist without its effect, thereby preserving the slave's transaction capacity despite blocked ownership.

Possession (Yad) for the Slave and Its Legal Consequences: Diyah and Value
Hanafi derivation of yad for slave and its consequences for diyah and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school establishes possession (yad) for the slave based on transaction capacity, and derive the resulting rulings for blood money (diyah) and value in liability.

Core Idea
The slave's possession (malak al-yad) is established through transaction capacity, not ownership, leading to unique rulings on diyah and liability.

Having rebutted the Shafi'i entailment argument, the Hanafi school now proceeds to establish that the slave can have possession (yad) even without full ownership. This possession is based on transaction capacity, not on ownership of the slave's person. From this, they derive rulings on blood money and liability, showing that the slave's malikiyyah is partial and affects compensation amounts. This lesson explores the application of the earlier usul principle to specific fiqh issues.

Guided Path
1
Re-establish the link between yad and transaction
The Hanafi argue that yad is obtained through transaction capacity, not just through ownership. Since they proved transaction capacity, yad is established.
The text says 'فإن المقصود بيان اللزوم بين أهلية اليد وأهلية التصرف'.
2
Address the objection about multiple causes
The objection that yad could arise from other causes is dismissed because here the focus is on the entailment between yad and transaction capacity.
The multiple causes argument does not harm the Hanafi position.
3
Apply the malikiyyah concept to diyah
The slave's malikiyyah is less than a free man's, so his diyah is reduced. The reduction is not fixed to half like a woman, but is calibrated differently.
The text explains that the slave's malikiyyah is more than half of a free man's but not full.
4
Analyze the specific reduction for diyah
The reduction is based on the minimum for theft (nisaab al-sariqah), which has a textual basis in the law.
This is a specific numerical reduction, not a fraction.
5
Consider the final proof on liability value
The slave's value in liability is based on monetary value, not humanity, to avoid equality with a free man.
This is a separate proof adduced by the author.

Key Terms
ملك اليد (malak al-yad): Possessory ownership: the right to hold and benefit from property, distinct from full ownership of the substance (raqabah).
نصاب السرقة (nisaab al-sariqah): The minimum threshold for theft that triggers the hadd punishment (1/4 dinar or 3 dirhams). Here used as a benchmark for diyah reduction.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for homicide or bodily injury.

Why Hanafi Usul Rejects Qiyas in Arabic Language
Refuting the permissibility of qiyas in language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects qiyas in Arabic language and distinguish it from shar'i qiyas.

Core Idea
Meaning attracts ruling, not words: linguistic qiyas fails.

In this passage, the author refutes those who permit qiyas (analogical reasoning) in the Arabic language. While qiyas is a valid method in shar'i rulings, it cannot be applied to linguistic coinage because the relationship between meaning and word is not causal like the relationship between cause and effect. The Hanafi position rests on the principle that suitability (munasaba) only validates coinage, not necessitates it, and transmitted proof is required for linguistic assignments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The passage begins by addressing the argument that qiyas could be valid in language if it were a proof in non-shar'i matters. Recognize that this is the claim being refuted.
The opponent is likely a grammarian who permits linguistic qiyas.
2
Understand the rebuttal: qiyas ma'a al-fariq
The author responds with an 'analogy with a difference' – in shar'i qiyas, the meaning ('illa) attracts the ruling (hukm) because of causal connection. In language, meaning does not attract the word; otherwise it would imply natural indication (dalala bi al-tab').
This is the core Hanafi argument: linguistic meaning and word are not causally linked.
3
Analyze the examples
The passage cites 'tard al-adham' (refraining from extending 'adham' to all dark things), 'qarurah' (not applying to all things with a base), and 'ajdal' (not applying to all strong things). These show that grammarians themselves prohibited analogical extension despite shared attributes.
These examples illustrate the rejection of linguistic qiyas in practice.
4
Apply Sadr al-Shari'ah's principle
Sadr al-Shari'ah states that suitability (munasaba) only validates coinage (wad'), not necessitates it. Even if a meaning is suitable for a word, it does not force the coinage; a transmitted proof (naql) is necessary.
This principle distinguishes between possibility and actual occurrence in language.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
The overall position is that qiyas is not permissible in language because the relationship is not causal, suitability is not sufficient, and transmitted usage is required. The disagreement is only about naming unmentioned items, but the possibility of prohibition remains.
Hanafi usul maintains that language is based on convention (tawqif) and analogy cannot create new words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللغة، وأين هذا من ذاك؟ نعم : إنما ينتهض لو كان القياس في غير الشرعيات حجة (قلنا) قياس مع الفارق إذ ثبت هناك أي في القياس الشرعي (الحكم عقلاً، لأن المعنى يجذب المعنى إذ يجوز أن يكون علاقة العلية بين المعاني فيجذب المعنى العلة لمعلوله وهو الحكم، و (لا) يجذب المعنى اللفظ وإلا لزم الدلالة بالطبع فتفكر فالحق (لا) أي لا يجوز القياس في اللغة كيف ويحتمل التصريح منهم (بالمنع) عن القياس (فإن الخلاف إنما هو في تسمية مسكوت عنه هل يجوز بالقياس أم لا؟ وفيه احتمال المنع قائم، ولم يثبت هذا الجواز عموماً في الأحكام الشرعية، حتى لا يصح القياس فيها أيضاً ألا يرى أنهم منعوا طرد الأدهم) في كل ما وجد فيه دهمة (والقارورة) في كل ما فيه قرار ( والأجدل) في كل ما فيه قوة (وغيرهما مما لا يخفى) قال صدر الشريعة : اعتبار المناسبة أمر مصحح للوضع لا موجب، وليس أن كل ما يجوز يقع، فلا بد من دليل يقوم على وقوع الوضع من النقل وغيره وضاع القياس فافهم

So reflect. So the truth is (not), i.e., analogy is not permissible in language. How? And their explicit prohibition of analogy is possible. (For the disagreement is only about naming something unmentioned: is it permissible by analogy or not? And in it the possibility of prohibition stands, and this permissibility has not been established generally in shar'i rulings, so that analogy would not be valid in them either. Do you not see that they prohibited (the extension of 'adham) to everything in which there is duskiness, and (the extension of 'qarurah') to everything in which there is a base, and (the extension of 'ajdal') to everything in which there is strength, and others which are not hidden. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: Consideration of suitability is a validator for coinage, not a necessitator. And not everything that is permissible occurs. So there must be a proof that establishes the occurrence of coinage from transmission or other. And analogy is lost, so understand.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who permit qiyas (analogy) in the Arabic language. The author presents the opposing claim that qiyas in language could be valid if it were a proof in non-shar'i matters, but responds with 'qiyas ma'a al-fariq' (analogy with a difference). In shar'i qiyas, the ruling is derived intellectually from a cause ('illa) that attracts its effect. However, in language, meaning does not attract the word itself; that would imply natural indication. The Hanafi position is that qiyas is not permissible in language, as evidenced by grammarians' explicit prohibitions against analogizing certain terms (like 'adham for every dark thing). The disagreement is only about whether an unmentioned term can be derived by analogy, but the possibility of prohibition remains. Sadr al-Shari'ah explains that suitability (munasaba) only validates coinage (wad'), it does not necessitate it. Without a transmitted proof, analogy fails.

Key Terms
القياس في اللغة (al-qiyas fi al-lugha): Applying analogical reasoning to derive linguistic meanings or coinages based on shared attributes.
طرد الأدهم (tard al-adham): A refrained extension of 'adham (a dark object) to all dark things, used as evidence against linguistic qiyas.
مصحح للوضع (musahhih li al-wad'): A validator for coinage – something that makes coinage possible but does not necessitate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is qiyas not valid in language?
A) Because language is based on convention, not causation.
B) Because meaning does not attract the word; otherwise would imply natural indication.
C) Because grammarians prohibit all forms of analogy.
D) Because shar'i qiyas requires a text, which is absent in language.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects qiyas in Arabic language because the causal relationship between meaning and ruling in shar'i qiyas does not apply to the connection between meaning and word; linguistic coinage requires transmitted proof, not analogy.

Condition for Valid Attribution of a Derived Noun (Mushtaqq) and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Distinguishing condition for mushtaqq and theological refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi condition for the valid attribution of a derived noun and evaluate the Mu'tazila objection using the Quranic proof and rational argument.

Core Idea
A derived noun is valid only if its original meaning exists in the subject; for God, the attribute of knowledge is real, not merely relational.

This lesson examines a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the condition for the truth of a derived noun (mushtaqq). The passage discusses the requirement that the original meaning (asl) must be present in the subject for the mushtaqq to be correctly attributed. It then addresses the Mu'tazila challenge regarding God's attributes, where they claimed that God is 'alim without 'ilm. The Hanafi response uses both rational necessity and a Quranic verse to uphold the reality of divine attributes while refuting the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for mushtaqq
Recognize that the validity of a derived noun requires the original meaning to exist in the subject, because the whole cannot exist without the part.
This is the majority (Hanafi) view.
2
State the Mu'tazila objection
Note that the Mu'tazila deny the existence of 'ilm in God to avoid multiplicity of eternals, yet still apply the derived noun 'alim.
Their motive is to avoid ta'addud al-qudama'.
3
Examine the Hanafi counter-proof
Understand that the Quranic condemnation of the Christian trinity (5:73) condemns multiplicity of ancient essences, not attributes which are not independent essences.
The verse is used to distinguish between essence and attribute.
4
Analyze the refinement of the argument
Appreciate that divine attributes have two meanings: a conceptual meaning and self-unveiling. The second is God's essence itself, so the derived noun is valid because the origin (conceptual meaning) subsists in His essence in a manner befitting Him.
This shows that the Mu'tazila's worry is unfounded.

Issue: The condition for the validity of attributing a derived noun is the truth of its original meaning in the subject (because the whole, which is the derived noun, cannot be realized without the part, which is the original). This is apparent according to the majority view, and it is not valid according to what he will later choose (that there is no composition in the concept of the derived noun). So the intended meaning is what is like a part, or it is built on the well-known view. Contrary to the Mu'tazila regarding the attributes of God Almighty, for they said that He is 'alim (knowing) without His knowledge, thus they admitted the truth of the derived noun 'alim without the realization of its origin which is knowledge. They said this to avoid the necessity of multiplicity of eternals (ta'addud al-qudama') by affirming the subsistence of attributes, since it is impossible for accidents to subsist in His essence and multiplicity of eternals is false. Do you not see how God Almighty condemned the Christians: 'Those who say that Allah is the third of three have certainly disbelieved' (5:73)? If it is said: This applies to them as well, for they affirm the subsistence of 'alimiyya (being knowing) in His essence. They said: 'As for 'alimiyya, it is only a relational consideration, not an essential attribute.' The answer is that what is impossible is only multiplicity of ancient essences, and God condemned this statement because the Christians only say there are three gods, and a god is only an ancient necessary essence. As for the attributes we affirm, they are necessary for the essence, not by themselves, for they need the essence, so they are not necessary. And if they are not necessary, they are not deities by any reasonable person. So this is not included in the verse. And it is not the purpose of the answer that multiplicity of ancient essences entails necessity by themselves without multiplicity of attributes, such that it would be objected that pre-eternity although for the essence does not necessitate necessity nor negate contingency. Do you not see that the philosophers claim the pre-eternity of the celestial sphere etc. while believing in its contingency? So ponder. And for the knower of secrets, there is a statement here that must be noted: that knowledge and other attributes are used in two meanings: one is the infinitive meaning understood by the general public; the second is the disclosure of things and consequent understanding. The second in regard to God is His very essence. The third article on linguistic principles: For His essence by itself discloses things to Him, and He does not consider in the disclosure another matter subsisting in Him as we need something additional to our essence in the disclosure of things. The origin of these derived nouns is the first conceptual meaning subsisting in His essence, and for the truth of the derived noun, it is not necessary that the origin subsist as a separate subsistence. Do you not see how the derivatives of conceptual matters apply to the essences from which the conceptual matters are abstracted? Thus they do not say the truth of a derived noun without the subsistence of the origin. This is better than what was said: that what matters in the truth of derivatives is subsistence in a general sense, meaning the negation of subsistence by another, and that is realized. For it may be correct, but the statements of al-Zamakhshari and al-Sakkaki despite their rigidity in their evil from Mu'tazilism do not support it. And the used (terms).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition for the validity of attributing a derived noun (mushtaqq) to something: the original meaning (al-asl) must actually be present in the subject, because the whole (the mushtaqq) cannot exist without the part (the original meaning). This is the view of the majority. However, the Mu'tazila objected regarding the attributes of God, claiming that 'alim (knowing) is attributed to God without knowledge (al-'ilm) as an attribute, to avoid multiplicity of eternals. The response is that the impossibility is only in multiple ancient essences (dhawat qadima), not in attributes that are not self-existent. The passage then explains that divine attributes like knowledge have two meanings: one is the conceptual meaning (ma'na masdari) and the other is the self-unveiling (inkishaf al-ashya') which in God is His very essence. Thus, the derived noun is valid because the original meaning (the conceptual meaning) does exist in God's essence as a considerational attribute (i'tibari). This refutes the Mu'tazila claim and upholds the Hanafi position that God's attributes are real and not merely relational. The section heading 'المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية' (Third Article on Linguistic Principles) appears within the discussion.

Key Terms
المشتق (al-mushtaqq): The derived term that requires the original meaning to be present.
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila): A theological school that denied the reality of God's attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) exist as a separate essence
B) exist in some form, conceptual or otherwise
C) have no actual existence
D) be a mere relational consideration

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul posits that a derived noun is only valid if its original meaning exists in the subject, and this holds for God's attributes in a manner befitting His essence, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Temporal Usage of Mushtaqq: Reality and Metaphor
Analyzing the reality and metaphor of using derived nouns for present, future, and past actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rule for using derived nouns (mushtaqq) in present, future, and past tenses and apply the test of negation to distinguish haqiqah from majaz.

Core Idea
Using a mushtaqq for the present is real; for the future it's metaphor; for the past, the preferred view is absolute metaphor.

Derived nouns like 'kaatib' (writer) or 'daarib' (hitter) are common in legal texts, yet their temporal scope is often misunderstood. This lesson clarifies when such terms are used literally (haqiqah) and when metaphorically (majaz), focusing on the Hanafi preferred view and the powerful test of negation that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three temporal contexts
From the passage, note that using a mushtaqq for the present action is haqiqah by consensus, for the future is majaz by consensus (with a nuance from Ibn Sina), and for the past is disputed among Hanafis and Mu'tazila.
Future consensus is challenged but defended by distinguishing language from logic.
2
Understand the test of negation (sihhat al-nafy)
The proof for past being majaz is that we can truthfully say 'Zayd is not standing' about someone who stood in the past. If 'standing' were true of the past, it would also be true of the present (by analogy), leading to contradiction with customary usage.
This test is a key indicator of majaz in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the counterarguments
Examine the views of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i (haqiqah mulaqan) and those who differentiate between permanent and transient attributes. The Hanafi response uses the contradiction argument to refute them.
Also note the digression about Ibn Sina – he is discussing logic, not language.
4
Apply the rule to legal interpretation
Consider a Qur'anic verse like 'The thief' (al-sariq). According to the Hanafi rule, applying it to someone who stole in the past is metaphorical, so the verse serves as a general principle applicable to past and future thieves via the extended usage.
This affects how broad the legal ruling becomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أن إطلاق المشتق كالضارب لمباشر الضرب) في الحال (حقيقة اتفاقاً فإن قلت : كيف هذا الإجماع مع إجماعهم على أن المشتقات لا تدل بالوضع على الزمان أصلاً! قلت يصح لعل ذلك الإجماع على وضعه مفرداً، وهذا في حال التركيب لإفادة المعنى فتدبر (و) إطلاق المشتق (باعتبار المستقبل أي إطلاقه على ما ليس مباشراً لكنه سيباشر في المستقبل (مجاز اتفاقاً كذا قالوا : أقول فيه أي في نقل الاتفاق نظر فإن ابن سينا وأتباعه ذهبوا) في تحصيل معنى القضية إلى أن معنى كل أبيض كل ما يصدق عليه أبيض بالفعل في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة فلا اتفاق، والجواب : أنه ليس مقصوده بيان اللغة، بل بيان أن القضية المعتبرة في المنطق في الفلسفة هو ما اعتبر صدق العنوان عليه بالفعل، ولو سلم أنه حسب أن في اللغة الاعتبار بهذا فأي حجة في حسبانه فإنه ليس من رجال هذا المقال فلا يضر إجماع رجال هذا الشأن، فإن قلت : فما تصنع بقول أتباعه في ترجيح اعتباره على اعتبار الفارابي أنه موافق للغة والعرف ومنهم من هو من رجال هذا الشأن قلت إنهم أرادوا أنه قريب من العرف واللغة صرح به بعض منهم فتدبر ( وأما ) إطلاق المشتق باعتبار) الماضي) في المباشر أي على من باشر في الماضي لكنه غير مباشر في الحال فقيل وهو الأصح) المختار (مجاز مطلقاً، سواء أمكن بقاؤه كالأعراض الباقية من السواد والبياض (أو) لم يمكن) بقاؤه (كالسيالة) من الأعراض كالحركة ونحوها (وقيل حقيقة مطلقاً سواء أمكن بقاؤه أو لم يمكن (وهو مذهب أبي علي الجبائي (وابنه ، وقيل بالتفصيل بين ممكن (البقاء فقالوا فيه : الإطلاق باعتبار الماضي مجاز (وغيره) وقالوا فيه حقيقة لنا التكاذب عرفاً بين قولنا زيد قائم وزيد ليس بقائم) فإن أهل العرف يعدون هذين القولين متناقضين متكاذبين ولو صح للماضي) حقيقة كما زعموا صح للحال) بالاتفاق (فيجتمعان حقيقة إذ لا منافاة بين سلب القيام في زمان كالحال وثبوته في آخر كالماضي فبطل التكاذب هذا خلف فافهم واستدل) على المختار (بأنه يصح النفي أي يصح نفي المشتق عن مباشر الأصل في الماضي وصحة النفي من أمارات المجاز، أما صحة النفي فلأنه يقال : ليس بكاتب في الحال، فصدق الإطلاق، وهو ليس بكاتب مطلقاً (وقد
Issue: The use of the derived noun like 'al-darib' (the hitter) for the one who is directly hitting in the present is haqiqah by consensus. If you say: How is this consensus with their consensus that derived nouns by themselves do not indicate time at all? I say: It is valid; perhaps that consensus is on its individual usage, and this is in the case of composition to convey meaning, so reflect. And the use of the derived noun in consideration of the future (i.e., applying it to what is not currently being done but will be done in the future) is majaz by consensus; thus they said. I say about this (i.e., regarding the transmission of consensus) there is consideration. For Ibn Sina and his followers held in deriving the meaning of a proposition that the meaning of 'every white' is everything that is actually white in one of the three times; thus there is no consensus. The answer: He does not intend to explain language, but rather to explain that the valid proposition in logic and philosophy is what considers the truth of the title in actuality. Even if it were conceded that in language the consideration is according to this, what proof is in his conjecture? For he is not among the men of this art, so the consensus of the men of this field is not harmed. If you say: What do you make of the statement of his followers in preferring his view over that of al-Farabi that it agrees with language and custom, and among them are some of the men of this art? I say: They meant that it is close to custom and language; some of them explicitly stated that, so reflect. As for the use of the derived noun in consideration of the past (i.e., for one who performed in the past but is not direct in the present), it is said (and it is the most correct, the chosen view) that it is majaz mulaqan, whether its remaining is possible like permanent accidents such as blackness and whiteness, or its remaining is impossible like transient accidents such as motion and the like. And it is said that it is haqiqah mulaqan, whether its remaining is possible or not; that is the school of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son. And it is said with detail: regarding what can remain, they say: its use for the past is majaz; and for what cannot remain, they say it is haqiqah. We have the customary contradiction between our saying 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing', for the people of custom consider these two statements contradictory. If it were valid for the past as haqiqah as they claim, it would be valid for the present by consensus, so they would combine as haqiqah, since there is no contradiction between the negation of standing in one time (like the present) and its affirmation in another (like the past). Thus the contradiction would be nullified; this is a consequence of the opposite. So understand. And he argued for the chosen view that negation is valid (i.e., it is valid to negate the derived noun from the performer of the original action in the past, and the validity of negation is an indication of metaphor. As for the validity of negation, it is because it is said: 'He is not a writer in the present', so the utterance is true, and he is not a writer at all.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage discusses the temporal dimensions of mushtaqq usage. There is consensus that using a mushtaqq for the present (e.g., 'hitting' for someone actually hitting) is haqiqah (real). For the future (e.g., someone who will hit) is majaz (metaphorical) by consensus, though the author notes a disagreement with Ibn Sina who considered it real if true in one of the three times. The response is that Ibn Sina was discussing logic, not language. For the past, there is dispute: the preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that it is always metaphorical, whether the attribute can remain (like whiteness) or not (like motion). Some say it is always real, others differentiate. The proof for the preferred view is that we can truthfully say 'Zayd is not standing' about someone who stood in the past, showing contradiction if the past usage were real. This test of negation (sihhat al-nafy) indicates metaphor.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word used for its original sense.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative or metaphorical usage, not the original sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi preferred view on using a mushtaqq (e.g., 'kaatib' for someone who wrote in the past but is not writing now)?
A) Haqiqah always
B) Majaz always
C) Haqiqah if the attribute can remain, majaz otherwise
D) Haqiqah for present only, majaz for past and future

Key Takeaway
The temporal dimension of derived nouns in Hanafi usul: present is haqiqah, future is majaz by consensus, and past is majaz mulaqan (preferred), determined by the test of negation.
