Refutation of Mu'tazila on the Attribute of Speech
Refuting Mu'tazila claim that Allah is mutakallim without kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle of ishtiqaq (derivation) to refute the Mu'tazila denial of Allah's eternal attribute of speech and identify the role of ijma in affirming kalam nafsi.

Core Idea
The active participle requires subsistence: Allah is Mutakallim because speech subsists in His essence, not in a created body.

This passage tackles a foundational usul dispute: does Allah possess an eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi), or is He called ‘Speaker’ merely by virtue of creating speech in a body? The Hanafi school, supported by linguistic induction and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah, affirms the former. Let us examine the arguments and the precision of the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed term
The Mu'tazila and Hanafis agree that Allah is Mutakallim, but disagree on what that means. Pinpoint the definition of 'mutakallim' as it relates to the attribute of speech.
The term 'mutakallim' is an active participle (ism fa'il).
2
State the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila say Allah is Mutakallim without an attribute of speech; 'mutakallim' is derived from the act of producing speech (takallum), which is the composition of verbal speech in a body.
They deny kalam nafsi entirely.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic induction
The Hanafis argue from linguistic usage: an active participle is only applied to one in whom the action subsists. Denying this is obstinacy. This is based on a survey (istiqra) of Arabic usage.
The induction yields necessary (daruri) knowledge.
4
Apply the Hanafi response to the khallaq objection
The Mu'tazila point to 'khallaq' derived from 'khalq' (created thing). The response distinguishes real derivation (from verbs) from invented derivation (from nouns); 'khallaq' comes from effecting (ta'thir), not the created thing.
This protects the principle from the counterexample.
5
Evaluate the role of ijma
The author cites decisive consensus (ijma qati) that speech is an independent, real, eternal attribute. This consensus, from Ahl al-Sunnah, is a proof that cannot be overridden.
Ijma here is qati al-thubut and qati al-dalala.

مسألة (خلافاً للمعتزلة) في الأول (فإنهم قالوا إنه) أي الله تعالى متكلم ولا كلام له) عندهم لعدم قولهم بالكلام النفسي حتى يقوم بذاته تعالى واللفظي حادث لا يصح قیامه به لإفادة المعنى (بل) كلامه قائم (بجسم هو يخلقه فيه قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: إن أخذوا من الكلام فليس بسديد فإن المتكلم عندهم مشتق من التكلم وهو إحداث الكلام لإفادة المعنى عندهم والإحداث قائم بذاته تعالى كما أن المعلم يطلق على من قام به التعليم لا من قام به العلم هذا وقالوا في تحقيق الكلام إن ههنا كلاماً لفظياً هو هذا النظم المقروء وقدرة على تأليفه على الوجه المخصوص فهذه القدرة نفس ذاته تعالى فهو بذاته يقدر على هذا التأليف لا بقوة زائدة عليه، كما أنَّا نقدر بقوة زائدة، فهذا التأليف يقال له التكلم هذا وقد عرفت فيما مر أن هذه التمحلات باطلة قطعاً لدلالة الإجماع القاطع على أن الكلام صفة مستقلة غير القدرة والإرادة والعلم وهو صفة حقيقية قديمة والتكلم عندنا ليس إلا الاتصاف بتلك الصفة أو التأليف مطابقاً لتلك الصفة، وعلى كل تقدير لا بد من كلام له تعالى، وإن كنا لا نعلم كنه كلامه وقيامه، لكن نؤمن به كما لا نعلم كنه ذاته ونؤمن به ولتحقيق هذه المباحث مقام آخر وسنذكر ثمة منه لنا الاستقراء) فإنا استقرينا الإطلاقات اللغوية، وحدث لنا علم ضروري بأنه لا يطلق اسم الفاعل إلا على ما قام به الفعل وإنكاره مكابرة قطعاً (وما قيل) نقضاً عليه أنه يقال : زيد متكلم بهذا اللفظ فليس هذا الإطلاق باعتبار الكلام النفسي بل باعتبار اللفظي (مع أن اللفظ قائم بالهواء المجاور لفمه فصح إطلاق اسم الفاعل مع قيام الفعل بغيره (فهو) أي اعتبار قيام اللفظ بالهواء المجاور (دقة فلسفية، وعرف اللغة مبني على الظاهر) وفي ظاهر الأمر يعلم أن الألفاظ قائمة بالفم على أنه لا دليل للفلاسفة على عدم قيام الكلام باللسان أو الفم، ومنهم فرقة يسمون بالإشراقية يزعمون أن بالحركة يحدث للأفلاك أصوات قائمة بها، ومن زعم منهم بالهواء بين الفلكين فشنع صاحب المطارحات عليه، وأثبت أن الأصوات تقوم بالمجردات وبأجسام أخر غير الهواء والماء، هذا إنما يحتاج إليه إذا قلنا التكلم الاتصاف بالكلام ولو قلنا : التكلم تأليف الألفاظ على حسب الكلام الحقيقي القائم بالنفس، فلا يضر قيام الألفاظ بالهواء فافهم المعتزلة قالوا : أطلق الخالق المشتق من الخلق والخلق هو المخلوق) الغير القائم بذات الخالق فقد صح حمل اسم الفاعل مع عدم قيام المبدأ ومن ههنا علم أن مشايخنا لم يأخذوا قول المعتزلة من مسألة الكلام (والقول) في الجواب بأنه غير محل النزاع لأن الكلام في اشتقاق اسم الفاعل من مبدأ مع قيامه بغيره، وههنا الخلق غير قائم بالغير، بل مجموع الجواهر والأعراض الغير القائمة بشيء ليس بسديد، لأن الفرق تحكم فإن أوضاع أسماء الفاعلين على نمط واحد (نعم : الاشتقاقات الجعلية كالخمار والحداد) لم يرد بالاشتقاق الجعلي ما كان مخترعاً من غير أن يكون من الواضع، حتى يرد أنها ليست جعلية بل واقعة في استعمال البلغاء، بل ما يعتبر اشتقاقه من الجوامد على غير طريق الاشتقاق من الأفعال فإن الحداد من يعمل بالحديد وطريق الاشتقاق من الفعل من يتصف به ليست من محل النزاع، لأنها مشتقات من الجوامد لا من الفعل) والكلام في من الفعل والكلام في المشتقات من الفعل فتدبر فإنه دقيق، وإن حملت عبارة المجيب عليه فله وجه غير بعيد (والجواب أن الخلق هو التأثير) المخلوق وهو قائم به تعالى فلا نسلم أنه المخلوق، فعادت المعتزلة (فقالوا) ليس الخلق التأثير لأنه إن قدم التأثير (قدم العالم إذ لا تأثير و الحال أنه لا أثر) فالأثر لازم للتأثير (وإن حدث التأثير احتاج إلى تأثير آخر وتسلسل ولا يخفى على من له أدنى فطانة أنه لو تم هذا لزم أن لا يكون للتأثير واقعية فلا يكون الباري سبحانه مؤثراً في الحادث، وهو كفر صريح (والجواب : أن للقدرة تعلقاً حادثاً به الحدوث، فللتعلق نسبة إلى ذي القدرة وهذه النسبة هي التأثير والخلق وباعتباره الاشتقاق فيختار الشق الثاني، وهو حدوث التعلق، ولا بد له تعلق آخر وهي اعتبارات والاعتبارات وإن كانت محتاجة إلى المؤثر كالحقيقيات) لا كما أنها لاعتباريتها لا تحتاج إلى مؤثر وتأثير لكن التسلسل فيها ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار) ولهذا اشتهر أن استحالة التسلسل مختصة بالأمور العينية لكنه غير وافٍ إذ هذا التسلسل في جانب المبدأ وهو باطل قطعاً اعتبارية كانت أو حقيقية وهي لا تنقطع عن الواقع بانقطاع الاعتبار، فإنه لو انقطعت لزم عدم العالم لأنها علل فافهم والحق أن يقال : إن هذا التعلق قديم لكن تعلق بأن يوجد المعلول بعد علة كذا، فحينئذ لا يلزم القدم ولا التسلسل، فإن أعيد وقيل: لم تعلق بهذا النمط، فالأصوب عندي أن يقال : لأن الممكن لم يكن صالحاً لأن يوجد على هذا الوجه فتدبر وأنصف .
Issue: (Contrary to the Mu'tazila) in the first (they said that He) i.e. Allah Almighty is a speaker without having speech) according to them because they do not affirm inner speech (kalam nafsi) that subsists in His essence, and the verbal speech is originated and cannot subsist in Him to convey meaning. (Rather) His speech subsists (in a body which He creates in it. The author of 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: If they derive from speech, it is not correct, because for them the speaker is derived from speaking (takallum) which is producing speech to convey meaning according to them, and production subsists in His essence, just as 'teacher' is applied to one in whom teaching subsists, not knowledge. And they said in analyzing speech: There is here verbal speech, which is this recited composition, and a power to compose it in the specific manner. This power is His essence itself, so He by His essence is able to compose, not by an additional power, just as we are able by an additional power. This composition is called speaking. And you have known previously that these fabrications are absolutely false due to the decisive consensus that speech is an independent attribute distinct from power, will, and knowledge, and it is a real eternal attribute. For us, speaking is nothing but being described with that attribute, or composition matching that attribute. In any case, He must have speech, even if we do not know the reality of His speech and its subsistence, but we believe in it just as we do not know the reality of His essence and believe in it. For investigation of these topics there is another place and we will mention there (the proof for us is induction) for we have surveyed linguistic usages and acquired necessary knowledge that the active participle is not applied except to that in which the action subsists; denying this is sheer obstinacy. (And what is said) as a counterexample that it is said: Zaid is speaking with this utterance, this application is not based on inner speech but on verbal speech (even though the utterance subsists in the air adjacent to his mouth, so the active participle is correctly applied while the action subsists in something else) then (it i.e. considering the utterance to subsist in the adjacent air is a philosophical subtlety, and the linguistic convention is based on the apparent) and in appearance it is known that words subsist in the mouth. Moreover, there is no proof for the philosophers that speech does not subsist on the tongue or mouth. Among them is a group called the Illuminationists who claim that through motion sounds are generated in the spheres. And whoever among them claims that the air between the spheres is the substrate, the author of al-Matarahat criticized this and established that sounds subsist in abstract entities and in other bodies besides air and water. This is needed only if we say speaking is being described with speech; but if we say speaking is composing words according to real speech subsisting in the self, then the subsistence of words in air does not harm. Understand. The Mu'tazila said: 'Al-Khallaq' (the Creator) is derived from creation (khalq), and creation is the created thing) which does not subsist in the essence of the Creator, so the active participle is applied despite the absence of the origin's subsistence. From here it is known that our scholars did not accept the Mu'tazila's statement regarding speech. (And the response) that it is not the subject of dispute because we are discussing derivation of the active participle from an origin while it subsists in something else, while here creation does not subsist in something else, rather it is a collection of substances and accidents not subsisting in anything, is not sound because the distinction is arbitrary; the formations of active participles are of one type. (Yes, the invented derivations like 'al-khammār' (wine seller) and 'al-ḥaddād' (blacksmith)) – by 'invented derivation' is not meant something invented by the language coiner, so that it could be objected that they are not invented but occur in eloquent usage; rather it refers to derivation from nouns not following the path of derivation from verbs. The blacksmith is one who works with iron, and the path of derivation from a verb is one who is described with it; these are not the subject of dispute because they are derivatives from nouns, not from verbs) and the discussion is about derivatives from verbs. So ponder, for it is subtle. And if the responder's expression is interpreted accordingly, it is not far-fetched. (The response is that creation is the effecting) i.e. the created thing, and it subsists in Him, so we do not accept that it is the created thing. Then the Mu'tazila returned (and said) creation is not effecting because if effecting is pre-eternal, then the world would be pre-eternal, since there is no effect while there is no effect; effect is necessary for effecting. And if effecting occurs newly, it requires another effecting, leading to infinite regress. It is not hidden from anyone with some intelligence that if this were valid, effecting would have no reality and the Almighty would not be an effecter in the originated, which is clear disbelief. (The response: Power has a newly occurring relation [ta'alluq] to the originated at the time of origination. So the relation has a connection to the possessor of power, and this connection is effecting and creation, and based on it derivation is considered. So the second option is chosen, which is the occurrence of the relation, and it requires another relation, and these are mental considerations. Although mental considerations need an effecter like real things, not that because of their being mental they do not need an effecter and effecting, but the regress in them ends with the end of consideration.) Hence it is well-known that the impossibility of regress is specific to actual things, but it is insufficient, because this regress is in the aspect of the origin and it is absolutely invalid, whether considerations or real, and they do not end from reality by the end of consideration, for if they ended, the non-existence of the world would follow because they are causes. Understand. And the truth is to say: This relation is pre-eternal, but it is related to the existence of the effect after such a cause, so then neither pre-eternity nor regress follows. If it is objected: why is it related in this manner? The most correct in my opinion is to say: Because the possible was not fit to exist in that way. So ponder and be fair.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This is an usul dispute passage. The author refutes the Mu'tazila position that Allah is called 'Mutakallim' (Speaker) without an eternal attribute of speech. The Hanafi position affirms that speech (kalam) is an eternal attribute of Allah, and that the active participle 'mutakallim' is derived from this attribute which subsists in Him. The Mu'tazila argue that speech is created in a body, and that 'mutakallim' is derived from the act of speaking (takallum) which they define as producing verbal speech. They also point to derived names like 'khallaq' (Creator) which they claim is derived from 'khalq' (creation) and applied to Allah even though the created thing does not subsist in Him. The author responds by distinguishing between real derivation (from an attribute that subsists in the subject) and invented derivation (from nouns like 'khammar' and 'haddad'). He further argues that the Mu'tazila's objection based on 'khallaq' fails because creation (khalq) is actually the effecting (ta'thir) which does subsist in Allah. The Mu'tazila then raise the problem of pre-eternity and infinite regress regarding effecting, which the author refutes by explaining that the relation of power to the effect is newly occurring and that regress in mental considerations is not problematic. The passage concludes with the correct Hanafi position that the relation is pre-eternal but related to the existence of the effect after a certain cause, avoiding both pre-eternity and regress.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): The eternal attribute of speech subsisting in Allah's essence, which is the source of verbal expression.
اسم فاعل (ism fa'il): Active participle; applied to one in whom the action or attribute subsists.
اشتقاق (ishtiqāq): Derivation of a word from another word, here specifically from a verb (fa'il) indicating the subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi refutation of the Mu'tazila, from what is 'khallaq' (Creator) derived?
A) From the created thing (makhluq)
B) From effecting (ta'thir)
C) From the verbal speech (kalam lafzi)
D) From the power (qudra)

Key Takeaway
Allah's attribute of speech is eternal and subsisting in His essence; the linguistic principle of active participles and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah establish this.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
6 min
The Hanafi Doctrine on Common Words (Mushtarak): Necessity and Occurrence
Debating the necessity, possibility, and actual occurrence of common words in Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the existence of common words (mushtarak), identify the key proofs for and against, and evaluate the tarjih.

Core Idea
Common words exist in Arabic, proven by the Quranic term 'al-qur' used for both menstruation and purity.

The concept of mushtarak (common words) is critical in Hanafi usul al-fiqh because such words require context for interpretation. This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut examines the dispute over whether common words occur in language. The Hanafi school, following the lead of the Quranic example 'al-qur', affirms their occurrence, while some scholars, notably Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, deny it, especially between opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and main positions
State the dispute: does mushtarak (common words) exist? There are four views: wujub (necessity), istihalah (impossibility), imkan ma'a 'adam al-wuqu' (possibility without occurrence), and imkan ma'a al-wuqu' (possibility with occurrence = al-ashah).
The text clarifies that wujub and istihalah are relative, not absolute.
2
Analyze the scriptural proof from 'al-qur'
Examine how the word 'al-qur' in Quran 2:228 is given as evidence that a single word can have two opposite meanings (menstruation and purity), proving commonality between opposites.
This directly counters those who deny commonality between opposites.
3
Examine the rational argument from infinite meanings
The Hanafi argument: meanings are infinite while words are finite, so without common words, many meanings would lack expression. The opponent's responses and the Hanafi rebuttals are detailed.
Key rebuttal: degrees of numbers are infinite and different, proving infinite different meanings.
4
Evaluate the opponent's main objections
List the objections: commonality only between different meanings, general placement for specifics suffices, what we understand is finite, words are finite due to finite letters, metaphorical expression works. Note the Hanafi responses.
Pay attention to the refutation of general placement: it only works for similar individuals, not different universals.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the preferred view
Recognize the practical consequence: the existence of mushtarak affects interpretation of ambiguous Quranic and Hadith terms. The text concludes that occurrence is al-ashah (most correct).
This is the mufta bihi position in Hanafi usul.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية المبادىء مخالفة لها بالذات والحقيقة، ووجوداتها غير رابطية ويكون قيامها بالموضوع شرطاً للحمل فافهم (ثم إنهم قالوا إن أسماء الزمان والمكان والآلة تدل على ذوات مخصوصة من الزمان والمكان والآلة، وإن كانت مبهمات بالنظر إلى أفرادها من الأزمنة المخصوصة، والأمكنة كذلك، والآلات المخصوصة، فالخصوصية من الزمان والمكان والآلة غير داخلة (وربما يمنع) في الأطول اعتبار ذوات مخصوصة لجواز أن يكون الخصوص من اللوازم) فالمعتبر فيها الشيء المطلق ومصداقه الخصوصيات فإن شيئاً يقع فيه الضرب) الذي هو مفهوم المضرب (مثلاً ليس إلا الزمان أو المكان فتدبر). (الفصل الثاني) (وهو) أي المفرد (إن تعدد معناه فإن وضع لكل من المعاني المتعددة (ابتداء) من غير اعتبار أنه كان موضوعاً لمعنى قبل فمشترك وإلا يوضع لكل ابتداء فإن ترك استعماله في الأول ونقل إلى الثاني بحيث يفهم من غير قرينة (المناسبة فنقول أولا لمناسبة فمرتجل وإلا) أي وإن لم يترك الأول بل تارة يستعمل فيه وتارة في الثاني ( فحقيقة) في المنقول منه ومجاز) في المنقول إليه . مسألة المشترك قد اختلف فيه فقيل بوجوبه ، وقيل باستحالته المراد بالوجوب الضرورة بالنظر إلى معاش العباد لا الوجوب الذاتي، وكيف يتفوه به عاقل والمراد بالاستحالة ضده (وقيل بإمكانه فقيل بعدم وقوعه، وقيل بوقوعه وهو الأصح قد عرفت معنى الوجوب والاستحالة، فحينئذ لم يكن القول بالوجوب إلا بالوجوب بالغير، وهو الإمكان مع الوقوع وكذا الاستحالة المقابلة له، ليست إلا الاستحالة بالغير، وليست إلا الإمكان مع عدم الوقوع، فالأقوال إذن قولان : الوقوع ،وعدمه، ولذا أورد استدلال الفريقين فقط (لنا القرء) موضوع (للحيض والطهر معاً لتبادرهما حين الإطلاق، وإذا ثبت كونه مشتركاً بينهما وهما ضدان (فسقط منع جماعة الاشتراك بين الضدين و روي عن الإمام فخر الدين الرازي منعه بين النقيضين) لكمال التباعد (واستدل) على الوقوع بل الوجوب (أولاً) بأنه لو لم يكن) أي لم يوجد في كلام العرب الخلت أكثر (المسميات عن الألفاظ بإزائها واللازم باطل، لأنه حينئذ يفوت التعبير عنها، والملازمة (لأنها أي المسميات (غير متناهية والألفاظ متناهية فلا يفي كل واحد منها بإزاء كل واحد من المسميات بأن يكون واحد بإزاء واحد، بل يبقى الأكثر من المسميات والألفاظ تنفد، وإنما كانت متناهية لتركبها من حروف متناهية والمركب من المتناهي متناه، (وأجيب) عن هذا الاستدلال بأن الاشتراك إنما يكون بين معان متضادة أو متخالفة) فإن أردتم أن هذه المعاني غير متناهية فلا نقنع به ولا نسلم أنها غير متناهية وإن أردتم أن مطلق المعاني غير متناهية فمسلم، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تخلو الجزئيات عن الأوضاع لها بخصوصها وتكون الألفاظ المتناهية موضوعة بإزاء المتخالفات ويعبر عن هذه الجزئيات بالإضافة، هذا وأيضاً غاية ما يلزم على هذا التقرير لو سلم استحالة الخلو الاشتراك بين الجزئيات المتماثلة وهو غير المدعي فإن المدعي وقوع الاشتراك بين الكليات المتخالفة (وفيه أن مراتب الأعداد غير متناهية وهي أنواع متخالفة فثبت عدم تناهي المعاني المتخالفة، وارتفع المنع عن الشق الأول وهو المختار، وما قيل إن التخالف النوعي بين مراتب الأعداد ممنوع، بل استدل على خلافه، فليس بشيء لأنه لم يرد بالتخالف النوعي لم يرد بالتخالف النوعي تخالف النوع الحقيقي بل ما يعد في العرف تخالفاً نوعياً، وأيضاً أصحاب الكلام والفلاسفة يعدون أمثال هذه المنوع مكابرة فتفكر (وبه) أي بهذا الجواب اندفع ما قيل لا نسلم خلو المسميات عند عدم الاشتراك كيف و(إنه يجوز وضع لفظ لكثير من المعاني) مرة واحدة من قبيل الوضع العام للموضوع له الخاص، وذلك) الاندفاع (لأنه) أي الوضع العام للموضوع له الخاص إنما يكون بين الأفراد (المتماثلة) أي من حيث إنها متماثلة (دون) (المتخالفة أي من حيث إنها ،متخالفة لأن الهذية في اسم الإشارة إنما تعتبر من حيث إنها محسوسة ومشار إليها والإنسان والفرس سواء في هذا المعنى وليس المراد التخالف النوعي حتى يرد عليه ما يرد ومراتب الأعداد متخالفة، فلا بد بإزائها بالتماثل عدم من وضع بتلك الحيثية، وإذ ليس فالخلو لازم كذا في الحاشية والإيراد عليه بتجويز جريان الوضع العام في المتخالفة من حيث التخالف يعد من المكابرات كيف لا ولا بد فيه من معرفة متماثلة الجزئيات الموضوع لها اللفظ بالوجه الكلي المشترك فيها، فهي من حيث هي هذا في الوجه العام موضوع لها لا غير فتدبر نعم يرد عليه أن وجوب الوضع من حيث التخالف ممنوع : بل يمكن أن يوضع لها اسم من حيث التماثل المذكور ويعبر حين الإفادة بهذا الاسم وتفهم المعاني بالقرينة كما لو كان اللفظ مشتركاً فتدبر و أجيب بأن ما نعقله متناه، وهو المحتاج إليه حين الإفادة فإن أريد خلو ما نعقله عن الوضع له فممنوع فإنها متناهية، كالألفاظ، فيجوز التساوي بينهما، وإن أريد خلو غير المتعقل فبطلان التالي ممنوع (وفيه أنه) أي ما نعقله (غير متناه، بمعنى لا يقف عند حد، وإن كان متناهياً بالتناهي الكمي (وهو المراد) أي غير المتناهي اللايقفي هو مراد المستدل (بل) (الجواب عن الدليل (منع) أن الألفاظ متناهية و ( أن المركب من المتناهي متناه وإنما يكون المركب من المتناهي متناهياً (لو كان) التركيب بمرات متناهية) وهو ممنوع بل عدد التركيب غير واقف إلى حد كالمعاني، فإن قلت: هذا لأن المفرد لا يتركب بأزيد من سبعة أحرف والمركب لا يتركب بأزيد من ألفاظ، فلا يكون عدد التركيب غير واقف قلت : إن مراتب الأعداد لا يعبر عنها إلا بالمركب ألفاظ كثيرة، فكذا هذا، فكذا ،هذا نعم لا يصح أن يجعل المركب من ألفاظ فوق ثلاثة أسماء واحداً كبعلبك، ولا حاجة إليه، بل يكفي الوضع ولو بالوضع الذي يكون في المركبات، سواء جعل اسماً واحداً أو لا فتدبر (وأيضاً) الجواب بتسليم لزوم الخلو ومنع بطلانه ولا يضر الإفادة، إذ (يجوز التعبير بالألفاظ المجازية ولا بأس به قيل: أكثر اللغة مجاز، وأيضاً الجواب بالنقض، فإنه يقال : (لو تم الدليل لكان بعض الألفاظ موضوعاً لمعان غير متناهية) إذ لكان البعض المتناهي بإزاء المتناهي منه، ويبقى الباقي الغير المتناهي خالياً، قال في لا من يصح، ثلاثة
The third article on linguistic principles: Principles are contrary to them in essence and reality, and their existences are not copulative, and their subsistence in the subject is a condition for predication, so understand. Then they said that nouns of time, place, and instrument indicate specific entities of time, place, and instrument, although they are vague with respect to their individuals of specific times, places likewise, and specific instruments. So specificity from time, place, and instrument is not included, and perhaps it is prevented in the longer consideration of specific entities because specificity may be among the necessary concomitants. So what is considered in them is the absolute thing, and its instances are the specificities, for something in which striking occurs, which is the concept of a place of striking, for example, is nothing but time or place, so reflect. (The second chapter) (It) i.e., the single word (if its meaning is multiple then if it is placed for each of the multiple meanings (initially) without consideration that it was placed for a meaning before, then it is common; otherwise it is placed for each initially. If its usage in the first is abandoned and transferred to the second such that it is understood without a context clue (the appropriateness, then we say firstly: for appropriateness it is coined; otherwise) i.e., if the first is not abandoned but sometimes used in it and sometimes in the second, (then it is literal) in what is transferred from, and metaphorical in what is transferred to. Issue: The common word has been disputed: it is said it is necessary, and it is said it is impossible. By necessity is meant the necessity with regard to the livelihood of people, not essential necessity, and how could a rational person utter it? And by impossibility is meant its opposite. (And it is said it is possible: then it is said it does not occur, and it is said it occurs, which is the most correct view. You have known the meaning of necessity and impossibility; then, the statement of necessity is only necessity through another, which is possibility with occurrence; similarly, the impossibility opposite to it is only impossibility through another, which is possibility with non-occurrence. So the views are then only two: occurrence and non-occurrence; hence only the arguments of the two parties are presented. (For us, 'al-qur' is placed (for menstruation and purity together because they immediately come to mind when used absolutely; and if it is established that it is common between them, and they are opposites, then the prohibition of some people of commonality between opposites falls. And it is narrated from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi his prohibition of it between contradictories) due to extreme distance. (And he argued) for occurrence, even necessity, (firstly) that if it were not (i.e., if common words did not exist in the speech of the Arabs, then most meanings would lack words for them; the consequent is false, because then expression of them would be lost; and the correlation is (because the meanings (are infinite while the words are finite, so each one cannot suffice for each one of the meanings by being one for one; rather most of the meanings would remain and the words would be exhausted; and they are only finite because they are composed of finite letters, and the composite of the finite is finite). (And it was answered) to this argument that commonality occurs only between opposite or different meanings. If you mean that these meanings are infinite, we are not convinced and do not accept that they are infinite. If you mean that meanings absolutely are infinite, that is granted, but why is it not permissible that the particulars lack specific assignments for them, and the finite words are assigned for the different ones, and expression of these particulars is by addition? Also, the utmost necessary by this account, if impossibility of lack of commonality is conceded, is commonality between similar particulars, which is not what is claimed; for the claimant claims the occurrence of commonality between different universals. (And in it that the degrees of numbers are infinite and they are different types; so the non-finiteness of different meanings is established, and the prohibition of the first proposition is removed, and it is the chosen view. And what is said that the specific difference between the degrees of numbers is prohibited, rather it was argued against it, is nothing; because by specific difference is not meant the difference of true species, but rather what is considered in custom as specific difference. Also the theologians and philosophers consider such denial as obstinacy, so reflect. (And by it) i.e., by this answer, what was said is repelled: we do not concede the lack of meanings when there is no commonality; rather, (it is permissible to place one word for many meanings) at once, of the type of general placement for a specific thing. And that repulsion is because general placement for a specific thing occurs only between similar individuals, i.e., in terms of their being similar, not between different ones, i.e., in terms of their being different, because the 'thisness' in the demonstrative pronoun is considered only in terms of being sensible and pointed to; man and horse are equal in this meaning; and specific difference is not intended such that what is objected would be objected; and the degrees of numbers are different, so there must be for them by similarity a lack of placement with that aspect; since there is not, then lack is necessary, as in the gloss. And the objection against it by permitting the operation of general placement in different ones in terms of being different is considered among obstinacies; how not, and it requires knowledge of similar particulars of the word by the universal aspect common among them; they are, in terms of being this, in the general aspect placed for them, nothing else; so reflect. Yes, it is objected against it that the necessity of placement in terms of being different is prohibited; rather it is possible to place a name for them in terms of the aforementioned similarity and express with this name when communicating, and the meanings are understood by context, as if the word were common; so reflect. And it was answered that what we understand is finite; and it is what is needed when communicating; if it is intended that what we understand lacks placement for it, it is prohibited; for it is finite like words, so equality between them is permissible; and if what is beyond understanding is intended, then the falsity of the consequent is prohibited. (And in it that) i.e., what we understand (is infinite, in the sense of not stopping at a limit, although it is finite by quantitative finiteness; (and that is what is intended) i.e., the non-stopping infinite is what the arguer intends. (Rather) (the answer to the argument (is preventing) that the words are finite and (that the composite of the finite is finite; and that the composite of the finite is finite (only if) the composition is by finite degrees, and that is prohibited; rather the number of composition does not stop at a limit like meanings. If you say: this is because a single word is not composed of more than seven letters, and a compound is not composed of more than words, so the number of composition is not non-stopping. I say: the degrees of numbers are not expressed except by many compound words; similarly this; yes, it is not valid to make a compound of more than three words as a single name like Baalbek, and there is no need for it; rather placement is sufficient, even by the placement that occurs in compounds, whether it is made a single name or not; so reflect. (Also) the answer is by conceding the necessity of lack and preventing its invalidity; and it does not harm communication, for (it is permissible to express with metaphorical words and there is no harm; it is said: most of language is metaphorical. Also the answer is by counter-argument, for it is said: (if the argument were sound, then some words would be placed for infinite meanings) because then some finite words would be for a finite part, and the remaining infinite part would be empty; he said in... 'la min yasihh, thalaatha'.)
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion (from a Hanafi usul commentary) of the concept of mushtarak (common/ambiguous words) and the dispute over its existence in language. The author begins by clarifying that 'necessity' here refers to practical necessity for human communication, not essential necessity, and 'impossibility' is similarly relative. The correct view, according to the author, is that common words do occur (al-waqi'). The primary evidence is the word 'al-qur' (Quranic term for menstrual period), which is used for both menstruation and purity; since it is commonly used for both opposite meanings, it proves the occurrence of commonality between opposites, countering those who deny it. The central rational argument is presented: meanings are infinite while words are finite, so without common words, many meanings would lack expressions. The opponent's responses are systematically refuted: (1) that commonality is only between different meanings, not similar; the author argues that degrees of numbers are infinite and different, proving non-finiteness of different meanings; (2) that general placement for specifics can cover many meanings; but this only applies to similar individuals, not different universals; (3) that what we understand is finite; but the author counters that what we understand is infinite in the sense of not stopping at a limit; (4) that words are finite due to finite letters; but compound words can be infinite in number; (5) that metaphorical expression suffices; the author notes that this is possible but does not negate the need for commonality. The discussion reflects the Hanafi usul position that mushtarak exists, and engages with opposing views, notably from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word placed for multiple meanings initially, each without considering prior usage.
الاشتراك (al-ishtirak): The phenomenon of a word having multiple common meanings.
القرء (al-qur'): A term used for both menstruation and purity; a prime example of mushtarak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Quranic example used in this passage to prove the occurrence of common words (mushtarak)?
A) The word 'salat' for prayer
B) The word 'zakat' for charity
C) The word 'qur' for menstruation and purity
D) The word 'hajj' for pilgrimage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms the occurrence of common words in Arabic, as evidenced by the Quranic term 'al-qur' and rational necessity.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
The Dispute on Using Common Words for All Meanings
A detailed refutation of the claim that a common word can be used for all its meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using a common word for multiple meanings and evaluate the opposing proof using the Quranic verses cited.

Core Idea
A common word cannot be used for all its meanings at once; usage requires intending one specific meaning.

This passage tackles a foundational usul dispute: can a word with multiple meanings (mushtarak) be used for all of them simultaneously? The Hanafi school firmly rejects this, arguing that usage requires a single intended meaning. The passage refutes opposing arguments, including Quranic examples like sujud and salah, and clarifies that even apparent generality in negation is metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
State the core issue
Identify the disputed ruling: can a common word be used for all its meanings at once (عموم المشترك حقيقة)?
The passage starts by asserting that intending one meaning is a condition for usage.
2
Analyze the Hanafi position
Extract the Hanafi argument: usage requires specification (تخصيص) for one meaning; using for multiple contradicts placement. Note that the exception in negation is considered مجاز, not حقيقة.
The text refutes several objections to this position.
3
Identify the opposing proof
Locate the opponents' evidence: Quran 22:18 and 33:56, where words like يسجد and يصلون are attributed to diverse subjects, ostensibly requiring different meanings.
The opponents claim this shows a common word can be used for multiple meanings.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: each word has a single core meaning (غاية الخضوع for sujud, الاعتناء بإظهار الشرف for salah) that applies to all subjects, so they are not truly common in those contexts.
The response also suggests grammatical alternatives (e.g., omitted predicate).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical impact: this dispute affects how ambiguous texts (e.g., 'qur'') are interpreted in fiqh rulings. The Hanafi position requires context to specify one meaning.
The passage emphasizes methodological clarity over mere acceptance of ambiguity.

يشهد الاستقراء بها (فهو ) أي قصد أحدهما شرط استعماله (لغة) وإلا لما تبادر (فالحكم بظهوره في الكل تحكم باطل، بل لا يصح الاستعمال فيهما ولو نادراً لانتفاء شرطه، ومن ههنا لاح صحة تقرير صدر الشريعة أنه إما موضوع لكل مع الآخر أو بدون الآخر أو لكل مطلقاً، والأول باطل، وإلا لما الاستعمال في أحدهما حقيقة، وعلى الثاني المطلوب، وكذا على الثالث لأن الاستعمال إنما هو لوضعه وتخصيصه له فهو ينافي وضعه وتخصيصه للآخر فلا يجوز الاستعمال في كل منهما، وسقط عنه ما قال في التلويح إنه ليس المراد بالتخصيص ههنا أن اللفظ له لا لغيره حتى يلزم التنافي بل المراد أن المعنى إنما هو مخصوص بهذا اللفظ من بين الألفاظ لا بغيره من الألفاظ، وذلك لأن الاستعمال في معنى لا يكون إلا من جهة وضعه له، ويلزم من استعماله لذلك الوضع إرادته فقط كما بينه المصنف، فلزم التنافي قطعاً ومن ههنا علم اندفاع قول المصححين للعموم (حقيقة أنه وضع لكل مطلقاً) أي من غير لحاظ أن يكون مع الآخر أو بدونه فإذا قصد الكل كان الاستعمال (فيما وضع) المشترك (له) فكان حقيقة (وذلك) الاندفاع (لأن الوضع لا يكفي للحقيقة، بل يجب الاستعمال لها حتى لو وضع اللفظ ولم يستعمل أصلاً لم يكن حقيقة (ومن شرطه عدم الجمع فلو استعمل فيهما كان خطأ لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً واندفع أيضاً ما أورد في «شرح الشرح» أنه لو تبادر أحدهما كان متواطئاً لا مشتركاً، وذلك لأنه ليس يتبادر المفهوم المردد الشامل لهما، بل يتبادر كل بدلاً بأن يكون هذا هو المراد أو ذلك فافهم، واندفع أيضاً ما قيل في «شرح المختصر أن اللفظ كان لواحد واحد فإذا أريد الكل كان كل جزء لما استعمل فيه فيكون مجازاً، كيف لا واللفظ كان لواحد واحد، وأريد الآن هو مع الغير، وذلك لأن الكلام في إرادة كل بحيث يكون مناط الحكم أولاً، وبالذات كل واحد واحد لا الكل بما هو الكل، حتى يكون كل جزء مراداً ويكون مجازاً ،البتة وإرادة الكل من قبيل إطلاق الجزء على الكل إنما يصح إذا كان الكل بحيث يكون له اسم على حدة ويكون بحيث ينتفي بانتفاء الجزء عرفاً، هذا وقال الشيخ ابن الهمام بتجويز العموم في النفي أنه يراد كل ما هو مسمى بهذا الاسم، فيعم في النفي، لأن المبهم إذا ورد عليه النفي يعم، ولا يخفى أنه ليس حينئذ من عموم المشترك في شيء، بل إرادة معنى مجازي واقع تحت النفي، فيعم ولعل هذا هو مراد صاحب «الهداية»، وحينئذ فلا نزاع المجوّزون (قالوا: قال الله تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ تَرَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ يَسْجُدُ لَهُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَمَن فِي الْأَرْضِ وَالشَّمْسُ وَالْقَمَرُ وَالنُّجُومُ وَالْجِبَالُ وَالشَّجَرُ وَالدَّوَاتُ وَكَثِيرٌ مِّنَ النَّاسِ [الحج: (۱۸] (الآية، والسجود من الناس وضع الجبهة على الأرض، ومن غيرهم غيره) وهو الخضوع القهري، وقد أريدا من لفظ يسجد لأنه أسند إليهم جميعاً (وأيضاً) قال الله تعالى : ( إنَّ اللهَ وَمَلَيْكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيُّ ) [الأحزاب: ٥٦] وهي من الله رحمة ومن الملائكة استغفار) وقد أريد أيضاً لفظ يصلون لأنه أسند إلى الله تعالى وإلى الملائكة (والجواب) عن الأول من (أن السجود) حقيقة غاية الخضوع، وهو في الإنسان يتحقق بوضع الجبهة اختياراً، فإنه غاية الخضوع في ذي الجبهة المختار ( وفي غيره) أي غير الإنسان (بغيره) أي بغير وضع الجبهة بل بالانقهار تحت حكمه، فلا يرد ما في التلويح ( إن أريد) السجود القهري شمل الكل) أي كل الناس (فلا وجه لتخصيص كثير من الناس و إن أريد السجود الاختياري) فهو (لا يتأتى في غيرهم) فلا الإسناد : يصح وجه الدفع ظاهر على أن لنا أن نقول : أريد القهري الشامل لكل الناس ولا تخصيص ههنا، إذ يجوز أن تكون من للبيان والمعنى والله أعلم. والله يسجد كثير وهم الناس كلهم لكون اللام للاستغراق (و) الجواب عن الثاني (أن الصلاة موضوعة للاعتناء بإظهار الشرف ويتحقق هذا الاعتناء منه تعالى بالرحمة و يتحقق من غيره بدعائه له) فإنه أيضاً نوع اعتناء بإظهار الشرف وإنما كانت موضوعة للاعتناء المذكور (تقديماً للاشتراك المعنوي على اللفظي وأهل التفسير على إضمار خبر (للأول أي أن الله يصلي وملائكته يصلون (كقوله : نحن بما عندنا وأنت بما عندك راض والرأي مختلف أي نحن بما عندنا راضون فحذف الخبر .
Common usage bears witness that intending one of the meanings is a condition for its use linguistically; otherwise it would not be understood. Thus, ruling that it appears in all meanings is an invalid arbitrary judgment. Rather, using it for both meanings is not valid, even rarely, because the condition is absent. Hence, the correctness of Sadr al-Shari'ah's statement becomes clear: it is either placed for each meaning together with the other or without the other or for each absolutely. The first is false; otherwise it would not be used for one of them as a reality. The second is what is required. Similarly, the third, because usage is only for its placement and specification, which contradicts its placement and specification for the other; so using it for each is not permissible. What was said in al-Talwih falls away: that specification here does not mean the word is solely for it such that contradiction follows, but rather that the meaning is specifically associated with this word among words. That is because usage in a meaning only comes from its placement, and using it for that placement necessitates intending only that meaning, as the author clarified; contradiction necessarily follows. From here, the refutation of those who affirm generality as a reality becomes known: that it is placed for each absolutely, i.e., without considering being with the other or without it. So if one intends all, the usage is in what the common word is placed for, thus it is a reality. That refutation is because placement alone is insufficient for reality; rather, usage is required. Even if the word is placed and not used at all, it is not a reality. Among its conditions is that it not be combined; if used for both, it is an error, neither reality nor metaphor. Also refuted is what was objected in Sharh al-Sharh: that if one meaning is immediately understood, the word would be univocal, not common. That is because the ambiguous concept encompassing both is not understood; rather, each is understood alternatively—either this is intended or that. Also refuted is what was said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: that the word was for one, so if all is intended, each part is used in what it was placed for, making it a metaphor. This is because the discussion concerns intending each individually as the basis of the ruling, not the totality. Intending all is a case of naming the whole by its part, which is only valid if the whole has a separate name and is conventionally negated by the absence of the part. Ibn al-Humam permitted generality in negation, meaning every individual named by this word is intended, so it is general in negation because the vague term when negated becomes general; but this is not a case of common word generality, but rather intending a metaphorical meaning under negation. This is probably the intent of the author of al-Hidayah, and then there is no dispute with those who permit. They said: Allah said, 'Do you not see that to Allah prostrates whoever is in the heavens and whoever is on the earth and the sun and the moon and the stars and the mountains and the trees and the animals and many people?' (Quran 22:18). Prostration from people is placing the forehead on the ground, and from others it is something else—compulsory submission. Both meanings are intended from the word 'yasjudu' because it is attributed to all of them. Also, Allah said, 'Indeed, Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet' (Quran 33:56). 'Yusalluna' from Allah is mercy, and from angels it is seeking forgiveness. Both meanings are intended because it is attributed to Allah and the angels. The answer to the first: Prostration is truly utmost submission; in humans it is realized by placing the forehead voluntarily, which is the utmost submission for one with a forehead who has free choice; in others, it is realized by something else, namely being subdued under His command. If compulsory submission is intended, it includes all people, so there is no specification of 'many people.' If voluntary submission is intended, it cannot occur in non-humans, so the attribution is not valid. The refutation is clear. Moreover, we can say: compulsory submission is intended, encompassing all people, and there is no specification here because 'min' could be for explanation. And Allah knows best. 'And many of the people' means all people because 'al' is for encompassing. The answer to the second: 'Salah' is placed for showing honor and esteem. This care from Allah is realized as mercy, and from others as supplication for him. It is also a type of showing honor. It was placed for the mentioned care, giving priority to commonality of meaning over verbal commonality. Exegetes assume a missing predicate for the first clause, i.e., 'Allah sends blessings and His angels send blessings,' similar to the saying: 'We are pleased with what we have and you are pleased with what you have, but opinions differ,' meaning 'We are pleased with what we have'—so the predicate is omitted.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute regarding whether a common word (مشترك) can be used for all its meanings simultaneously. The author refutes the claim of those who affirm such generality as a reality (حقيقة). He argues that a common word is placed for each meaning individually, and usage requires intending one specific meaning. Using it for multiple meanings at once violates its placement and would be an error, neither حقيقة nor مجاز. He rebuts several objections, including arguments from Sharh al-Sharh and Sharh al-Mukhtasar. An exception is noted from Ibn al-Humam, who permits generality in negation (النفي), but the author clarifies this is not true generality of a common word but a metaphorical usage. The opponents cite Quranic verses (22:18 and 33:56) to argue that words like 'yasjudu' and 'yusalluna' are used for different meanings in the same context. The author responds that these words have a single core meaning (utmost submission/shown honor) that encompasses both, so they are not genuinely common. The passage follows the Hanafi position that a common word cannot be used for all meanings simultaneously, except in negation as a metaphorical extension, and that the Quranic examples are better understood through a unified meaning.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that has multiple meanings, each placed for it equally.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Use of a word in its original placed meaning.
مجاز (majaz): Use of a word in a non-placed meaning with a contextual clue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, can a common word be used for all its meanings simultaneously?
A) Yes, absolutely.
B) No, except in negation as a metaphorical generality.
C) Yes, but only in the Quran.
D) No, never under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
A common word cannot be used for all its meanings at once; usage requires a single intended meaning, except in negation where metaphorical generality is allowed.

The Requirement of Transmission in Majāz: Relation vs. Typewise Placement
Debate on majāz: relation vs. transmission, resolved via typewise placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on the sufficiency of 'alāqah in majāz and identify the role of typewise placement (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī) in resolving the debate.

Core Idea
Figurative usage needs more than a relation — typewise placement preserves the need for transmitted practice without requiring individual attestation.

This passage tackles a foundational question in usul al-fiqh: when we use a word metaphorically, do we need to hear that specific usage from Arabs, or is it enough that a plausible connection exists? The Hanafi response is nuanced: while a relation alone might seem sufficient, deeper analysis reveals that correct composition requires a type of linguistic placement (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī). This lesson unpacks the arguments and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Determine the precise issue: whether figurative usage (majāz) requires individual transmission (samā') or whether the existence of a relation ('alāqah) suffices.
This is a debate between the Hanafi school and opponents, and also within the Hanafi school.
2
Outline the opponent's argument
The opponents argue that without transmission, a new metaphorical usage would be either qiyas in language (if based on a connecting factor) or invention (if arbitrary), both invalid.
They view qiyas in language as impermissible.
3
Examine the initial Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (al-Jawnfūrī) claims that no invention occurs because the universal placement of the word for the related meaning is known through induction; rational indication suffices for understanding.
This response dispenses with any need for transmission.
4
Analyze the refined response from al-Ḥāshiyah
The hashia criticizes al-Jawnfūrī's position as a breach of consensus, arguing that rational indication and contextual indicators satisfy understanding but not correctness of composition; audition and permission (typewise placement) are needed.
Typewise placement (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī) is the middle ground.
5
Determine the practical outcome
The debate is resolved by affirming the necessity of typewise placement: the word must be formally configured for a category of meanings, preserving the need for transmitted practice without requiring individual attestation.
This reconciles the intuitive sufficiency of relation with the reality of linguistic convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية العلاقة لصح) المجاز أينما وجدت العلاقة ولصح (نخلة لطويل غير إنسان أيضاً) للمشاركة في الطول وأب) للابن (وبالعكس لوجود السببية والمسببية (قلنا) الملازمة ممنوعة، بل يصح إذا لم يمنع مانع والتخلف لمانع لا يقدح في تمامية المقتضى فالتخلف لمانع لا يقدح في استقلال العلاقة من غير حاجة إلى السماع ( ولعل ذلك) المانع (نصهم بالمنع للبعد عن الطبع جداً) بحيث لا ينتقل إليه الذهن فتدبر، وقد يجاب بأنه لا يلزم من عدم وجوب النقل استقلال العلاقة، بل يجوز أن يكون السبب مركباً منها ومن غيرها، ولعله انتفى ههنا الغير، وتعقب عليه المصنف بأنه لا خلاف لأحد في عدم دخول الغير إنما الخلاف في كفاية العلاقة أو اعتبار النقل معها فتدبر، (و) قالوا (ثانياً) لو لم يجب النقل في استعمال المجاز (لكان) الاستعمال في غير ما وضع له (قياساً) في اللغة (إن كان لجامع مستلزم للحكم وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن لجامع كذلك كان اختراعاً وهماً أي القياس في اللغة والاختراع (باطلان) فلا بد من السماع (قلنا) لا نسلم الاختراع إذا لم يكن لجامع مستلزم للحكم و إنما يلزم الاختراع لو لم يعلم الوضع للمعنى الملابس لما وضع له بأحد الملابسات المذكورة (علماً كلياً بالاستقراء) وههنا قد علم الوضع الكلي فلا اختراع (أقول ) مطابقاً لما أجاب الجونفوري (وأيضاً إنما يلزم) الاختراع (لو لم يدل اللفظ على المعنى المجازي عقلاً) و لو لم تمنع القرينة عن إرادة (الملزوم) الموضوع له إلى اللازم المتعلق به لكن ههنا الدلالة بالعقل، والانتقال بالقرينة، وحينئذ لا ،اختراع، وعلى هذا لا يحتاج إلى النقل أصلاً لا في الجزئيات ولا في الكليات، وقد التزمه الجونفوري، ولعل هذا خرق للإجماع، قال في «الحاشية»: ولك أن تقول إن الدلالة العقلية تكفي للمفهومية، والقرينة للمرادية، لكن لا بد لصحة التركيب من أمر زائد، فلا بد من السماع والإجازة منهم للاستعمال حتى يكون جارياً على قوانينهم وهذا هو الوضع النوعي، وحينئذ لا مخلص عن القول بالوضع، وهذا كلام متين عند المنصف الحاذق .
The third article on linguistic principles: The relation suffices for majāz wherever the relation exists, and it would also be valid [to call] a tall [person] a date-palm? due to participation in length, and [calling] a father a son and vice versa due to causality and effect. We say: The necessary concomitance is denied; rather, it is valid if no preventer prevents, and failure due to a preventer does not detract from the perfection of the requisite, so failure due to a preventer does not detract from the independence of the relation without need for audition. Perhaps that preventer is their explicit prohibition due to extreme unnaturalness such that the mind does not transfer to it, so reflect. And it may be answered that from the non-necessity of transmission, the independence of the relation does not follow; rather, it is possible that the cause is composed of it and something else, and perhaps the other is absent here. The author objected that there is no disagreement about the non-inclusion of the other; rather, the disagreement is about the sufficiency of the relation or the consideration of transmission with it, so reflect. And they said, secondly: If transmission were not obligatory in the use of majāz, then the use in other than what it was placed for would be an analogy in language if there is a connecting factor requiring the ruling, otherwise it would be an imaginary invention, i.e., analogy in language and invention are invalid, so audition is necessary. We say: We do not accept invention when there is no connecting factor requiring the ruling; invention would only follow if the placement for the meaning related to what it was placed for by one of the mentioned relations was not known as a universal knowledge through induction. Here, the universal placement is known, so there is no invention. [I say: This is in accordance with the answer of al-Jawnfūrī]. Also, invention would follow only if the word did not indicate the figurative meaning rationally, even if the contextual indicator did not prevent intending the original to the necessary related to it, but here indication is by reason and transfer by contextual indicator, and then there is no invention. On this basis, there is no need for transmission at all, neither in particulars nor in universals, and al-Jawnfūrī committed to this, and perhaps this is a breach of consensus. He said in al-Ḥāshiyah: You may say that rational indication suffices for conceptual understanding, and the contextual indicator for intended meaning, but for the correctness of composition, an additional thing is needed, so audition and permission from them for usage is necessary so that it follows their laws, and this is the typewise placement. Then there is no escape from affirming placement. This is a solid statement for the impartial discerning one.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul debate on whether figurative usage (majāz) requires linguistic transmission (samā') or whether the existence of a relation ('alāqah) between the literal and figurative meanings suffices. The opponents argue that without transmission, any use based solely on relation would be either qiyas (analogy) in language or innovation, both invalid. The Hanafi response, attributed to al-Jawnfūrī, denies the necessity of innovation because the universal placement of the word for the related meaning is known through induction, so no invention occurs. A stronger response holds that rational indication suffices for understanding the metaphorical meaning without any transmission. However, the commentator from al-Ḥāshiyah objects that while rational indication and contextual indicators satisfy understanding and intended meaning, correctness of composition requires an additional element of audition and permission from native speakers—this is the typewise placement (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī). So ultimately, the debate leads to affirming the need for some form of placement, though not necessarily individual transmission for each instance.

Key Terms
السماع (samā'): Audition or linguistic transmission; the requirement that a figurative usage be attested by native speakers.
العلاقة ('alāqah): The relation or connection between the literal and figurative meanings, such as similarity or causality.
الوضع النوعي (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī): Typewise placement; the specification of a word for a category of meanings, not for an individual instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi response to the opponents' claim that using majāz without transmission would be qiyas in language or invention?
A) They accept and require transmission for each use.
B) They deny that it would be invention because universal placement is known.
C) They say it is always forbidden.
D) They say it is allowed only if no preventer exists.

Key Takeaway
Figurative usage in language requires typewise placement (al-waḍ' al-naw'ī) to ensure correctness of composition, preserving the need for transmitted practice while avoiding the burden of individual attestation.

Debate: Does Majaz Require Prior Haqiqah?
Dispute on whether metaphorical usage necessitates prior literal use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether majaz requires prior haqiqah, explain the proof using 'al-Rahman', and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
Metaphor does not require prior literal usage; meaning known suffices, existence not required.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, we explore a foundational usul dispute: does metaphorical usage (majaz) necessarily imply that the word was once used literally (haqiqah)? The Hanafi school, represented here, takes the position that majaz does not require prior literal usage. The key proof is the divine name 'al-Rahman', which is used metaphorically for Allah yet has no literal usage. We will trace the arguments, including objections from compound metaphors and the challenge of Musaylimah, and understand why the Hanafi view is considered al-asahh (most correct).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed question
The passage begins with the agreed premise that a word after coinage but before usage is neither haqiqah nor majaz. The dispute: does majaz entail (istilzam) a prior literal usage? Some say yes, the correct view (al-asahh) says no.
2
State the Hanafi position and primary proof
The Hanafi position is negation (nafi). The proof is the word 'al-Rahman': it is used only for Allah, but its literal meaning (tenderness of heart) is inapplicable, so it is a metaphor without a literal antecedent.
3
Examine the two proofs for 'al-Rahman'
First: it is only used for Allah and its literal meaning cannot be realized. Second: it is used only for a specific individual (Allah) and never for the universal. The text notes these proofs may be critiqued later.
4
Analyze the rebuttals to counterarguments
The followers of Musaylimah used 'Rahman' for him; this is rejected as linguistically invalid. Compound metaphors like 'the war stood on a leg' are outside the dispute (single words only). Additionally, for metaphor, the meaning must be known but need not exist in reality (like lies).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence affects Quranic interpretation and legal rulings. The text declares the Hanafi position al-asahh (most correct), so it is the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Issue: After agreement that a word after coinage and before usage is neither literal nor metaphorical — for they are categories of correctly used words — there is disagreement on whether metaphor entails literalness and whether the word must have been used in its literal sense at least once. Some say it does entail; the correct view is negation, so it does not entail. For us, [the word] 'al-Rahman' is metaphorical linguistically or customarily and has no literal meaning. They presented two proofs: first, it is only used for Allah and its literal meaning (tenderness of heart) cannot be realized for Him; second, it is only used for a specific individual of 'possessor of mercy', namely Allah, and was never used for the absolute universal. If you say: the followers of Musaylimah the liar used it for him, and it became known, so Abu Jahl said upon hearing 'al-Rahman' from the Messenger of Allah: 'We do not know al-Rahman except the Rahman of Yamamah' — the response is: 'The Rahman of Yamamah is rejected, for it is not in accordance with the language, but rather from their obstinacy and ignorance.' Then the two proofs are insufficient, for it has not been proven that mercy is tenderness of heart; it could be coined for favor and beneficence. For us also [the words] 'asa' and 'ni‘ma', for they are forms coined for informing but never used in that sense, only in performative speech. Also for us, indefinite nouns (mubhamat) according to one view... and as for arguing with compounds like 'the war stood on a leg' and 'the night's hair turned gray' – these are metaphors but the compounds were never used in their literal meanings – this is outside the dispute, for the dispute is about single words. And what was said that it is a shared obligation... is a false illusion, for what is required for metaphor is that the meaning be known even if it is imaginary; its actual existence is not necessary, as in lies. Hence another answer emerges: it is possible that [the word] has a literal meaning but is used by the liar and the joker and the transmitter. Understand. And what was said in 'al-Tahrir' that it is shared... then the discussion leads to that metaphor must have...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usuli dispute: whether metaphorical usage (majaz) necessarily implies that the word was previously used literally (haqiqah). The author states that the correct (asahh) position is that majaz does not require a prior literal usage. The proof includes the word 'al-Rahman', which is used exclusively for Allah in a metaphorical sense (since its literal meaning, 'tenderness of heart', does not apply to Allah), yet it has no literal usage. Similarly, the words 'asa' and 'ni‘ma' were coined for informing but never used in that function; they are always used performatively. The author also addresses counterarguments: the claim that followers of Musaylimah used 'Rahman' literally is rejected as ignorant misuse. The two proofs regarding 'Rahman' are critiqued — the literal meaning might be 'favor' not 'tenderness of heart'. The passage then discusses compound metaphors, arguing they are irrelevant since the dispute is about single words. Finally, the author explains that for metaphor, the meaning must be understood (even if fictitious), not necessarily real; thus, a word can be metaphorical even if the literal referent never existed in reality, like lies.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Linguistic usage where a word is used in a sense other than its original coinage, with a relationship (al-‘ilaqah) between the two meanings.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as per its coinage (wad‘).
استلزام (istilzam): Entailment or necessary implication; here, whether the existence of majaz necessarily implies a prior literal usage of the same word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Majaz always requires prior literal usage.
B) Majaz does not require prior literal usage.
C) The requirement depends on the specific word.
D) The issue is unresolved.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical usage does not require prior literal usage; it is sufficient that the meaning is understood.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Nature of Majaz: Substitute in Utterance or Ruling?
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn over what substitution in majaz consists of.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on majaz as substitution in utterance, and evaluate the Sahibayn's objection using the example of 'anti abni'.

Core Idea
Majaz substitutes for haqiqah in utterance alone; grammatical correctness suffices.

This passage delves into a foundational usul dispute: when we use a word metaphorically, what exactly is substituting for what? Imam Abu Hanifa argues the substitution is in the very act of speaking, while his two foremost students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) place it in the derived ruling. Understanding this distinction is critical for determining legal effects, such as whether saying 'you are my son' to an older non-relative causes emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus and core definition
The passage opens by establishing that majaz is a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah by consensus. First, note that the substitution is of the same word used in a different sense, not a different word. Then proceed to the point of disagreement.
This rejects a competing view that would cause logical problems.
2
Compare the two positions on the locus of substitution
Compare Imam Abu Hanifa's view (substitution in utterance) with the Sahibayn's view (substitution in ruling). For Abu Hanifa, the metaphorical utterance substitutes for the literal utterance; for the Sahibayn, the metaphorical ruling substitutes for the literal ruling.
The example 'anti abni' meaning emancipation is used to illustrate.
3
Apply to the case 'you are my son' to an older person
Work through the practical consequence: According to Abu Hanifa, because the utterance is grammatically correct and the literal meaning is impossible (the addressee cannot be a biological son), the word is validly used metaphorically and emancipation occurs. According to the Sahibayn, since the original ruling (sonship) is impossible, the metaphor has no sound basis and emancipation does not occur.
Both sides aim to avoid laghw (futile speech).
4
Analyze the author's tarjih and evidence
The author sides with Abu Hanifa, calling his view 'the truth' and citing the usage of Allah, the Prophet, Companions, and eloquent speakers. He argues that denying the validity of majaz when only structure and impossibility of literal are met is obstinacy.
The author also discusses the Jahiliyyah origin of the word 'ibni' for emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة بالاتفاق يعني أن اللفظ المستعمل في المعنى المجازي خلف لنفسه المستعمل في الحقيقي، لا كما قيل إن اللفظ المستعمل في المعنى المجازي خلف للفظ آخر موضوع بإزاء هذا المعنى، وإلا لزم أن يكون هذا خلفاً هذا يستقيم عليه التفريعات كما لا يخفى ويأبى عنه كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام كل الإباء. لكن اختلفوا في جهة الخلفية فهي عند الإمام أبي حنيفة في التكلم فقط أي التكلم بالمجاز خلف عن التكلم بالحقيقة، فلفظ هذا ابني مراداً به العتق خلف عن لفظه مراداً به النبوة، وإذ لا بد من إمكان الأصل لثبوت الخلف فيكفي صحة التركيب على ضابطة العربية ليصح التكلم وهو الحق بشهادة استعمال الله تعالى ورسوله صلاة الله وسلامه عليه وآله وأصحابه واستعمال البلغاء. وقالا: الخلفية في الحكم فحكم أنت ابني مراداً به العتق خلف عن حكمه مراداً به النبوة، فلا بد عندهما لصحة المجاز من إمكان الحكم المستفاد من الحقيقة. فأنت ابني مقولاً لأكبر سناً أي لمن لا يولد مثله عن مثله يوجب العتق عنده لوجود شرط المجاز وهو صحة التركيب واستحالة الحقيقة حذراً عن اللغو، لا يوجب العتق عندهما لعدم إمكان حكم الأصل وهو النبوة. فإن قلت: إن الخلفية ههنا بمعنى أنه لا يصح الحمل على المجاز ما أمكن الحمل على الحقيقة ولا يلزم منه اشتراط المجاز بإمكان الحقيقة، فالخلفية في الحكم لا توجب إمكانه بخلاف خلفية الحنث للبر، فإن الحنث إنما يكون فيما وجب فيه البر. قلت: لا شك أن المجاز لا بد له من محل صحيح لتحققه فيه يخرج به عما كان عليه من الأصلية، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، ثم هذا المحل اللفظ المذكور من حيث إنه يفيد المعنى بحسب التركيب عند الإمام وعندهما ذلك اللفظ من حيث يصح حكمه المفاد منه حين التركيب، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم. فإن قلت: سلمنا صحة التجوز لكنه يمكن على أنحاء فلم أوجب الإمام أنه يمكن حمله على الشفقة أجاب بقوله وتقديم العتق على الشفقة لأنه أي العتق لازم للبنوة لا يتخلف فالحمل عليه أولى بخلاف الشفقة ولهذا لا يعتق في أخي لشيوعه في الدين فيحمل عليه اللهم إلا إذا قال: أردت العتق. ثم هذا غير واف، لأن الشفقة أيضاً لازم غير متخلف عرفاً حتى يعدّونه من الأحوال المؤكدة، وأما العتق فإنما يعرف لزومه للبنوة من له نوع من التمييز، فينبغي أن يحمل على الشفقة ولا أقل من أن يحال على النية، ولا يحكم بالعتق لا قضاء ولا ديانة. هذا والحق عند هذا العبد في الجواب أن هذه كلمة تبن في الجاهلية ويقصدون به الإعتاق من حين الملك ويريدون به ثبوت الميراث، مثل ميراث الابن حتى صار الإعتاق لازماً عرفياً بحيث يفهم من هذا اللفظ لا غير صار صريحاً في العتق. ولما نسخ الشرع التبني سقط حكم الميراث وبقي حكم الإعتاق، ثم العلاقة بين الحقيقي والمجازي ههنا اللزوم، فالحرية من حين الملك من لوازم البنوة، فأطلق الملزوم وأريد به اللازم على سبيل إرسال المجاز، وقيل: استعارة للمشابهة الظاهرة بين الابن والحر من حين الملك، وما قيل إنه لا تصح الاستعارة ههنا لأن المشبه مذكور، ومن شرط الاستعارة أن لا يذكر المشبه.
Majaz is a substitute for haqiqah by consensus, meaning that the word used in the metaphorical sense is a substitute for itself used in the literal sense, not as it is said that the word used in the metaphorical sense is a substitute for another word coined for that meaning, otherwise it would lead to [problem] as is clear, and the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam firmly reject it. But they differed in the aspect of substitution: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is in the utterance only, i.e., speaking the majaz is a substitute for speaking the haqiqah, so the word 'this is my son' meaning emancipation is a substitute for the word meaning biological sonship. Since the possibility of the original is necessary for the existence of the substitute, correctness of the syntactic structure according to Arabic rules suffices to make the utterance correct, and this is the truth as evidenced by the usage of Allah, His Messenger, his Companions, and eloquent speakers. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: the substitution is in the ruling, so the ruling of 'you are my son' meaning emancipation is a substitute for the ruling of it meaning biological sonship. Thus, according to them, correctness of majaz requires the possibility of the ruling derived from the haqiqah. So 'you are my son' said to an older person (i.e., one who cannot be a son by age) causes emancipation according to him [Abu Hanifa] because the condition of majaz is present: correct structure and impossibility of the literal, avoiding futility, but not according to them because the ruling of the original (sonship) is impossible. If you say: substitution here means that it is not valid to interpret as majaz as long as interpretation as haqiqah is possible, and this does not necessitate conditioning majaz on the possibility of haqiqah; substitution in ruling does not require its possibility, unlike the substitution of breaking an oath for fulfilling it. I say: there is no doubt that majaz must have a valid locus for its realization by which it departs from the original; denying this is obstinacy. This locus according to the Imam is the mentioned word in terms of its conveying meaning according to syntax; according to them, it is the word in terms of the validity of its ruling derived from it at the time of composition. This is how it should be understood. If you say: we grant the validity of metaphorical usage, but it can be in various ways; why did the Imam deem it possible to interpret it as compassion? He answered: giving precedence to emancipation over compassion because emancipation is a necessary concomitant of sonship that does not fail, so it is more appropriate to interpret it as such, unlike compassion. Hence, he does not cause emancipation in the case of 'brother' because it is common in religion, so it is interpreted accordingly, unless he says: I intended emancipation. But this is insufficient because compassion is also a necessary concomitant customarily, to the point that they consider it among confirmed states. As for emancipation, only those with some discernment know its necessity from sonship. So it should be interpreted as compassion, or at least referred to intention, and emancipation should not be ruled neither judicially nor conscientiously. The truth according to this humble servant in response is that this word was used in Jahiliyyah for emancipation from the time of ownership, intending to establish inheritance like a son, so emancipation became customarily necessary such that it is understood from this word alone, making it explicit for emancipation. When Shariah abrogated adoption, the ruling of inheritance fell, and the ruling of emancipation remained. Then the relationship between literal and metaphorical here is necessity; freedom from the time of ownership is among the necessities of sonship, so the necessary was omitted and the necessary concomitant was intended by way of majaz mursal. Or it is said: it is a metaphor for the apparent similarity between the son and the freeman from the time of ownership. And what is said that metaphor is not valid here because the likened is mentioned, whereas the condition of metaphor is that the likened should not be mentioned.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute over the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) as a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah (literal usage). First, the author clarifies that the substitute is the same word used in a different sense, not a different word. The core dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa considers the substitution to be in the utterance (takallum) itself—the act of speaking the metaphor is a substitute for speaking the literal word—while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) consider it to be in the ruling (hukm) derived from the word. This leads to a practical difference: if someone says 'you are my son' to an older person (impossible for biological sonship), it causes emancipation according to Abu Hanifa because the utterance is structurally correct and the literal is impossible (to avoid meaningless speech); but according to the Sahibayn, since the original ruling (sonship) is impossible, the metaphor lacks a sound basis. The author defends Abu Hanifa, citing usage of Allah, the Prophet, and experts. He then discusses the interpretation of 'anti abni' in Jahiliyyah context, where it became explicit for emancipation, and the legal effect after the abrogation of adoption. The relationship is identified as lazum (necessity), with freedom being a necessary concomitant of sonship, used in majaz mursal (by dropping the mushabbah). The passage emphasizes that the condition for majaz is only grammatical correctness and impossibility of literal meaning, not the feasibility of the original ruling.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Using a word in a non-literal sense that is substituted for its literal use due to a relation (علاقة).
خلف (khalaf): Substitute; the metaphorical usage stands in place of the literal usage of the same word.
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit; a word that clearly and directly indicates its meaning without need for context (here, 'ibni' for emancipation became explicit in Jahiliyyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for majaz according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The ruling derived from the literal meaning must be possible.
B) The utterance must be grammatically correct and the literal meaning impossible.
C) The metaphorical meaning must be more common than the literal.
D) The word must have been used metaphorically in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, majaz only requires correct syntax and impossibility of literal meaning; analogical extension of rulings depends on utterance, not the feasibility of the original ruling.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Majaz as Substitution: The Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Its Direction
Explaining the concept of majaz as substitution and the Hanafi ikhtilaf on its direction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views on the direction of substitution in majaz, and apply their respective conditions to the example of 'you are my son' to determine the ruling on emancipation.

Core Idea
Majaz is substitution for the same word; the dispute is whether the substitution is in utterance (Abu Hanifa) or in ruling (Sahibayn), affecting the conditions for validity.

This lesson examines a foundational usul principle: the nature of majaz (figurative usage) as a substitute for haqiqah (literal meaning). All Hanafis agree that majaz is a substitute for the same word used literally, not for a different word. However, they differ on the direction of substitution: is it in the utterance (التكلم) or in the ruling (الحكم)? Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for determining when a figurative phrase yields legal effects, such as emancipation. We will explore both positions, their proofs, and the practical outcome through the classic example of saying 'you are my son' to an older man.

Guided Path
1
Define majaz as a substitute
Understand the agreement that the word used figuratively is a substitute for the same word used literally, not for a different word. This is the basis for all subsequent discussion.
See first paragraph of the source: 'المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة بالاتفاق يعني أن اللفظ المستعمل في المعنى المجازي خلف لنفسه المستعمل في الحقيقي'
2
Identify the ikhtilaf on direction
Learn that the schools of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ: Abu Hanifa says the substitution is in the utterance (التكلم), while the two companions say it is in the ruling (الحكم).
The text says: 'لكن اختلفوا في جهة الخلفية فهي عند الإمام أبي حنيفة في التكلم فقط ... وقالا الخلفية في الحكم'
3
Analyze the conditions for validity
For Abu Hanifa, the conditions are grammatical correctness (صحة التركيب) and impossibility of literal meaning (استحالة الحقيقة). For the Sahibayn, additionally require the possibility of the original ruling (إمكان الحكم المستفاد من الحقيقة).
The example: 'أنت ابني' said to an older man. Literal meaning (prophethood) impossible; original ruling (prophethood) also impossible.
4
Apply to the example: 'you are my son'
See how each position leads to a different ruling. Abu Hanifa: the expression triggers emancipation because the literal meaning is impossible and grammatical. Sahibayn: it does not trigger emancipation because the original ruling (prophethood) is impossible.
The text explicitly contrasts: 'يوجب العتق عنده لوجود شرط المجاز ... لا يوجب العتق عندهما لعدم إمكان حكم الأصل'
5
Evaluate the author's tarjih
Understand why the author prefers Abu Hanifa's view, citing the usage of Allah, the Prophet, and eloquent speakers, and providing Quranic evidence (e.g., white thread for dawn).
The author states: 'وهو الحق بشهادة استعمال الله تعالى ورسوله'
