المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية مسألة (المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة بالاتفاق يعني أن اللفظ المستعمل في المعنى المجازي خلف لنفسه المستعمل في الحقيقي، لا كما قيل إن اللفظ المستعمل في المعنى المجازي خلف للفظ آخر موضوع بإزاء هذا المعنى، وإلا لزم أن يكون هذا خلفاً هذا يستقيم عليه التفريعات كما لا يخفى ويأبى عنه كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام كل الإباء (لكن) اختلفوا في جهة الخلفية فهي (عند) الإمام أبي حنيفة في التكلم فقط أي التكلم بالمجاز خلف عن التكلم بالحقيقة، فلفظ هذا ابني مراداً به العتق خلف عن لفظه مراداً به النبوة، وإذ لا بد من إمكان الأصل لثبوت الخلف فيكفي صحة التركيب على ضابطة العربية ليصح التكلم (وهو الحق بشهادة استعمال الله تعالى ورسوله صلاة الله وسلامه عليه وآله وأصحابه واستعمال البلغاء (وقالا) الخلفية في الحكم فحكم أنت ابني مراداً به العتق خلف عن حكمه مراداً به النبوة، فلا بد عندهما للصحة المجاز من إمكان الحكم المستفاد من الحقيقة فأنت ابني) مقولاً ( لأكبر سناً) أي لمن لا يولد مثله عن مثله ( يوجب العتق عنده لوجود شرط المجاز وهو صحة التركيب واستحالة الحقيقة حذراً عن اللغو (لا) يوجب العتق ( عندهما) لعدم إمكان حكم الأصل وهو النبوة، فإن قلت : إن الخلفية ههنا بمعنى أنه لا يصح الحمل على المجاز ما أمكن الحمل على الحقيقة ولا يلزم منه اشتراط المجاز بإمكان الحقيقة، فالخلفية في الحكم لا توجب إمكانه بخلاف خلفية الحنث للبر، فإن الحنث إنما يكون فيما وجب فيه البر، قلت : لا شك أن المجاز لا بد له من محل صحيح لتحققه فيه يخرج به عما كان عليه من الأصلية، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، ثم هذا المحل اللفظ المذكور من حيث إنه يفيد المعنى بحسب التركيب عند الإمام وعندهما ذلك اللفظ من حيث يصح حكمه المفاد منه حين التركيب، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم فإن قلت : سلمنا صحة التجوز لكنه يمكن على أنحاء فلم أوجب الإمام أنه يمكن حمله على الشفقة أجاب بقوله (وتقديم العتق على الشفقة لأنه) أي العتق (لازم) للبنوة (لا يتخلف فالحمل عليه أولى بخلاف الشفقة ولهذا لا يعتق في أخي لشيوعه في الدين) فيحمل عليه اللهم إلا إذا قال: أردت العتق ثم هذا غير واف، لأن الشفقة أيضاً لازم غير متخلف عرفاً حتى يعدّونه من الأحوال المؤكدة، وأما العتق فإنما يعرف لزومه للبنوة من له نوع من التمييز، فينبغي أن يحمل على الشفقة ولا أقل من أن يحال على النية، ولا يحكم بالعتق لا قضاء ولا ديانة هذا والحق عند هذا العبد في الجواب أن هذا كلمة تبن في الجاهلية ويقصدون به الإعتاق من حين الملك ويريدون به ثبوت الميراث، مثل ميراث الابن حتى صار الإعتاق لازماً عرفياً بحيث يفهم من هذا اللفظ لا غير صار صريحاً في هو العتق ولما نسخ الشرع التبني سقط حكم الميراث وبقي حكم الإعتاق، ثم العلاقة بين الحقيقي والمجازي ههنا اللزوم فالحرية من حين الملك من لوازم البنوة، فأطلق الملزوم وأريد به اللازم على سبيل إرسال المجاز، وقيل : استعارة للمشابهة الظاهرة بين الابن والحر من حين الملك، وما قيل إنه لا تصح الاستعارة ههنا لأن المشبه مذكور، ومن شرط الاستعارة العتق مع عدم ذكره نسياً منسياً، فهو من قبيل: زيد أسد، وهو تشبيه كما نص عليه علماء البيان، ولا إعتاق في التشبيه فإنه لا يعتق في هذا مثل الحر ففاسد لا لما في التلويح أن المشبه هو الحر المطلق، والمذكور ههنا الخاص، لأن هذا النحو من الذكر أيضاً لم يجوّزه علماء البيان، حتى حكموا بأن نحو زيد أسد تشبيه، حتى حمل صاحب الكشاف قوله تعالى: ﴿صم بكم عمى [البقرة: ۱۸] [البقرة : ۱۷۱] على التشبيه وقالوا ذكر المشبه في الكلام بحيث ينبيء عن التشبيه ممنوع في الاستعارة، وأبعد من هذا ما في التوضيح أن الممنوع إنما هو إذا كان المشبه مبتدأ والخبر جامداً، وههنا الابن مشتق فيصح الاستعارة، كما في الحال ناطقة ولا يخفى ما فيه، بل لأن هذا القول مما ادعته علماء البيان ولم يصححوه ببرهان أصلاً، فلا اعتبار لقولهم بل الظاهر من الاستعمال خلافه، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا حَتَّى يَتَبَيَّنَ لَكُمُ الْخَيْطُ الْأَبْيَضُ مِنَ الْخَيْطِ الْأَسْوَدِ مِنَ الْفَجْرِ ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۷] فإن المشبه الذي هو الفجر مذكور على نحو ينبيء عن التشبيه، مع أنه أريد من الخيط الأبيض الفجر مجازاً، وإلا لم يصح البيان به، وكذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاشْتَعَلَ الرَّأْسُ شَيْبًا ﴾ [مريم : ٤] فإن الشيب هو المراد باشتعال الرأس وإلا لم يقع مميزاً، وفي قول الشاعر: أسد علي وفي الحروب نعامة يسمع فأريد من الأسد المجترىء وإلا لما تعلق الظرف به وأمثال هذا كثيرة، وبالجملة صح الاشتراط في الاستعارة لعدم ذكر المشبه مما لم يؤيده استقراء ولا شاهد عليه أصلاً فلا قولهم، هذا ولك أن تقول : سلمنا أن الاستعارة مشروطة بذلك، وأن نحو زيد أسد تشبيه، فليس هذا التشبيه بأن تكون الأداة مقدرة كيف وحينئذ يكون كلاماً غير فصيح ولم يكن تشبيهاً بليغاً، بل المعنى أن المتكلم قصد منه التشبيه الكامل وقوة المشابهة في وجه الشبه، فادعي أن زيداً عين الأسد على طريق الإسناد المجازي، فهذا ابني إن كان تشبيهاً بليغاً، فيكون معناه أن مشابهته للابن في التعلق من حين الملك بلغت إلى أن صار عين الابن، وفي هذا الاعتاق لازم قطعاً، وليس مثل هذا مثل ابني فإنه لم يدع فيه كونه من أفراد الابن حتى يلزم العتق فافهم فإنه سانح عزيز لنا أن الانتقال إلى المجازي من المعنى) الحقيقي فإنه فهم من اللفظ ودل القرينة على أنه غير مراد انتقل إلى المجازي (وهو) أي الانتقال من المعنى الحقيقي ( يعتمد صحة الكلام من حيث العربية، إذ بصحة العربية يفهم ما وضع له في تلك اللغة، فينتقل منه إلى ملابساته (لا) صحة (الحكم ألا ترى أنه يفهم من اللفظ عند الإطلاق المحال كما لا يخفى على ذي كياسة فإذن لا فرعية إلا من جهة التكلم وهذا أولى مما هو المشهور أن المجاز تغيير في اللفظ من حال إلى حال فتكون الفرعية من جهة اللفظ، لا من جهة الحكم فإنه يرد عليه أنه مسلم أن التغيير فيه في اللفظ، لكن لا يلزم أن التغيير فيه في اللفظ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن الفرعية من جهة التكلم بل غاية ما لزم أن اللفظ من حيث إنه متغير فرع لنفسه من حيث إنه متغير عنه، وأما جهة الخلفية فلم يعلم أنه أي شيء ولم يتعين بعد، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن التجوز لما كان تغيير اللفظ من معنى إلى آخر فلا ينتظر في هذا التغيير إلا إلى صحة الإفادة، وذا بصحة
The third treatise on linguistic principles. Issue: (Majaz is a substitute for the real meaning by agreement, meaning that the word used in the metaphorical sense is a substitute for itself used in the real sense, not as it is said that the word used in the metaphorical sense is a substitute for another word placed for that meaning, otherwise it would necessitate that this is a substitute... This is valid for the branchings as is not hidden, and the statement of Imam Fakhr al-Islam absolutely rejects it. (But) they differed regarding the direction of substitution: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is only in the utterance, i.e., the utterance of the majaz is a substitute for the utterance of the haqiqah. So the word 'this son of mine' meaning emancipation is a substitute for his word meaning prophethood. And since the possibility of the original is necessary for the establishment of the substitute, the validity of the grammatical construction according to Arabic rules is sufficient for the utterance to be valid. (And it is the truth by the testimony of the usage of Allah Most High and His Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions, and the usage of eloquent speakers. (And they two said) the substitution is in the ruling, so the ruling of 'you are my son' meaning emancipation is a substitute for its ruling meaning prophethood. Thus, according to them, for the validity of majaz, the possibility of the ruling derived from the haqiqah is required. So 'you are my son' said to an older person, i.e., to one whose like cannot be born from his like, necessitates emancipation according to him due to the existence of the condition of majaz, which is the validity of construction and the impossibility of the real meaning, to avoid futility. (It does not) necessitate emancipation (according to them) due to the impossibility of the original ruling, which is prophethood. For if you say: Substitution here means that it is not valid to apply the majaz as long as application to the real meaning is possible, and this does not entail that majaz is conditioned upon the possibility of the real meaning, so substitution in the ruling does not necessitate its possibility, unlike substitution of breaking an oath for fulfilling it, for breaking an oath only occurs in what the oath required. I say: There is no doubt that majaz must have a valid locus for its realization in it, by which it emerges from its original state. Denying this is stubbornness. Then this locus is the aforementioned word in terms of its conveying meaning according to construction according to Imam, and according to them it is that word in terms of the validity of its ruling derived from it at the time of construction. This is how it should be understood. For if you say: We grant the validity of figurative usage, but it can be in various ways, so why did Imam consider it possible to be interpreted as compassion? He answered with his statement: (And precedence of emancipation over compassion because) emancipation (is a necessary concomitant) of sonship (that does not lag, so interpreting it as such is more appropriate, unlike compassion. And for this reason, it does not necessitate emancipation in 'my brother' due to its commonness in religion), so it is interpreted accordingly, unless he says: I meant emancipation. But this is not sufficient, because compassion is also a necessary concomitant customarily, to the extent that they count it among emphatic states. As for emancipation, only those with some discernment know its necessity from sonship. So it should be interpreted as compassion, or at least deferred to intention, and emancipation is not judged neither judicially nor inwardly. This, and the truth according to this servant in the answer is that this word was used in the Jahiliyyah for adoption, and they intended by it emancipation from the moment of ownership and they intended by it the establishment of inheritance, like the inheritance of a son, until emancipation became customary such that it is understood from this word only, becoming explicit for emancipation. When the Law abrogated adoption, the ruling of inheritance fell and the ruling of emancipation remained. Then the relationship between the real and metaphorical here is necessitation, so freedom from the moment of ownership is among the necessary concomitants of sonship. So the necessitating thing was uttered and the necessary concomitant was intended, by way of freeing the metaphor. And it is said: It is a metaphor for the apparent similarity between the son and the free person from the moment of ownership. And what is said that metaphor is not valid here because the compared thing is mentioned, and one of the conditions of metaphor is that the compared thing is not mentioned, forgotten, so it is of the type 'Zayd is a lion', which is a simile as stated by the scholars of rhetoric, and there is no emancipation in simile, for he would not be emancipated in this like a free person, so it is invalid. Not for what is in al-Talwih that the compared thing is the absolute free person, and what is mentioned here is specific, because this kind of mention also was not permitted by the scholars of rhetoric, to the extent that they ruled that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, and the author of al-Kashshaf interpreted the verse 'Deaf, dumb, blind' [2:18] as a simile, and they said: Mentioning the compared thing in speech in a way that indicates simile is prohibited in metaphor. And more far-fetched than this is what is in al-Tawdih that the prohibition is only when the compared thing is the subject and the predicate is a noun, and here 'son' is derived, so metaphor is valid, as in 'the state is speaking'. And its deficiency is not hidden. Rather, because this statement is something claimed by the scholars of rhetoric and they did not substantiate it with proof at all, so their statement is not considered. Rather, the apparent from usage is contrary to it, as in the verse 'And eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn' [2:187], for the compared thing which is dawn is mentioned in a way that indicates simile, yet the intended meaning from 'white thread' is dawn metaphorically, otherwise the clarification would not be valid. Similarly in the verse 'And the head has become white with hoary hair' [19:4], for the hoary hair is what is intended by the kindling of the head, otherwise it would not be a distinguisher. And in the poet's saying: 'A lion you are in battles, an ostrich in hearing', so the intended meaning from 'lion' is the brave one, otherwise the adverb would not attach to it. And many examples like this. In short, the condition of metaphor being that the compared thing is not mentioned is not supported by induction nor witnessed at all, so their statement is invalid. This, and you may say: We grant that metaphor is conditioned by that, and that 'Zayd is a lion' is a simile, but this simile is not with the tool being estimated; how then would it be non-eloquent speech and not a concise simile? Rather, the meaning is that the speaker intended complete simile and the strength of similarity in the point of similarity, so it is claimed that Zayd is the very lion by way of metaphorical attribution. So 'this is my son' if it is a concise simile, then its meaning is that his similarity to a son in attachment from the moment of ownership has reached the point that he became the very son, and in this emancipation is necessarily required. And this is not like 'my son', for in that it is not claimed that he is an individual of sonship such that emancipation is required. So understand, for it is a fortunate occurrence for us that (the transition from the real meaning to the metaphorical) is understood from the word and the context indicates that it is not intended, so one transitions to the metaphorical. And it (i.e., the transition from the real meaning) depends on the validity of speech from an Arabic perspective, for by Arabic validity one understands what it is placed for in that language, then one transitions to its associated meanings, (not) validity of the ruling. Do you not see that from the word upon absolute usage the impossible is understood, as is not hidden from one of intelligence? So there is no derivative relationship except from the perspective of utterance. And this is more appropriate than the well-known view that majaz is a change of the word from one state to another, making the derivative relationship from the perspective of the word, not from the perspective of the ruling. For it may be objected that it is granted that the change is in the word, but it does not necessitate that the derivative relationship is from the perspective of the utterance; rather, the most that is necessitated is that the word in terms of being changed is a branch of itself in terms of being changed from. As for the direction of substitution, it is not known what it is and it is not determined afterwards. And it can be answered that since figurative usage is a change of a word from one meaning to another, nothing is expected in this change except the validity of conveying meaning, which is through validity of

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the usul principle that majaz (figurative usage) is a substitute (خلف) for haqiqah (real meaning). The author explains that the word used figuratively is a substitute for that same word used literally, not for a different word. The main ikhtilaf discussed is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the direction of this substitution (جهة الخلفية). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the substitution is in the utterance (التكلم) only: the act of speaking the word figuratively is a substitute for speaking it literally. Hence, for a majaz to be valid, it is sufficient that the Arabic construction be grammatically correct (صحة التركيب) and that the literal meaning be impossible (استحالة الحقيقة). The example given is the phrase 'أنت ابني' (you are my son) said to an older person, meaning emancipation. Since the literal meaning (prophethood/nobility) is impossible, the phrase is valid as a majaz and necessitates emancipation according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn, however, hold that the substitution is in the ruling (الحكم): the ruling derived from the figurative usage is a substitute for the ruling derived from the literal meaning. Therefore, they require that the ruling of the original (hukm al-asl) be possible; if not, the majaz is invalid. In the same example, since the literal ruling (prophethood) is impossible for an older person, they do not necessitate emancipation. The text then defends Abu Hanifa's position, arguing that the condition for majaz is merely grammatical correctness, and that the usage of the Quran, the Prophet, and eloquent speakers supports this view. The author also engages with objections and explores the nature of the relationship between the literal and figurative meanings, such as necessary concomitance (اللزوم) and metaphor (الاستعارة). A lengthy discussion ensues about whether the phrase is a metaphor or simile, and whether the condition for metaphor (that the compared thing not be mentioned) is valid. The author ultimately sides with the view that the substitution is in utterance, not ruling, and that the well-known definition of majaz as a change in the word supports this. The passage is dense but systematically defends the Hanafi position on majaz and its conditions.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majāz): Figurative usage; a word used in a meaning other than its original meaning with a contextual clue, and it is a substitute for the same word used literally.
الخلفية (al-khilāfiyyah): The quality of being a substitute; the relationship of substitution between the figurative and literal usage.
التكلم (al-takallum): The act of speaking; utterance.
الحكم (al-ḥukm): The legal ruling or effect derived from the word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the condition for the validity of a majaz expression like 'أنت ابني' said to an older person?
A) The literal meaning must be possible.
B) The phrase must be grammatically correct and the literal meaning impossible.
C) The speaker must explicitly intend the literal meaning.
D) The listener must understand the figurative meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ikhtilaf on the direction of substitution in majaz determines the conditions for its validity: Abu Hanifa focuses on utterance, the Sahibayn on the ruling, leading to different outcomes in cases like 'you are my son'.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Distinguishing Iqrar from Insha': The Case of 'You Are My Son'
Debate over the classification of a statement as iqrar or insha' in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that distinguishes iqrar from insha' to the statement 'أنت ابني' and evaluate the proof from coercion in determining its legal nature.

Core Idea
The statement 'أنت ابني' to a slave is iqrar of pre-existing freedom, not insha' of emancipation.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, classifying a speech act as either a declaration (iqrar) or a creation (insha') carries significant legal consequences. The statement 'أنت ابني' addressed to a slave is a classic dispute: does it acknowledge pre-existing freedom or create new emancipation? This lesson examines the arguments, including Imam Muhammad's proof from coercion, to understand how Hanafi scholars resolve such linguistic ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State what each position claims about the statement: iqrar (acknowledgment of pre-existing freedom) vs insha' (direct creation of emancipation).
The iqrar view means the slave is freed only if the master actually freed him before; the insha' view means the statement itself frees him.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof
Find the proof from Imam Muhammad's al-Ikrāh: coercion invalidates iqrar but not insha'. The invalidity under coercion shows the statement must be iqrar.
The proof is an athar (statement) from Imam Muhammad, cited in al-Mabsūt.
3
Analyze the proof's logic
Explain why this reasoning works: if the statement were insha', it would be valid under coercion; since it is not, it must be iqrar.
This step requires understanding the usul principle that coercion blocks iqrar but allows insha'.
4
Examine counterarguments
Consider the opposing view that priority should be given to the intended purpose (freedom), and the Hanafi response that preserving literal wording from redundancy is more important.
The disputants are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, who argue for insha' based on the ruling being the purpose.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under iqrar, the mother becomes umm walad only if the slave was actually freed before; under insha', she does not.
This shows how the classification directly affects legal outcomes.

التركيب على القانون العربي، وهو لا يتوقف على صحة الحكم في نفسه فإنه مما لا دخل له في الإفادة فتدبر (ثم قيل) أنت ابني (إقرار) للحرية من وقت الملك فعلى هذا يعتق قضاء وأما ديانة فإن كان تحقق منه الإعتاق فيعتق وإلا لا فتصير أمه أم ولد له ، أقول : وفيه ما فيه) لأنه وإن كان إقراراً، لكنه إقرار بالحرية لا بالبنوة، والمستلزم لأمومية الأم هو الثاني لا الأول، إلاّ أن يقال إنه كان يقصد من هذه العبارة التبني وثبوت جميع أحكام الأبناء من العتق من حين الملك وأمومية الأم والميراث حتى صار عرفاً فيه إلا أن الشرع لما نسخ التبني والميراث بقي العتق فيه، وفي أمه حق العتق كما كان فتأمل فيه (وقيل) ليس بإقرار (بل إنشاء) للإعتاق بمنزلة أنت حرّ من حين الملك، وعلى هذا يعتق قضاء وديانة فلا تصير أمه أم ولد له (وفي التحرير الأول) أي كونه إقراراً أصح لقوله أي قول الإمام محمد (في) كتاب (الإكراه) من المبسوط إذا أكره) رجل (على) قول هذا ابني لعبده لا يعتق عليه والإكراه إنما (يمنع صحة الإقرار بالعتق لا انشاءه) فعلم أنه إقرار (أقول) ليس عدم العتق فيه لعدم صحة الإقرار حتى لو كان إنشاء يعتق بل لأن المجازي يتوقف على النية لأن اللفظ للحقيقة فهي الأسبق إلا بنية انصرافه عنها والإكراه محل فتور الإرادة والقصد فلا يثبت هناك إلا ما جعل اللفظ فقط علة تامة له) لا ما يثبت بالنية، فهذا لتوقفه على النية لا يصح إقراراً كان أو إنشاء حال الإكراه، وهذا الكلام غير موجه فإنه ليس يلزم توقف على معنى مجازي على النية، بل المتوقف عليها هي الكناية، سواء كانت حقيقة أو مجازاً، وجعل الألفاظ الصريحة علة تامة، سواء كانت حقائق أو مجازات، وأنت ابني من الصريح صرح به صدر الشريعة وغيره فإن استحالة الحقيقي صرف اللفظ إلى المجازي صرفاً ظاهراً لا خفاء فيه، وكذا صيرورته عرفاً، فلو كان إنشاء لم يتوقف على النية وصح في الإكراه، فعدم صحته حال الإكراه دل على كونه إقراراً فتدبر (ولهما أن الحكم هو المقصود من اللفظ (فالخلفية باعتباره أولى والجواب أن هذا ممنوع، كيف ولا : ملاءمة للمقصودية باعتبارها فيما يتعلق بالدلالة، بل الخلفية ههنا في التركيب على قانون اللغة (أقول) لا نسلم أن الخلفية بالنظر إلى الحكم أولى بل الصون) أي صون اللفظ (عن (اللغو) الذي يلزم على الخلفية في الحكم (أولى) لأن الكلام للإفادة لا للإلغاء فتأمل، فإن لهما أن يقولا نعم الصون أولى لكن مهما ، أمكن وههنا غير ممكن لانتفاء شرط المجاز، لكن الأمر غير خفي على المتأمل (وأما قولهما في الاستدلال (لغي قطعت يدك إذا أخرجهما صحيحين ولم يجعل هذا الكلام (مجازاً) عن الإقرار بالمال) والتركيب صحيح والقطع سبب لوجوب المال، فعلم أن إمكان الحقيقي شرط وقد انتفى ففيه أن القطع ليس سبباً للمال مطلقاً بل إذا قطع خطأ يجب المال على وجه مخصوص فالعلاقة قاصرة لا تكفي للانتقال عرفاً لا لعدم صحة حكم الحقيقة، وحاصل الجواب أنه لا يلزم من صحة التركيب صحة المجاز، إذ لا يلزم من تحقق شرط واحد تحقق المشروط لاحتمال فقدان شرط آخر، ولعل هذا عدم تحقق العلاقة المصححة فإنه ليس القطع سبباً للمال المطلق: «بل للمال المخصوص الذي لا يصح وجوبه»، ولا ينتقل الذهن من القطع إلى المال المطلق أصلاً، فلا يرد أن البنوة ليست سبباً أيضاً للإعتاق مطلقاً، بل عند وجودها كالقطع فإنه سبب عند وجوده خطأ ومفض إلى المال، وجه الدفع ظاهر ، ثم نقول : هما لا يشترطان إلا إمكان الحقيقي عقلاً في ظاهر الأمر لا وقوعه في نفس الأمر ، وههنا القطع ممكن عقلاً»، وإن لم يقع فلا دخل لفقدان المعنى في عدم صحة المجاز، فإن قلت : قد اتفقا على انعقاد النكاح بلفظ الهبة في أن المعنى الحقيقي لا يصح لأنها لا توهب أجاب بقوله (وأما اتفاقهما على انعقاد النكاح بالهبة في الحرة ولا يتصور) المعنى الحقيقي فلأنهما لم يشترطاه إلا) إمكانه (عقلاً) ألا ترى أنهما قالا فيما إذا قال : أنت ابني للأصغر المعروف النسب يعتق (وهو) أي الحقيقي (ممكن عقلاً كيف لا وقد وقع التملك للحر (في شريعة يعقوب عليه السلام) أي في الشريعة الخليلية التي كان يعقوب يعمل بها، وإذا التملك صح الهبة عقلاً (و) قد وقع أيضاً (في أول الإسلام) ثم نسخ (كذا قيل).
The third article on linguistic principles. Composition is according to Arabic rules; it does not depend on the validity of the ruling itself, for that has no part in conveying meaning, so reflect. (Then it is said): 'You are my son' is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of freedom from the time of ownership; thus he is freed judicially (qada'), but privately (diyanatan), if his emancipation (i'taq) is verified then he is freed, otherwise not; so his mother becomes an umm walad for him. I say: There is much in it. For although it is an iqrar, it is an iqrar of freedom, not of sonship; and that which implies the mother being an umm walad is the second (sonship), not the first (freedom). Unless it is said that he intended by this expression adoption (tabannī) and the establishment of all rulings of children: emancipation from the time of ownership, mother being umm walad, and inheritance, until it became an 'urf in that; but when the Law abrogated adoption and inheritance, emancipation remained in it, and the mother's right to emancipation remained as before, so ponder on it. (And it is said): It is not an iqrar, but rather a construction (inshā') of emancipation, like 'you are free from the time of ownership'; on this basis he is freed both judicially and privately, and his mother does not become an umm walad for him. (In al-Tahrīr al-Awwal), i.e., its being an iqrar is more correct, because of the statement of Imam Muhammad in the book al-Ikrāh from al-Mabsūt: If a man is coerced into saying 'this is my son' to his slave, he is not emancipated; coercion only prevents the validity of iqrar of emancipation, not its inshā'. So it is known that it is an iqrar. I say: The lack of emancipation is not due to the invalidity of the iqrar, such that if it were inshā' he would be emancipated; rather, because the metaphorical (majāzī) depends on intention, since the literal meaning is primary unless there is an intention to turn away from it, and coercion is a place of weakness of will and intention; so only that is established which makes the mere wording a complete cause, not that which is established by intention. Thus this, because it depends on intention, is not valid whether it is iqrar or inshā' during coercion. This statement is not directed, for it is not necessary that a metaphorical meaning depends on intention; rather, what depends on intention is kināyah (allusion), whether literal or metaphorical. And making explicit expressions a complete cause applies whether they are literal or metaphorical. 'You are my son' is explicit, as stated by Sadr al-Sharī'ah and others; for the impossibility of the literal meaning directs the word to the metaphorical meaning in a clear manner, and likewise when it becomes customary. So if it were inshā', it would not depend on intention and would be valid under coercion; so its invalidity under coercion indicates it is an iqrar, so reflect. (And for them two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The ruling is the intended purpose of the wording, so consideration of priority is based on it. The answer: This is denied; how can it be that there is suitability for purpose with respect to indication? Rather priority here is in composition according to linguistic rules. I say: We do not accept that priority with respect to the ruling is better; rather, preserving the wording from redundancy that would result from priority in the ruling is better, because speech is for conveying meaning, not for abolition. So reflect; for them two could say: yes, preservation is better, but only when possible, and here it is not possible because the condition for metaphor is absent, but the matter is not hidden from the contemplator. (As for their argument in evidence): 'I cut your hand' when he releases them both soundly, and they do not make this speech a metaphor for acknowledgment of property; the composition is sound and cutting is a cause for obligation of property; so it is known that the possibility of the literal meaning is a condition and it is absent. The answer: Cutting is not a cause for property absolutely, but rather if cutting occurs by mistake, property is obligatory in a specific manner; the relationship is deficient, not sufficient for transfer customarily, not because the ruling of the literal meaning is invalid. The summary of the answer: The validity of composition does not imply the validity of metaphor, for the fulfillment of one condition does not imply the fulfillment of the conditioned due to the possibility of the absence of another condition. Perhaps the absence of the correct relationship is that cutting is not a cause for absolute property, but for specific property whose obligation is not valid; the mind does not transfer from cutting to absolute property at all. So it is not objected that sonship is also not a cause for emancipation absolutely, but rather when it exists, like cutting is a cause when it exists by mistake and leads to property; the answer is clear. Then we say: They both do not require except the possibility of the literal meaning rationally in outward appearance, not its actual occurrence; and here cutting is rationally possible, even if it did not occur; so the absence of the meaning does not affect the validity of the metaphor. If you say: They both agreed on the validity of marriage by the word 'hiba' (gift) regarding a free woman, and the literal meaning is not possible because she is not given as a gift. He answered: (As for their agreement on the validity of marriage by hiba for a free woman, the literal meaning is not possible; they required only rational possibility). Do you not see that they said: In the case where one says 'you are my son' to a younger known lineage, he is freed, and the literal meaning is rationally possible; how could it not be, since ownership of a free person occurred in the law of Jacob (peace be upon him), i.e., in the Patriarchal law that Jacob followed. And if ownership is valid, hiba is rationally possible. And it also occurred in early Islam, then abrogated, as is said.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion (usul al-fiqh dispute) about the legal nature of the statement 'أنت ابني' (You are my son) when addressed to a slave. The commentator (the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut) is expanding on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. The core question: Is this statement an iqrar (declaration) of pre-existing freedom or an insha' (creation) of emancipation? The first view (iqrar) means that the speaker is acknowledging that the slave was already free from the time he owned him; consequently, the slave is freed only judicially (qada'), but privately (diyanatan) only if the speaker had actually freed him before. If not, the slave is not privately free, and the mother becomes an umm walad (mother of the master's child) only if the speaker's statement is considered an iqrar of sonship. The second view (insha') treats the statement as a direct creation of freedom analogous to saying 'You are free from the time I owned you.' The author of the sharh mentions that the view that it is iqrar is more correct (أصح) based on Imam Muhammad's proof in al-Ikrāh: under coercion, the statement does not free the slave. Coercion invalidates iqrar but not insha', so the fact that it is invalid shows it is iqrar. However, the commentator (the author of Fawatih) critiques this reasoning, arguing that the statement is explicit (ṣarīḥ) and would not depend on intention if it were insha'. He insists that the invalidity under coercion actually proves it is iqrar. The argument continues with responses from the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the commentator's further rebuttals. The Hanafi position ultimately favors the iqrar view, which affects the mother's status. The passage illustrates the Hanafi method of combining literal analysis, intention, and customary usage ('urf) to determine legal effects.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A declaration or acknowledgment of a pre-existing right or status, not a creation of a new legal effect.
إنشاء (insha'): A speech act that creates a new legal status or effect, e.g., 'you are free' creates emancipation.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; a word used in a meaning other than its original linguistic coinage, due to a relationship ('alaqa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, is the statement 'أنت ابني' to a slave considered iqrar or insha'?
A) Iqrar
B) Insha'
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the iqrar view for the statement 'أنت ابني', based on the effect of coercion, preserving the literal wording, and the requirement of intention for metaphorical usage.

The Rule Against Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: Bequests and Aman
Usul dispute: applying the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule against combining haqiqah and majaz to analyze whether secondary relatives (mawali al-mawali, grandchildren) are included in a bequest or aman when the testator uses a plural term but has only one primary relative.

Core Idea
You cannot intend both literal and figurative meanings from one word without context.

This passage shows how a core usul principle—the prohibition on combining haqiqah and majaz—governs the interpretation of wills and safe-conduct grants. When a testator says 'to my mawali' but has only one mawla, does the word include the mawla's own mawali? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no—that would require simultaneously using 'mawali' in its literal sense (the one you have) and a figurative sense (their relatives), which is impermissible. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that the context of knowing only one exists triggers a general figurative meaning (umum al-majaz) that naturally includes secondary relatives. The passage then tests this rule against a case in aman where grandchildren are included under 'sons' due to necessity, and explores whether grandparents are similarly included under 'fathers'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Usul Principle
The rule is: you cannot intend both the literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings from a single word without a clear contextual indicator. In this passage, the word 'mawali' is literal for one's direct clients/freedmen; including their clients would be figurative. Combining both in one utterance is what Abu Hanifa forbids.
This is a settled Hanafi rule, not a universal one.
2
Analyze the Bequest Scenario
A testator says 'to my mawali' but only has one mawla. Abu Hanifa: since the plural form implies at least two, and only one exists, that one mawla takes half the bequest (his share from the implied minimum of two) and the rest goes to heirs. The mawali al-mawali are excluded because including them would require figurative extension. The Sahibayn: because the testator knew he had only one, he must have intended a general meaning (umum al-majaz) that includes the mawali of that mawla. Thus they enter.
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: a different distribution.
3
Evaluate the Aman Exception
A musta'min says 'grant security to my sons' but included grandsons? Literally 'sons' does not include grandsons, yet grandsons are included. This seems to violate the rule. The Hanafi response: the inclusion is not because 'sons' was used figuratively for grandsons, but due to precaution in blood (shubha). Common usage like 'Bani Hashim' creates a semblance (shubha) of literalness, and aman is established by semblance due to the gravity of bloodshed.
This is an exception driven by necessity, not a general license to combine.
4
Assess the Grandparent Dispute
If someone says 'grant security to my fathers and mothers', do grandparents enter? Two narrations from Abu Hanifa: (1) yes, by textual implication (dalalat al-nass)—a person would not prefer his own parents over his grandparents; (2) no, because they are not literally 'fathers' and they are original roots, not followers. The preferred view (ashbah) is that they enter, supported by the linguistic fact that 'mother' means origin.
This shows the application of textual implication (dalalat al-nass) as a tool.

Branch: The ruling is specific to the mawali in the bequest if he says: 'I bequeath to my mawali of so-and-so' (not to their mawali), i.e., the mawali of the mawali are not included because the mawla to whom one is related is literal for the one who is related by self, whereas the mawali of mawali are not related to him literally, so the mawali are intended literally and not the mawali of mawali, otherwise combination would follow. Except if the mawali are one, then he takes half and the rest goes to the heirs according to him (Abu Hanifa), because he bequeathed to a group of mawali, and the minimum of a group is two, so each gets half of the bequest. When the mawla is one, he gets half and the rest is inheritance. The minimum is two because two and above is a group in bequests as in inheritance, because both are caliphates after death regarding property. The author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: There is no apparent reason for the minimum of a group in bequests being two, and analogy to inheritance is invalid, for it does not follow that using a word figuratively in one context necessitates using it in a similar context for that meaning. Yes, if supported by usage, there is a reason. Similarly, sons (abna') and grandchildren (hafada) according to him (Abu Hanifa): if he bequeaths to the sons of so-and-so, his own sons enter but not his sons' sons, except if the son is one, then he takes half and the rest to heirs, etc. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the mawali of mawali and grandchildren enter when with a single mawla or a single son, due to umum al-majaz, because when he utters a plural form while knowing there is no mawla or son except one, it is known that he intended a more general meaning that includes the mawali of the mawla and grandchildren as well. But not with two, by agreement, because there is no context indicating figurative intention. Then this ruling is contested first by the entry of the protegés (hafada) of a musta'min along with his sons in the aman if he says 'grant me security for my sons', for then combination follows, because 'ibn' (son) is literal for son and figurative for grandson. The answer: He did not intend the grandson with the word 'ibn', but precaution in sparing blood necessitated inclusion in the aman due to the presence of a semblance of literalness through common usage like 'Bani Hashim did such-and-such'. And aman is established by semblance because blood matters are not easy. The inclusion of grandfathers and grandmothers under fathers and mothers if he says 'grant me security for my fathers and mothers' is disputed. In one narration: they enter, which is evident. In another narration: they do not. Its justification: the entry of grandchildren was by way of following (taba'), and the entry of grandfathers and grandmothers if it were by following, they are original by creation, so they do not enter by following. This justification is weak, because originality in creation does not negate following in other rulings. Moreover, in al-Hidaya: the mother is linguistically an origin, so then the entry is by self, not by following. Therefore the more similar is the first narration, although the second is apparent in the riwaya. Then there is another explanation that would be easier: it is apparent that a man does not prefer the life of himself and his sons over the sons of his sons, so they enter by textual implication. But it is also apparent that grandfathers and grandmothers also enter by implication, unless they are corrupt and have opinions, so it is known that the Imam would not grant security to such people, so they are excluded from aman. Perhaps this is shared between them and grandchildren. And that...

Commentary
This branch (far') applies the usul principle against combining haqiqah and majaz to practical cases in bequests (wasiyya) and grants of security (aman). The central issue is: when a testator uses a plural word like 'mawali' or 'abna'' while having only one such relative, does the word include secondary relations (e.g., mawali of mawali, grandchildren)? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the literal meaning is taken, so only the direct relatives are included; if only one exists, he takes half the bequest (since plural implies at least two) and the rest goes to heirs. This avoids combining literal and figurative meanings. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, argue that in such a context (knowing only one exists), the plural is intended figuratively in a general sense (umum al-majaz) to include secondary relations. The text then discusses a counterexample from aman: if a musta'min says 'grant security to my sons', does it include grandsons? The Hanafi response is that while the literal word 'sons' does not include grandsons, precaution in blood matters (ihktiyat fi hagn al-dam) dictates their inclusion due to a semblance of literalness based on common usage. This is an exception driven by necessity, not a violation of the rule. The passage further debates whether grandparents enter under 'fathers and mothers' in aman, with two narrations from Abu Hanifa. The preferred view (ashbah) is that they enter, supported by the linguistic fact that 'mother' means origin. The teacher should emphasize that the general rule against combining haqiqah and majaz is maintained, but practical considerations like precaution in blood matters can create exceptions through semblance (shubha) or textual implication (dalalat al-nass).

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): A master or patron, or a freed slave; in this context, a relative by clientage or kinship
حفدة (hafada): Grandchildren, specifically the sons of one's sons
أمان (aman): Safe conduct or protection granted to a non-Muslim enemy
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): A general figurative meaning that includes the literal meaning as one of its individuals
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam‘ bayna al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): The prohibited act of intending both literal and figurative meanings from one word without context
شبهة (shubha): Semblance or doubt; a factor that can establish a legal ruling in matters of necessity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone bequeaths 'to my mawali' and he has only one mawla, what is the ruling?
A) The mawla takes the entire bequest.
B) The mawla takes half and the rest is inheritance.
C) The mawla and his mawali take the entire bequest.
D) The bequest is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The rule against combining haqiqah and majaz prevents extending a literal word to include related parties unless a clear contextual indicator or necessity (like shubha in aman) overrides.

The Fifth Objection: Intending Both Vow and Oath – Hanafi Resolution
A usul dispute analyzing the fifth objection and various Hanafi responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth objection regarding combining vow and oath in one utterance, analyze the Hanafi responses, and explain the final relied-upon position that distinguishes two types of vows.

Core Idea
The Hanafi resolution lies in recognizing a hybrid vow type that inherently includes the force of an oath.

This lesson examines the fifth objection in the debate over whether one can intend both a vow (nadhr) and an oath (yamin) in a single statement like 'upon me is fasting such-and-such'. The objection claims that such an utterance would require combining literal and metaphorical usage in one word, a violation of usul principles. We will walk through several Hanafi responses—from Shams al-A'imma, Sadr al-Shari'a, and a later shaykh—and see how the late shaykh's categorization of vows resolves the issue without falling into the alleged contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Recognize the core claim: if someone intends both nadhr and yamin, the utterance must carry both literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) meanings, which is problematic.
The passage states this is 'the fifth objection' against the Hanafi view that such an intention is valid.
2
Trace the Early Responses
Examine the answers by earlier scholars: (1) the oath is intended via necessary consequence (lazim) not the word; (2) Shams al-A'imma uses two words: 'by Allah' for oath and 'upon me' for vow.
Note that each response is criticized by the text's author.
3
Analyze the Critiques
Understand why each early response fails: the first leads to combination anyway, the second is not general (requires mentioning 'Allah'), and Sadr al-Shari'a's nullification theory is inconsistent.
The critiques expose the difficulty of avoiding combined usage.
4
Discover the Late Shaykh's Resolution
Learn the final classification: vows are of two types—pure obligation and obligation with kaffara. The latter is inherently both a vow and an oath, so intending both is simply intending this hybrid type.
This avoids needing to combine literal and metaphorical in one word.
5
Apply the Resolution
Conclude that the practical effect (thamarah al-khilaf) is that qada' and kaffara both become wajib, but no usul violation occurs.
The resolution preserves the Hanafi position without theoretical contradiction.

لو لم يشترط توسط اللفظ لكان تصور اليمين وإرادته يميناً، ولو كان مثل شراء القريب للزم بدون النية أيضاً، بل مع نفيه فتدبر أحسن التدبر (أقول) في تقرير الجواب عن أصل الإيراد (لا أن يقال : الفهم لا يقتضي الإرادة والاستعمال وصيغة النذر يفهم منها التحريم اللازم له من غير استعمالها فيه وإرادته منها (فعقد القلب بعد فهم اللازم وهو التحريم (من اللفظ) جعل يميناً، فلا يلزم الاستعمال في اليمين لأنه تصرف في غير وافٍ أيضاً، لأن التصرف لم يعتبر في الشرع إلا بما يفهم من حاق اللفظ حقيقة أو مجازاً، كيف لا ولو كان الأمر كذلك لكان التحريم المستفاد من التلبية الإحرامية بعد عقد القلب يميناً، وكذا التحريم المستفاد من تحريمة الصلاة يكون بعد عقد القلب يميناً وهكذا من المفاسد (وقال) الإمام (شمس الأئمة : أريد اليمين بقوله : الله والنذر بعلي فلا جمع) بل لفظان استعملا في معنييهما ولا يخفى ما فيه أما أولاً فلأنه لا يطرد فيما إذا لم يقل كلمة الله، بل علي صوم كذا، أو أوجبت على نفسي مراضاة الله وما أشبهه مع أن الحكم عام هذا، وأما ثانياً : فلان اللام للقسم لم يجيء إلا في مقام التعجب نحو قول ابن عباس دخل آدم الجنة وقت العصر، فلله ما غربت الشمس حتى خرج، وأما في غير هذا فالنحاة يمنعونه، ويمكن فيه المناقشة بأن التجوز لا يشترط فيه سماع الجزئيات فكيف سماع موارد الاستعمالات، وهل هذا إلا تهافت فحينئذ له أن يقول : لا حجة في حسبان علماء النحو، فإن بعد تسليم العلاقة وصحة الانتقال لا وجه للمنع، نعم : لو كان بعيداً بحيث لا ينتقل إليه الذهن لكان للمنع وجه كما في الأب والابن فتأمل فيه، وأما ثالثاً : فلما في الحاشية» بأنه على هذا يجب أن يكون يميناً عند نفيه، لأنه إذا قال مثلاً : هي طالق وأراد أن لا يكون طلاقاً فهي باطلة، وكذا ههنا، لأن اللام موجود، وهذا ليس بشيء، لأن دلالة اللام على القسم بالنية لا يستلزم دلالتها مطلقاً يكون صريحاً كالطلاق، بل مدعاه أن للام معنى مجازياً غير شائع يصح بالنية فافهم فإنه دقيق، وأجاب صدر الشريعة بأن إرادة النذر بطلت في صورة الإرادتين وبقي إرادة اليمين ولزم، كما إذا أراد اليمين وسكت عن النذر والنذر إنما يثبت بنفس الصيغة، وهذا مما يقتضي العجب مثله، أما أولاً فلأنه يلزم عليه أن يثبت مع كل معنى مجازي معنى حقيقي، أما المجازي : فبالنية والحقيقي بنفس الصيغة وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لم لا تبطل إرادة اليمين مع كونه معنى مجازياً، بل هو أولى بالبطلان من الحقيقي وأما ثالثاً : فلأن الحقيقي إنما يثبت في الصريح إذا لم يثبت منه معنى آخر وههنا قد ثبت المعنى المجازي فافهم ويمكن أن يقال : قصد اليمين على سبيل الكناية، فكأن الناذر أراد النذر لينتقل منه إلى اليمين فلا النحو جمعاً يبطل الكناية، وفيه أنه على هذا يلزم أن لا يصح النذر فإن المعنى الحقيقي في الكناية غير مقصود وليس مناط صدق وكذب وعقد وفسخ هكذا وقع القيل والقال، وههنا لبعض المشايخ من المتأخرين الذي بلغ مبلغ السابقين كلام هو أن النذر نوعان نذر بإيجاب شيء فقط، ونذر بإيجابه، بحيث لو لم يؤد المنذور لكان عليه كفارة اليمين فهذا القسم الأخير يلزم فيه أداء المنذور، فإن أدى فيها وإلا وجب عليه القضاء للمنذور والكفارة لنقض العهد المؤكد بإيجاب الكفارة، وهذا القسم قد يسمى يميناً لوجود موجب اليمين فيه أيضاً، فالمراد في المسألة من إرادة النذر واليمين إرادة هذا النحو من النذر ، لأنه نذر من وجه ويمين من وجه، ومن إرادة اليمين مع السكوت إرادة أن عليه الكفارة ولم ينو استقلالاً أن هذا واجب أصلاً؛ وصورة الجمع ليس إلا ما نوى فيه اليمين مع نفي النذر، يعني عن عليه، فحينئذ لا جمع أصلاً، وإن سمي هذا
And the fifth objection: that 'upon me is fasting such-and-such with the intention of an oath' whether with it is the intention of a vow or not, it is both a vow and an oath, so qada' is obligatory indicating it is a vow, and kaffara upon violation indicating it is an oath, contrary to Abu Yusuf who says the literal is vow and the metaphorical is oath. And it was answered that prohibition of what is permissible is necessary for the vow because making something obligatory necessitates its opposite being prohibited, so the oath is intended by the necessary consequence of the meaning of the word not by the word itself, and the vow is intended by the word so there is no use of the word in both, so no combination. But there is a consideration: intending the oath from the necessary consequence is dependent on intending the necessary consequence, otherwise the specific (i.e., the oath) would occur without the general (i.e., absolute prohibition). And if the necessary consequence is intended and the vow is intended by the word, then combination of literal and metaphorical necessarily occurs. I say: Also intending the oath by the necessary consequence does not negate the metaphorical nature of the object (i.e., the vow used for this intended oath), because the word is only for the object (i.e., the vow) by agreement, so intending the oath from it is intending a metaphorical meaning. Yes: if mere conceptualization of prohibition were sufficient for intending the oath without the mediation of the word, so the word would not then be used in the oath, or it would be like buying a relative necessitating emancipation, then it would be established without intending from the word the establishment of consequences from the establishment of the object. The answer would be complete, but the matter is not so, for if mediation of the word were not required, then conceptualizing the oath and intending it would be an oath, and if it were like buying a relative, it would necessarily occur without intention, even with its negation. So ponder well. I say in clarifying the answer to the original objection: It is not to be said that understanding does not necessitate intention and usage, and the wording of the vow is understood from it the prohibition necessary to it without using it in that and intending it from it. So after understanding the necessary (i.e., prohibition) from the word, the heart's intention makes it an oath, so usage in the oath does not occur because it is a disposition in the necessary meaning, nor does lack of mediation of the word occur, for the meaning obtained by disposition in which the oath is understood from the word by implication even if the word is not used in it. Rather, the oath, after joining the intention, becomes necessary for the vow like the emancipation of a relative. Understand. But this is also inadequate, because disposition is not considered in the law except by what is understood from the literal wording, whether literally or metaphorically. How not? If the matter were so, then the prohibition derived from the talbiya of ihram after the heart's intention would be an oath, and similarly the prohibition derived from the takbir of prayer would be an oath after the heart's intention, and other corruptions. And Imam Shams al-A'imma said: The oath is intended by his saying 'by Allah' and the vow by 'upon me', so there is no combination; rather, two words are used in their two meanings. Its deficiency is not hidden. First, it is not consistent where one does not say the word 'Allah', but rather 'upon me is such fasting' or 'I have obligated upon myself to please Allah' and the like, while the ruling is general. Second, the lam for oath only comes in exclamatory contexts like Ibn Abbas's statement: 'Adam entered Paradise at 'Asr, and by Allah, the sun had not set until he came out.' In other than this, grammarians forbid it. One can argue that figurative usage does not require hearing particular instances, so why require hearing instances of usage? Is this not contradiction? Then he can say: There is no proof in the opinion of grammarians, for after accepting the relationship and correctness of transition, there is no ground for prohibition. Yes, if it were far to the extent that the mind would not transition to it, then prohibition would have a basis, as in 'al-ab' and 'al-ibn'. So ponder. Third, as in the margin: on this, it should be an oath when he denies it, because if he says 'she is divorced' and intends that it not be divorce, it is invalid. Similarly here, because the lam is present. But this is nothing, because the lam's indication of oath by intention does not entail its indication absolutely to be explicit like divorce. Rather, his claim is that the lam has an uncommon metaphorical meaning that is valid with intention, so understand for it is subtle. And Sadr al-Shari'a answered that the intention of the vow is nullified in the case of two intentions, and the intention of the oath remains and is binding, as when one intends the oath and is silent about the vow, and the vow is only established by the wording itself. This is astonishing. First, it implies that with every metaphorical meaning a literal meaning is established: the metaphorical by intention, the literal by the wording itself. Second, why is not the intention of the oath voided while being a metaphorical meaning, indeed it is more worthy of voiding than the literal? Third, the literal is only established in the explicit if no other meaning is established from it, and here the metaphorical meaning has been established. So understand. And it can be said: intent of the oath is by way of metonymy (kinaya), as if the vow maker intended the vow to transition from it to the oath, so no combination is needed, and the metonymy is not invalidated. But this would entail that the vow is not valid, for the literal meaning in metonymy is not intended and is not a criterion for truth/falsehood, contract, or dissolution. Thus the discourse occurred. And here, for some late shaykhs who attained the rank of the early scholars, there is a statement: Vows are of two types: a vow obligating something only, and a vow obligating something such that if the vowed thing is not performed, kaffara of an oath is due. In the latter type, performing the vowed thing is required; if performed it suffices, otherwise qada' of the vowed thing and kaffara for breaking the covenant emphasized by the obligation of kaffara. This type may be called an oath because the cause of the oath exists in it. So the intended meaning in the issue of intending both vow and oath is intending this type of vow, for it is a vow in one respect and an oath in another. And intending the oath with silence intends that kaffara is due upon him and he did not independently intend that this is obligatory originally. The form of combination is only what he intended the oath with negation of the vow, meaning from him [؟]. Then there is no combination at all, even if this is called [؟]
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a legal dispute (usul dispute) within Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the fifth objection (خامساً) that arises from the case where someone says 'upon me is fasting such-and-such' while intending an oath (يمين). The objection is: if the person intends both a vow (نذر) and an oath simultaneously, then the ruling is mixed: qada' (making up the fast) is required because it is a vow, and kaffara (expiation) is required upon violation because it is an oath. Abu Yusuf held that the literal meaning is vow and the metaphorical is oath. The response attempts to avoid combining literal and metaphorical usage in one word by arguing that the oath is intended through the necessary consequence (لازم) of the word (i.e., the prohibition of what is permissible) rather than directly through the word itself. However, this response is criticized because intending the oath from the necessary consequence still requires intending the necessary consequence, and if the vow is also intended from the word, then combination of literal (حقيقة) and metaphorical (مجاز) occurs. Further criticisms are raised: if mere conceptualization of the necessary consequence sufficed, then many cases (e.g., talbiya in ihram, takbir in prayer) would become oaths, which is absurd. Shams al-A'imma proposed that the word 'Allah' indicates the oath and 'upon me' indicates the vow, thus two separate words, but this is criticized because it fails when 'Allah' is not mentioned. Grammatical issues with the lam of oath are discussed. Sadr al-Shari'a argued that the intention of the vow is nullified and only the oath remains, but this is criticized for inconsistency. A later shaykh resolved the issue by distinguishing two types of vows: one that only obligates an act, and another that obligates an act with kaffara for non-performance. The latter is both a vow and an oath by nature, so when one intends both, it is simply this hybrid type, and no combination of literal and metaphorical in one word occurs. This resolution is presented as the final relied-upon position.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow obligating an act of worship or charity, which may be of two types: one merely obligates the act, the other also imposes kaffara for non-performance.
يمين (yamin): An oath by Allah, which prohibits the opposite of the vowed act and entails kaffara upon violation.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning: the primary, original meaning of a word.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning: a secondary, figurative meaning of a word based on a relationship (علاقة).
اللازم (al-lazim): The necessary consequence; here, the prohibition of what is permissible that follows from obligating a fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the final relied-upon resolution to the fifth objection?
A) The vow intention is nullified and only the oath remains.
B) The word 'Allah' indicates the oath and 'upon me' indicates the vow, so two words are used.
C) Vows are of two types; the intended hybrid type inherently carries both vow and oath characteristics, avoiding combination of meanings in one word.
D) The utterance is invalid because it combines literal and metaphorical meanings.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the objection of combining vow and oath in one utterance by recognizing a hybrid vow type that inherently includes the force of an oath.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Dispute on Intending Both Oath and Vow in One Phrase
Analyzing whether intending both oath and vow constitutes combination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on combining oath and vow intentions in a single expression and evaluate the arguments of Shams al-A'imma and Sadr al-Shariah.

Core Idea
When both oath and vow are intended, later Hanafis nullify the vow and keep the oath, avoiding forbidden combination.

Consider a person who says 'Allah upon me to fast' while intending both an oath (yamin) and a vow (nadhr). Does this single phrase create two separate obligations? Or does one intention cancel the other? This passage examines a classical Hanafi dispute on whether the expression can bear both meanings simultaneously, rooted in the principles of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) usage. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school balances wording and intention in legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its two potential effects
Recognize that the utterance 'Allah upon me' can function as an oath (requiring expiation if broken) and a vow (requiring fulfillment). The dispute centers on whether both can be intended at once.
The key is the linguistic structure: 'Allah' typically signals an oath, while 'upon me' signals a vow.
2
Understand Shams al-A'imma's position: no combination
Shams al-A'imma argues that each word retains its literal meaning, so the expression is two separate utterances: an oath by 'Allah' and a vow by 'upon me'. No combination occurs.
His position avoids absurdities like deriving oaths from talbiyah or takbir.
3
Analyze the criticisms against Shams al-A'imma's view
Critics point out that his view fails when 'Allah' is omitted, that grammarians restrict the lam for oath to exceptional contexts, and that it leads to inconsistent legal consequences.
The rebuttal uses the principle that figurative language (majaz) does not require prior hearing of every instance.
4
Examine Sadr al-Shariah's counter: nullify the vow intention
Sadr al-Shariah argues that when both intentions are present, the literal meaning (vow) stands by the wording itself, while the metaphorical meaning (oath) requires intention. The vow intention is nullified, leaving only the oath binding.
This is analogous to a case where only the oath is intended; the wording alone would not create a vow.
5
Consider the later scholar's distinction between vow types
A later scholar differentiates between a simple vow and a vow that includes expiation (yamin-type nadhr). In the latter, intending the oath effect does not create a true combination because both aspects belong to a single category.
This view relies on the general command to fulfill vows (Qur'an 22:29) and the idea that both are covenants.
6
Determine the tarjih and practical consequence
The text favors the later Hanafi position: no forbidden combination occurs. The practical effect is that only the oath obligation (expiation) applies; the vow is not independently required.
The dispute illustrates how usul al-fiqh principles guide the resolution of ambiguous expressions.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية حتى فيه بل صار) اليمين (بعد انضمام النية، مثل عتق القريب) لازماً للنذر (فافهم) وهذا غير وافٍ أيضاً، لأن التصرف لم يعتبر في الشرع إلا بما يفهم من حاق اللفظ حقيقة أو مجازاً، كيف لا ولو كان الأمر كذلك لكان التحريم المستفاد من التلبية الإحرامية بعد عقد القلب يميناً، وكذا التحريم المستفاد من تحريمة الصلاة يكون بعد عقد القلب يميناً وهكذا من المفاسد (وقال) الإمام (شمس الأئمة : أريد اليمين بقوله : الله والنذر بعلي فلا جمع) بل لفظان استعملا في معنييهما ولا يخفى ما فيه أما أولاً فلأنه لا يطرد فيما إذا لم يقل كلمة الله، بل علي صوم كذا، أو أوجبت على نفسي مراضاة الله وما أشبهه مع أن الحكم عام هذا، وأما ثانياً : فلان اللام للقسم لم يجيء إلا في مقام التعجب نحو قول ابن عباس دخل آدم الجنة وقت العصر، فلله ما غربت الشمس حتى خرج، وأما في غير هذا فالنحاة يمنعونه، ويمكن فيه المناقشة بأن التجوز لا يشترط فيه سماع الجزئيات فكيف سماع موارد الاستعمالات، وهل هذا إلا تهافت فحينئذ له أن يقول : لا حجة في حسبان علماء النحو، فإن بعد تسليم العلاقة وصحة الانتقال لا وجه للمنع، نعم : لو كان بعيداً بحيث لا ينتقل إليه الذهن لكان للمنع وجه كما في الأب والابن فتأمل فيه، وأما ثالثاً : فلما في الحاشية» بأنه على هذا يجب أن يكون يميناً عند نفيه، لأنه إذا قال مثلاً : هي طالق وأراد أن لا يكون طلاقاً فهي باطلة، وكذا ههنا، لأن اللام موجود، وهذا ليس بشيء، لأن دلالة اللام على القسم بالنية لا يستلزم دلالتها مطلقاً يكون صريحاً كالطلاق، بل مدعاه أن للام معنى مجازياً غير شائع يصح بالنية فافهم فإنه دقيق، وأجاب صدر الشريعة بأن إرادة النذر بطلت في صورة الإرادتين وبقي إرادة اليمين ولزم، كما إذا أراد اليمين وسكت عن النذر والنذر إنما يثبت بنفس الصيغة، وهذا مما يقتضي العجب مثله، أما أولاً فلأنه يلزم عليه أن يثبت مع كل معنى مجازي معنى حقيقي، أما المجازي : فبالنية والحقيقي بنفس الصيغة وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لم لا تبطل إرادة اليمين مع كونه معنى مجازياً، بل هو أولى بالبطلان من الحقيقي وأما ثالثاً : فلأن الحقيقي إنما يثبت في الصريح إذا لم يثبت منه معنى آخر وههنا قد ثبت المعنى المجازي فافهم ويمكن أن يقال : قصد اليمين على سبيل الكناية، فكأن الناذر أراد النذر لينتقل منه إلى اليمين فلا النحو جمعاً يبطل الكناية، وفيه أنه على هذا يلزم أن لا يصح النذر فإن المعنى الحقيقي في الكناية غير مقصود وليس مناط صدق وكذب وعقد وفسخ هكذا وقع القيل والقال، وههنا لبعض المشايخ من المتأخرين الذي بلغ مبلغ السابقين كلام هو أن النذر نوعان نذر بإيجاب شيء فقط، ونذر بإيجابه، بحيث لو لم يؤد المنذور لكان عليه كفارة اليمين فهذا القسم الأخير يلزم فيه أداء المنذور، فإن أدى فيها وإلا وجب عليه القضاء للمنذور والكفارة لنقض العهد المؤكد بإيجاب الكفارة، وهذا القسم قد يسمى يميناً لوجود موجب اليمين فيه أيضاً، فالمراد في المسألة من إرادة النذر واليمين إرادة هذا النحو من النذر ، لأنه نذر من وجه ويمين من وجه، ومن إرادة اليمين مع السكوت إرادة أن عليه الكفارة ولم ينو استقلالاً أن هذا واجب أصلاً؛ وصورة الجمع ليس إلا ما نوى فيه اليمين مع نفي النذر، يعني عن عليه، فحينئذ لا جمع أصلاً، وإن سمي هذا إرادة اليمين من صيغة النذر ،مجازاً، فهذه النية نفي للنذر، سواء نفي صراحة أم لا، هذا خلاصة كلامه رحمه الله تعالى فإن قلت هذا إبداء تصرف جديد لا بد عليه من حجة من الشارع قلت ليس هذا إبداء تصرف من عند نفسه بل مأذون فيه، لأن النذر أيضاً عهد كاليمين، حتى لو نذر بالمحرّم يجب كفارة يمين لعدم إمكان الوفاء فيه، فإذا أراد تأكد العهد بإلزام الكفارة عند عدم الوفاء على نفسه فليس فيه بعد بل نذر في نذر داخل في عموم وَلْيُوفُواْ نُذُورَهُمْ ﴾ [الحج : ۲۹] هذا غاية التتميم لكلامه
The third section on linguistic principles. Until: 'but it became) the oath (after joining the intention, like the emancipation of a relative) binding for the vow (so understand). And this is also insufficient, because an action is not considered in the Sharia except by what is understood from the bare wording, literally or figuratively. How not? If it were otherwise, then the prohibition derived from the talbiyah of ihram after the heart's intention would be an oath, and similarly the prohibition derived from the takbir of prayer would be an oath after the heart's intention, and so on from corruptions. (And) the Imam (Shams al-A'imma) (said: The oath is intended by his saying: 'By Allah' and the vow by 'upon me', so there is no combination) rather two words used in their two meanings. And what is in it is not hidden. Firstly, because it does not apply when he does not say the word 'Allah', but rather 'upon me such-and-such fasting', or 'I obligated upon myself for the pleasure of Allah' and the like, while the ruling is general. Secondly, because the lam for oath only occurs in a situation of amazement, like the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'Adam entered Paradise at the time of Asr, so by Allah, the sun had not set until he left'. Other than that, the grammarians forbid it. And it can be debated that figurative usage does not require hearing of particulars, so how about hearing of cases of usage? Is this not contradiction? Then he can say: There is no proof in the reckoning of grammarians, because after conceding the relationship and correctness of transition, there is no ground for prohibition. Yes, if it were so remote that the mind does not transition to it, there would be ground for prohibition, as in 'al-ab' and 'al-ibn', so reflect. Thirdly, because of what is in the gloss: that on this basis it should be an oath when negating it, because if he says, for example, 'You are divorced' and intends that it not be divorce, it is invalid, and likewise here, because the lam exists. And this is nothing, because the indication of the lam for oath by intention does not entail its indication absolutely as explicit as divorce. Rather, his claim is that the lam has a metaphorical meaning that is not prevalent, which becomes valid by intention, so understand, for it is subtle. And Sadr al-Shariah responded: The intention of the vow is nullified in the case of two intentions, and the intention of the oath remains and becomes binding, just as if he intended the oath and remained silent about the vow, while the vow is established by the wording itself. And this requires wonder like it. Firstly, because it necessitates that with every metaphorical meaning, a literal meaning be established: as for the metaphorical, by intention, and the literal, by the wording itself. Secondly, why does not the intention of the oath become nullified while it is a metaphorical meaning? Rather, it is more deserving of nullification than the literal. Thirdly, because the literal is only established in explicit wording if no other meaning is established from it, but here the metaphorical meaning has been established, so understand. And it could be said: The intention of the oath is by way of allusion (kinayah), as if the vow-maker intended the vow to transition from it to the oath, so the combination does not nullify the allusion. And in it, that on this basis it necessitates that the vow be invalid, because the literal meaning in allusion is not intended and is not a criterion of truth/falsity, contract/annulment. Thus discussion occurred. And here from some later scholars who reached the level of the predecessors is a saying: Vows are of two types: a vow that only obligates something, and a vow that obligates it such that if the vowed thing is not performed, then upon him is the expiation of an oath. In this latter type, performing the vowed thing is obligatory, and if he performs it, fine, otherwise he must make up the vowed thing and pay expiation for breaking the covenant affirmed by making expiation obligatory. This type is sometimes called an oath because the cause of an oath exists in it as well. So what is intended in the issue of intending a vow and an oath is intending this type of vow, because it is a vow from one aspect and an oath from another. And intending the oath while remaining silent is intending that upon him is expiation, and he did not independently intend that this is obligatory originally. And the form of combination is only what he intended the oath with negation of the vow, meaning from him, thus there is no combination at all. And if this is called intending the oath from the wording of the vow metaphorically, then this intention is negation of the vow, whether he negates explicitly or not. This is the summary of his words, may Allah have mercy on him. And if you say: This is an innovation of a new action that requires a proof from the Lawgiver; I say: This is not an innovation of action from himself, but it is permitted, because the vow is also a covenant like the oath, such that if one vows something forbidden, the expiation of an oath is obligatory due to inability to fulfill. So if he intends strengthening the covenant by making expiation obligatory upon non-fulfillment, there is no harm in it; rather, it is a vow within a vow included in the generality of 'and let them fulfill their vows' (Qur'an 22:29). This is the utmost completion of his words.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the interpretation of a phrase like 'Allah upon me' where one might intend both an oath (yamin) and a vow (nadhr). Shams al-A'imma argued that the two words are used in their separate literal meanings—'Allah' for oath and 'upon me' for vow—so there is no combination. However, critics pointed out problems: it fails when 'Allah' is omitted, the lam for oath is restricted by grammarians, and the legal consequences are inconsistent. Sadr al-Shariah countered that intending both voids the vow intention, leaving only the oath, due to the principle that the literal meaning is established by the wording itself while the metaphorical requires intention. A later scholar distinguished between two types of vows, suggesting that intending the oath-with-expiation type is not really a combination but a single intention. The Hanafi position ultimately relies on the idea that both oath and vow are covenants, and the intention of strengthening the commitment is permissible. The dispute is about whether the expression can bear both meanings simultaneously.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah, often implying liability for expiation (kaffarah) if broken.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of obedience, which becomes obligatory upon fulfillment.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by language.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical or figurative usage of a word, requiring intention or context to be understood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi view in this passage, what happens when someone says 'Allah upon me' intending both an oath and a vow?
A) Both intentions are valid, so he must fulfill the vowed act and pay expiation if he fails.
B) The vow intention is nullified, and only the oath remains binding.
C) The oath intention is nullified, and only the vow remains binding.
D) The utterance is invalid, and nothing is required.

Key Takeaway
Later Hanafis resolve the conflict by prioritizing the metaphorical meaning (oath) via intention, considering that the literal meaning (vow) gives way.

Preference of Haqiqah over Majaz Muta'arif
Dispute on preferring literal vs customary figurative meaning in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions in the Hanafi school regarding haqiqah vs majaz muta'arif and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to oath scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prioritizes literal meaning; Sahibayn prioritize customary understanding.

When a word has both a literal meaning (haqiqah) and a customary figurative meaning (majaz muta'arif), which should be given priority in legal interpretation? This lesson examines a classic usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The disagreement has practical consequences in oaths: if someone swears 'I will not eat meat' and eats human flesh, does he violate his oath? Understanding the basis of each position will help you derive rulings in cases where custom and literal meaning conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's view (haqiqah preferred due to asalah) and the Sahibayn's view (majaz muta'arif preferred due to tabadur).
Asalah = principle of literal meaning; tabadur = immediate understanding.
2
Analyze the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa argues that haqiqah is the asl (original) and cannot be abandoned without a preventer. Sahibayn argue that customary usage causes immediate understanding, which is a strong preventer overriding the asl.
The key is whether custom (urf) constitutes a preventer (mani').
3
Apply to the oath example
For the oath 'I will not eat meat' and then eating human flesh: under Abu Hanifa, meat literally includes human flesh, so no violation; under Sahibayn, customary understanding excludes human flesh, so violation occurs.
The fatwa generally follows Sahibayn in this case.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is in the ruling on the oath: does the swearer break his oath or not? This directly affects whether expiation (kaffarah) is required.
This is a clear case where usul principle leads to different fiqh outcomes.
5
Scrutinize the tarjih
The author of the text leans towards Sahibayn, stating that tabadur and custom are strong preventers. Understand why this view is preferred in later Hanafi works.
The tarjih is not absolute; it depends on context (oaths vs. other matters).
