مسألة الحقيقة المستعملة أولى من المجاز المتعارف عنده عملاً بالأصل) فإن الحقيقة أصل فمهما أمكن لا يصح العدول عنه، في الحاشية»: هذا لا ينافي ما سيأتي أن التخصيص بالعرف عملياً كان أو قولياً يصح، وذلك لما سيأتي أن العام المخصص حقيقة عند الفقهاء، وهذا لا يتأتى منا، فإنا لا نقول بكونه حقيقة وعندهما بالعكس) أي المجاز المتعارف أولى من الحقيقة المستعملة للتبادر إلى الفهم فإن التعارف يوجب التبادر بلا ريب، ولا تعارضه الأصالة، لأن الأصالة إنما تقتضي الحمل عليه إذا لم يمنع مانع والتبادر والتعارف مانعان قويان فافهم فإنه أحق بالقبول وقيل : تساويا) فيتوقف حتى يتعين أحدهما بالدليل (أقول : ينبغي أن يكون النزاع فيما لم يكن مبناه على العرف كالأيمان لأن ما يكون مبناه على العرف يفهم منه المتعارف بالضرورة ولهذا أفتوا بعدم الحنث عنده في حلفه لا يأكل لحماً بأكل لحم آدمي إذا كان الحالف مسلماً) مع أن اللحم حقيقة يصدق على لحم الآدمي، قال «مطلع الأسرار» لا حاجة إلى التقييد بالإسلام في لحم الأدمي، فإن الكافر والمسلم سواء فيه، ونقل عن الزاهد العتابي أن الفتوى عليه وإلا ففيه رواية أخرى في المعتبرات كالهداية وغيرها أنه يحنث لكونه في الحقيقة، ثم إن ما ذكر غير ظاهر وإن كان قبل هذا القول من قبل أيضاً، لأن مسألة الفرات والحنطة أيضاً في اليمين الذي مبناه على العرف قال الإمام فخر الإسلام إن هذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في فرعية المجاز فلما كانت الفرعية عندهما باعتبار الحكم كان اعتباره أولى، ففي مسألة أكل الحنطة لما كان حكم المجاز المتعارف شاملاً لحكم الحقيقة اعتبراه ، وعنده : لما كان باعتبار التكلم اعتبر التكلم فرجح الحقيقة للأصالة، وهذا التعليل يدل على أن الخلاف في المجاز المتناول للحقيقة ثم في هذا التعليل نظر، لأنه لو تم لدل على رجحان كل مجاز متناول للحقيقة، سواء كان متعارفاً أم لا ، ثم أن اعتبار الخلفية في التكلم لا يوجب اعتبار الحقيقة وإن تشبث بالأصالة فهي كافية ولا حاجة إلى الخلفية في التكلم فالحق ما أشار إليه المصنف أن المبنى للخلاف أنه اعتبر الأصالة وهما التبادر.
Issue: The literal meaning (haqiqah) that is actually used is preferable over the customary figurative meaning (majaz muta'arif) according to him (Abu Hanifa) because of acting upon the principle (al-asl). For haqiqah is the principle, so whenever possible, departure from it is not valid. In the Hashiya: this does not contradict what will come that specification by custom ('urf), whether practical or verbal, is valid. That is because, as will come, the general that has been specified is haqiqah according to the jurists. But this does not follow from us, for we do not say that it is haqiqah. And according to them (the two companions), the opposite: i.e., the customary figurative meaning is preferable over the literal meaning that is used because of its immediacy to the mind (tabadur). For custom definitely causes immediate understanding, and the principle (asalah) does not oppose it because the principle only necessitates applying the term to its literal meaning when there is no preventer. But immediate understanding and custom are strong preventers. So understand, for it is more worthy of acceptance. And it was said: they are equal, so it is suspended until one of them is determined by evidence. I say: the dispute should be concerning that which is not based on custom, like oaths, because what is based on custom is necessarily understood by custom. That is why they gave fatwa of no violation according to him (Abu Hanifa) regarding one who swore 'I will not eat meat' and then ate human flesh, if the swearer was Muslim, even though meat literally applies to human flesh. 'Matali' al-Asrar' said: there is no need to restrict to Islam regarding human flesh, for the disbeliever and Muslim are equal in it. And it is reported from al-Zahid al-Atabi that the fatwa is upon it. Otherwise, there is another narration in Mu'tabarat like al-Hidayah and others that he does violate because it is literal. Then what is mentioned is not apparent, even if it was before this statement from before as well, because the issue of the Euphrates and wheat is also in the oath that is based on custom. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: this difference is built upon the difference regarding the derivative nature of majaz. So when the derivativity according to them (the two companions) is with regard to the ruling, they gave precedence to the ruling. In the issue of eating wheat, since the ruling of the customary majaz included the ruling of the haqiqah, they considered it. And according to him (Abu Hanifa): since it is with regard to the speaker, he considered the speaker, so he gave preference to the haqiqah due to asalah. This reasoning indicates that the difference is in majaz that encompasses haqiqah. Then in this reasoning there is a flaw: because if it were complete, it would indicate the preference of every majaz that encompasses haqiqah, whether customary or not. Then considering derivativity in the speaker does not necessitate considering haqiqah, even if he clings to asalah. Asalah is sufficient and there is no need for derivativity in the speaker. So the truth is what the author pointed out: that the basis of the difference is that he (Abu Hanifa) considered asalah, and they (the two companions) considered tabadur (immediate understanding).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage examines a core usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the priority when a word has both a literal meaning (haqiqah) and a customary figurative meaning (majaz muta'arif). Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning should be preferred because it is the original (asl). The Sahibayn argue that customary usage takes precedence because it leads to immediate understanding (tabadur) in the mind of the listener. The text then discusses a practical application: if someone swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats human flesh, Abu Hanifa would rule that he does not break his oath because meat is a haqiqah for human flesh as well, but the Sahibayn would consider the customary understanding that 'meat' refers to animal meat, not human flesh. The fatwa is given in accordance with the Sahibayn's view. The passage also connects this to a similar issue regarding oaths about Euphrates water and wheat. Ultimately, the author clarifies that the basis of the disagreement is whether one gives weight to the original literal meaning (asalah) or to immediate customary understanding (tabadur).

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as it was first coined.
المجاز المتعارف (al-majaz al-muta'arif): A figurative meaning that has become customary in common usage.
الأصالة (al-asalah): The principle that a word is to be understood in its original literal meaning unless there is evidence to the contrary.
التبادر (al-tabadur): The immediate understanding that springs to mind upon hearing a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediate understanding (tabadur)
B) The principle of asl (asalah)
C) Consensus (ijma)
D) Analogical reasoning (qiyas)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether literal or customary meaning takes precedence in oaths, with the relied-upon view favoring customary understanding.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Dispute on Haqiqah Shar'iyyah: Coinage or Popular Usage?
Analyzing whether shar'i terms are true (haqiqah) by transmission or by new coinage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions on haqiqah shar'iyyah, analyze their proofs, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to early revelation texts.

Core Idea
Shar'i terms like salat are understood without context, but is it from the Lawgiver's coinage or from widespread usage?

In usul al-fiqh, there is a foundational disagreement on the nature of shar'i terms: are they genuine haqiqah (true) coined by the Lawgiver, or are they well-known metaphors (majaz ishtahara) that gained prevalence among Muslims? This dispute affects how we interpret these terms when they first appear in revelation, before they became famous. This lesson dissects the arguments from both sides, focusing on the Hanafi engagement with this debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the disagreement is not about the effectiveness of shar'i terms, but about the basis of that effectiveness: is it due to the Lawgiver's direct coinage (haqiqah shar'iyyah) or due to widespread community usage (majaz ishtahara)?
All agree the terms convey legal meanings without context; the dispute is over the cause.
2
State the Two Positions
The majority (including most Hanafis) affirm haqiqah shar'iyyah: the Lawgiver transferred or coined terms for legal meanings. The deniers (al-Baqillani, al-Dabusi, al-Bazdawi, al-Baydawi) hold that they are majaz that became famous.
Note that some Hanafi luminaries are among the deniers, showing intra-school diversity.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proofs
Four main proofs are presented: (1) usage without qarina, (2) Companions' understanding without qarina, (3) impossibility of negating legal meaning (e.g., 'these pillars are not salat'), (4) continuity of Lawgiver's usage on legal meaning.
Each proof is contested by the deniers; the passage notes weaknesses.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The key difference appears when shar'i terms are used in early revelation (before becoming famous). Affirmers take them as legal; deniers take them as linguistic.
Example: 'salat' in early Meccan verses — does it mean ritual prayer (haqiqah) or supplication (linguistic)?
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The author sides with the majority, calling the denial 'necessarily false' in its extreme form, but acknowledges difficulties in proving the historical claim of transfer.
The Hanafi response relies on induction (istiqra) and logical necessity.

لا خلاف في أن الحقيقة الشرعية التي وضعها أهل الشرع كالفقهاء وعلماء الأصول، ولا خلاف في أن الألفاظ الشرعية لا تحتاج إلى القرينة في إفادة المعاني الشرعية، وإنما الخلاف في هذه الدلالة لأجل وضع الشارع أو أنها حقائق لغوية في المعاني الشرعية، وتارة أنها مستعملة في المعاني اللغوية، والزيادات شروط للاعتبار شرعاً، ولما كان هذا باطلاً بالضرورة للقطع بأنها مستعملة في المعاني الشرعية، وهذا الذي ذو اليد الطولى في العلوم كيف يتفوه بهذا قال «المصنف تبعاً لشارح المختصر والحق أنه لا ثالث لهذه المذاهب، ولعل القاضي لم ينص عليه وإلا فلا يصح هذا القول ففي كلام الشارع) إن وردت هذه الألفاظ الشرعية قبل الاشتهار عند عدم القرينة لشيء منهما على أيهما يحمل) فعند القائل بالحقيقة الشرعية يحمل على الشرعي وعند منكرها يحمل على اللغوي، وهذا فائدة الخلاف لنا الاستعمال بلا قرينة في الشرعي متحقق وهو أمارة الحقيقة وهذا إنما يتم لو ساعد عليه المنكر (و) لنا أيضاً (فهم الصحابة) الشرعي (كذلك) أي بلا قرينة، والفهم بدون القرينة دليل الحقيقة وهذا إنما يتم لو ثبت فهمهم قبل الاشتهار من غير قرينة وهذا في غاية الخفاء (و) لنا أيضاً لنا القطع بالاستقراء على أن الصلاة مثلاً للركعات وإذا كان مراده هذا الاستمرار على استعمالها في الركعات فاندفع ما في «التحرير» أنه لا يتم لجواز القطع) فيه (بالشهرة) بين كافة المسلمين وهجر اللغوي (أو) القطع (بوضع أهل الشرع) ثم إن الاستدلال بالاستمرار لا يغاير كثيراً للاستدلال بالاستعمال من غير قرينة وههنا أيضاً لا تخلو دعوى هذا الاستمرار عن توجه منع (والقول) في الجواب عن هذا الدليل بأنها باقية على اللغة وهو الدعاء (والزيادات) من القيام والركوع وغيرهما من الأركان شروط شرعاً لاعتبار الدعاء (مع أنها لا تعم كالزكاة) أي لا تعم مثل الزكاة فإنها لغة النماء وشرعاً التمليك المخصوص لمال مخصوص، فهناك تثبت
The Third Article on Linguistic Fundamentals. Issue 193. There is no disagreement that the shar'i haqiqah (legal true meaning) was established by the people of the Law like jurists and usul scholars, and no disagreement that shar'i terms do not need a contextual indicator (qarina) to convey their legal meanings. The disagreement is only about whether this indication is due to the Lawgiver's coinage or due to popular usage among the Muslim people of the Law. The first group chose that the shar'i haqiqah is because the Lawgiver transferred (naqala) the words from their linguistic meanings to legal meanings for a suitable reason, and that is apparent, or that the Lawgiver coined them from scratch (ibtida') on the basis of invention, and this is the occurrence according to the majority and what is mentioned in al-Minhaj and al-Mahsul according to the Mu'tazila. And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (from the Shafi'is), Qadi Abu Zayd al-Dabusi (from us), Imam Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi (from our great masters and his peers like Shams al-A'imma and Imam Sadr al-Islam), and Qadi al-Baydawi (from the Shafi'is) said that the shar'i haqiqah coined by the Lawgiver for legal meanings does not occur, and what is used for legal meanings is a well-known metaphor (majaz ishtahara). It is also attributed to Qadi al-Baqillani sometimes that they are linguistic true meanings used for legal meanings, and sometimes that they are used in their linguistic meanings, and the extra conditions are legally required for the validity of the basic meaning. Since this is necessarily false because it is certain that they are used for legal meanings, how can a master of sciences utter such? The author said, following the commentator of al-Mukhtasar, 'The truth is there is no third to these schools, and perhaps the Qadi did not explicitly state this, otherwise this statement would not be correct.' So if these shar'i terms appear in the speech of the Lawgiver before popular usage and without a contextual indicator for either meaning, which meaning should they be taken to? According to the proponent of shar'i haqiqah, they are taken to the legal meaning; according to its denier, they are taken to the linguistic meaning. This is the benefit of the disagreement. Our proof: usage without qarina in the legal meaning is verified, and that is a sign of truth (haqiqah). But this only works if the denier agrees. Also for us: the understanding of the Companions of the legal meaning likewise, i.e., without qarina, and understanding without qarina is evidence of truth. But this only works if it is established that their understanding occurred before popular usage without qarina, and this is extremely subtle. Also for us: the invalidity of negation, i.e., negating the name of shar'i terms from their legal meanings in the communicative convention of the Law; one cannot say of the specific pillars that they are not prayer, and the invalidity of negation is a marker of truth. But this only works if the invalidity is conceded before popular usage and in the speech of the Lawgiver, not in the speech of those following the Law. Also for us: the continuity (istimrar) of the Lawgiver upon the second (legal) meaning while leaving the first (linguistic) meaning except with evidence like a qarina. This continuity only occurs with truth. And this is the meaning of Ibn al-Hajib's statement: 'For us, certainty by induction that prayer, for example, is for the rak'ahs.' If he meant this continuity of usage in the rak'ahs, then the objection in al-Tahrir that it is not valid because one could be certain of popular usage among all Muslims and abandonment of the linguistic meaning, or certainty due to the people of the Law's coinage, is refuted. Then the argument by continuity is not much different from the argument by usage without qarina, and here too the claim of continuity is open to denial. And the answer to this argument is that the words remain in their linguistic meaning (e.g., prayer means supplication), and the extra conditions like standing, bowing, etc. are legal conditions for considering the supplication valid. But this answer does not apply to words like zakat, because zakat linguistically means growth and legally means the specific allocation of a specific property. There it is established.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (mas'ala) in the third article on linguistic fundamentals of usul al-fiqh. The issue is the nature of shar'i haqiqah (legal reality/true meaning). All agree that shar'i terms like salat, zakat, etc. have specific legal meanings and do not need contextual indicators (qarina) to convey those meanings among legal discourse. The disagreement is why these terms indicate their legal meanings without qarina: Is it because the Lawgiver (Allah or the Prophet) specifically coined or transferred these words to the legal meanings (thus they are haqiqah shar'iyyah), or is it simply due to widespread usage (ishtihar) among the Muslim community, making them 'well-known metaphors' (majaz ishtahara)? The passage presents the majority view (including the Mu'tazila) that they are genuine haqiqah through transfer (naql) or original coinage (wad'). Then it presents the view of several prominent scholars: al-Baqillani (Shafi'i), al-Dabusi (Hanafi), al-Bazdawi (Hanafi), and al-Baydawi (Shafi'i) who deny that there is a shar'i haqiqah coined by the Lawgiver; instead, the usage is a well-known metaphor. Some attribute to al-Baqillani an extreme view that the words remain linguistic truths and the extra conditions are merely legal prerequisites — but this is dismissed as obviously false. The practical consequence (fā'ida) of the disagreement is in the speech of the Lawgiver before the terms became famous: if a shar'i term is used without qarina, does it default to the legal meaning (according to those who affirm haqiqah shar'iyyah) or the linguistic meaning (according to the deniers)? The Hanafi authors provide four proofs for the majority position: (1) the actual usage without qarina is a sign of haqiqah; (2) the Companions' understanding of the legal meaning without qarina; (3) the impossibility of negating the legal meaning (e.g., you cannot deny that the pillars are prayer); (4) the continuity of the Lawgiver using the legal meaning while abandoning the linguistic meaning unless evidence indicates otherwise. Each proof is contested by the deniers, and the passage notes that the argument from continuity is similar to the first proof and also open to objection. The passage ends by discussing the counter-argument that the words remain in their linguistic sense and the legal conditions are merely add-ons, but notes this does not apply universally, as in the case of zakat where the legal meaning is entirely different.

Key Terms
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-haqiqah al-shar'iyyah): A word that has been assigned by the Lawgiver to a specific legal meaning, such that it is understood without a contextual indicator.
مجاز اشتهر (majaz ishtahara): A word that is originally metaphorical but has become so widely used in a particular meaning that no contextual indicator is needed; it is like haqiqah in its fame.
الاشتهار (al-ishtihar): Widespread usage among the Muslim community that causes a metaphorical meaning to become the default understanding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (fā'ida) of the disagreement over whether shar'i terms are haqiqah or majaz ishtahara?
A) Whether the terms can be used in non-legal contexts.
B) How to interpret these terms in the Lawgiver's speech before they became famous.
C) Whether the terms require a qarina in all contexts.
D) Whether the legal definitions are inherited from previous scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over haqiqah shar'iyyah centers on whether shar'i terms derive their immediate legal meaning from the Lawgiver's coinage or from popular usage, with the practical consequence affecting interpretation of early revelation.

The Legal Metaphor of Freeing for Divorce: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Analyzes the specific case of using 'freeing' for 'divorce' as a metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis reject the use of 'freeing' as a metaphor for 'divorce' and identify the key dispute about the linguistic meaning of 'freeing'.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, legal metaphor requires commonality in the legislated purpose, not just any shared attribute.

This passage dives into a subtle usul dispute: can the word 'freeing' (عتق) be used metaphorically for 'divorce' (طلاق)? At first glance, they share attributes like bindingness and permanence. But the Hanafi position, defended by Imam Fakhr al-Islam, insists that metaphor in legal acts must be based on the specific purpose for which each act is legislated. The discussion then unfolds into a deeper debate about the very meaning of 'freeing' — is it removal of ownership or establishment of legal strength? Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how usul principles govern the interpretation of legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage to find the central question: can 'freeing' be a metaphor for 'divorce'? One side says yes (common attributes suffice), the Hanafi side says no (requires commonality in legislated purpose).
The opposing view is attributed to an unnamed scholar, likely from another school (Shafi‘i) or an internal Hanafi dissenter.
2
Understand the Hanafi argument: the legislated purpose condition
Imam Fakhr al-Islam argues that mere shared attributes like bindingness or lack of jest are insufficient. Otherwise, absurd metaphors like 'sky' for 'earth' would be valid. The key is the specific legal purpose (ما شرع لأجله): freeing establishes strength, divorce removes a restriction.
This principle is central to Hanafi usul: the 'illah (effective cause) for legal acts is their legislated purpose, not any accidental feature.
3
Analyze the opposing argument and the rebuttal
The opponent counters that metaphor requires only some common attribute, and they share several like bindingness and effect on marital prohibition. The rebuttal reiterates that in legal contexts, the most specific attribute is the legislated purpose; ancillary attributes do not suffice for a valid linguistic transfer.
The passage notes 'traces of approval' for the opponent's view, but the Hanafi position prevails.
4
Examine the deeper linguistic dispute about 'freeing'
The discussion shifts to whether 'freeing' linguistically means removal of ownership or establishment of strength. The Revealer of Divine Secrets (a commentator) argues, citing linguistic evidence, that 'freeing' fundamentally means strength, not removal. This supports the Hanafi position by showing that the legal purposes of freeing and divorce are distinct.
This linguistic analysis is critical: if freeing means establishing strength, then its legislated purpose is that, not removal, so it cannot be metaphorically linked to divorce (which removes a restriction).
5
Reconstruct the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a husband tells his wife 'you are free' intending divorce, a Hanafi judge would not count it as a divorce, while a judge following the opposing view might. This shows how their methodological disagreement leads to different rulings.
Understanding the practical outcome reinforces why the dispute matters in real fatwa.

هذا ولنتكلم في فرعية مسألة الإعتاق، فاعلم أن تفريعهم عدم صحة تجويز الطلاق للعتاق باعتبار علاقة المسببية لا يوجب عدم الصحة لعلاقة أخرى، فإنه يجوز أن يكون استعارة لاشتراكهما في كونهما إزالتين للملك وتلوح آثار رضا مطلع الأسرار الإلهية بهذا، وجوابه أنّ مراد هذا الحبر أن لا بد ههنا من الاشتراك في المعنى المشروع كيف شرع لا في أي وصف كان مطلقاً، وذلك لأن الاستعارة مبناه على المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف المشتهرة بحيث يصح الانتقال، وهذا الوصف ههنا أي في الشرعيات ما شرع لأجله، فإن التصرفات الشرعية اشتهرت بأحكامها، فيعتبر الاشتراك فيها وههنا ليس الاشتراك في المعنى المشروع لأجله، إذ بالإعتاق تحدث قوة المعنى المنقول عنه مراعى في المنقول، بل نقول : لا نقل ههنا، فإن الإعتاق في اللغة لإزلة الملك لا غير وأما إثبات القوة فلا يفهمه إلا الأفراد من الفقهاء، ومعنى الإعتاق يفهمه كل أحد، هذا : قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية ناقلاً عن جدي المولى الشهيد قطب الملة والدين إنه لا نقل، بل في اللغة الإعتاق لإثبات القوة، فإن كل أحد يعلم ويحكم بأن الرقيق ضعيف والعتيق قوي والفقهاء إنما يعرفون كنه هذه القوة لا غير، فقد ظهر فساد قوله : إنّ إثبات القوة لا يعرفه إلا الآحاد من الفقهاء، والدليل على كونه موضوعاً بإزاء هذا المعنى أن العتيق في اللغة القوي والعتق القوة، فإذا جعل أفعالاً يكون إثبات القوة، فإن معنى المجرد محفوظ في أبواب المزيد ومن ادعى النقل في إزالة الملك فعليه البيان إذ الظاهر عدمه لأنه خلاف الأصل، وكذا لا مخلص عن البيان لمن ادعى أنه في اللغة لهذا المعنى فإن الظاهر أيضاً عدمه وإلا لزم
Now we speak on the sub-issue of freeing. Know that their subsidiary ruling—that permitting divorce for freeing is not valid based on the cause-effect relationship—does not necessitate invalidity based on another relationship. For it is possible that it be a metaphor (استعارة) due to their commonality in being removals of ownership and binding acts not affected by jest. The answer to this is from two aspects: First: What Imam Fakhr al-Islam stated: that mere commonality in absolute attributes is insufficient; otherwise, 'sky' for 'earth' and 'lion' for 'fox' would be valid due to commonality in 'thingness' and 'animality'. Rather, there must be commonality in the meaning for which it is legislated (المعنى المشروع كيف شرع), and that is not present here. For freeing is legislated to establish specific strength (القوة المخصوصة), and divorce to remove a restriction; there is no connection between them, so the metaphor is not valid. As for the statement from the Imam that freeing is removal of ownership such that it is divided by division of ownership, the intended meaning is that the act originates from him, not that freeing is this. How? While legal words are considered based on linguistic coinages. It is objected: we do not concede that metaphor requires commonality in the attribute for which it is legislated; rather, it requires commonality in some attribute. Here divorce and freeing share attributes such as bindingness, lack of effect of jest, and resulting prohibition of intercourse, etc., and these exist in both, so the metaphor between them should be valid. Traces of approval from the Revealer of Divine Secrets appear in this. The response: the intent of this learned one is that here there must be commonality in the meaning for which it is legislated, not in any attribute absolutely. Because metaphor is based on participation in the most specific well-known attributes such that transfer is valid. This attribute here, in legal matters, is that for which it is legislated. Legal acts are known by their rulings, and commonality is considered therein. Here, there is no commonality in the legislated meaning: by freeing, a legal strength is created; by divorce, a marriage restriction is lifted. Where is that? This is my view, and perhaps Allah will create something after this. And it was objected in al-Talwih: we do not concede that freeing is establishing strength; rather, it is removal of ownership. Whoever claims that must provide proof. It is insufficient that the original meaning is considered in the transferred. Rather, we say: there is no transfer here. For freeing in language is only for removal of ownership. As for establishing strength, only some jurists understand it. The meaning of freeing is understood by everyone. The Revealer of Divine Secrets said, quoting from his grandfather, the martyr, the Pole of the Religion and the Faith: there is no transfer; rather, in language freeing is for establishing strength. For everyone knows and judges that a slave is weak and a free person is strong; jurists only know the essence of this strength, nothing more. Thus, his statement that establishing strength is known only by a few jurists is proven false. The evidence that it is coined for this meaning is that 'عتيق' in language means strong, and 'عتق' means strength. When it is made into a verbal noun, it becomes establishing strength. The meaning of the simple form is preserved in the derived forms. Whoever claims transfer to removal of ownership must provide proof, since the apparent is its absence because it is contrary to the original. Similarly, there is no escape from proof for whoever claims that in language it is for this meaning; the apparent is also its absence, otherwise it would necessitate...

Commentary
This passage delves into the specific sub-issue of whether 'freeing a slave' (عتق) can be used metaphorically for 'divorce' (طلاق). The author explains that even if they deny the validity based on cause-effect relationship (i.e., freeing is a cause for marital separation?), it might be valid based on another relationship, such as shared attributes. The counterargument is presented: commonality in mere attributes is insufficient; it must be in the attribute for which the act is legislated (the legal purpose). Freeing is legislated to establish strength (القوة المخصوصة) which refers to the slave's new legal capacity, while divorce is legislated to remove a marital restriction. Since their legal purposes differ, metaphor fails. Then an objection is raised: they share attributes like being binding, unaffected by jest, and resulting in prohibition of intercourse—this should suffice. The response restates that only the legislated purpose matters. The discussion then moves into a debate about the true meaning of 'freeing' in language: is it removal of ownership or establishment of strength? The Revealer of Divine Secrets (likely a commentator) argues, quoting a chain, that linguistically 'عتق' means strength, not removal. The evidence is that 'عتيق' means strong. Thus, freeing cannot be simply removal of ownership. This supports the earlier argument that freeing and divorce have different legislated purposes, so metaphor is invalid. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti‘ārah): A type of metaphor based on similarity; here applied to legal acts like using 'freeing' for 'divorce'.
المعنى المشروع كيف شرع (al-ma‘nā al-mashrū‘ kayfa shuri‘a): The legal purpose or rationale for which an act is legislated; the specific wisdom behind the ruling.
إعتاق (i‘tāq): The act of freeing a slave; debated whether it is removal of ownership or establishment of legal strength.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis reject the metaphor of 'freeing' for 'divorce' according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam?
A) Because freeing and divorce do not share any linguistic attributes.
B) Because they share too many attributes, causing confusion.
C) Because metaphor requires commonality in the legislated purpose, and freeing establishes strength while divorce removes a restriction.
D) Because al-Shafi‘i forbids it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a legal metaphor (isti‘ārah) is only valid when the two acts share their legislated purpose (ma shar‘a lahu), not merely any common attribute.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Condition of Isti'ara in Legal Acts: Divorce and Manumission
Analyzing the validity of metaphor between talaq and itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition for valid metaphor in legal acts and apply it to the case of talaq and itq.

Core Idea
Metaphor in Shari'a requires sharing the specific legislative purpose, not just any shared attribute.

This passage examines a critical question in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can the word for divorce (talaq) be used metaphorically for manumission (itq), and vice versa? The discussion reveals a fundamental condition for valid isti'ara in legal contexts: the metaphor must be based on the specific purpose (ma'na mashru') for which the act was legislated, not merely any shared quality. This lesson unpacks the dispute between Fakhr al-Islam and his opponents, the linguistic analysis of itq, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what is being disputed: the validity of using the word for talaq as a metaphor for itq, and under what conditions.
The dispute is about the requirements for isti'ara in Shari'a.
2
State the opposing view
Summarize the argument that sharing in any attribute (e.g., both remove ownership, are binding) suffices for metaphor.
This view relies on linguistic practice and apparent approval by the Revealer of Divine Secrets.
3
Present the Hanafi position (Fakhr al-Islam)
Explain that sharing in the legislative purpose (ma'na mashru') is necessary. Manumission establishes strength; divorce removes a marital restriction; these purposes are unrelated.
The Hanafi position is supported by an analogy: if any attribute sufficed, 'sky' could be used for 'earth'.
4
Follow the linguistic proof
Analyze the definition of itq: linguistic evidence shows it means establishment of strength, not removal of ownership.
The Revealer of Divine Secrets cites that 'atiq' means strong and 'itq' means strength.
5
Apply the second aspect: stronger for weaker
Understand the Arabic principle that metaphor uses the stronger for the weaker, not vice versa. Since itq is stronger in removing ownership than talaq, it cannot be a metaphor for talaq.
This reinforces the invalidity of the metaphor in both directions.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية

المسبب للسبب وبالعكس كالنبت للغيث وبالعكس) ولعل الشافعية لا يقنعون بهذا، بل لا يسلمون عدم ثبوت هذا النوع ويتهمون الاستقراء وخصوص عدم إجازة المطر للسماء وتجويز العكس لا يصلح حجة لجواز أن يكون هناك مانع آخر، والمثال الجزئي لا يكفي لتصحيح القاعدة الكلية هذا ولنتكلم في فرعية مسألة الإعتاق، فاعلم أن تفريعهم عدم صحة تجويز الطلاق للعتاق باعتبار علاقة المسببية لا يوجب عدم الصحة لعلاقة أخرى، فإنه يجوز أن يكون استعارة لاشتراكهما في كونهما إزالتين للملك وتصرفين لازمين غير مؤثر فيهما الهزل، والجواب عنه من وجهين :

الأول : ما أفاده الإمام فخر الإسلام أنه لا يكفي الاشتراك في مطلق الأوصاف، كيف وإلا لصح السماء للأرض، والأسد للثعلب، لأجل الاشتراك في الشيئية والحيوانية، بل لا بد من الاشتراك في المعنى المشروح كيف شرع وليس ههنا فإن الإعتاق شرع لإثبات القوة المخصوصة، والطلاق لإزالة القيد ولا اتصال بينهما فلا تصح الاستعارة، وما عن الإمام أن الإعتاق إزالة الملك حتى يتجزأ بتجزىء الملك، فالمراد منه أن التصرف الصادر منه هذا لا أن الإعتاق هذا كيف والألفاظ الشرعية اعتبرت فيها الأوضاع اللغوية: ويرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن لا بد للاستعارة من الاشتراك في وصف شرع لأجله، بل لا بد لها من الاشتراك في وصف ما، وههنا الطلاق والعتاق قد اشتركا في أوصاف كاللزوم وعدم تأثير الهزل وإفادة حرمة الفرج وغير ذلك وهذا موجود في الطلاق والعتاق فينبغي أن تصح الاستعارة بينهما، وتلوح آثار رضا مطلع الأسرار الإلهية بهذا، وجوابه أنّ مراد هذا الحبر أن لا بد ههنا من الاشتراك في المعنى المشروع كيف شرع لا في أي وصف كان مطلقاً، وذلك لأن الاستعارة مبناه على المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف المشتهرة بحيث يصح الانتقال، وهذا الوصف ههنا أي في الشرعيات ما شرع لأجله، فإن التصرفات الشرعية اشتهرت بأحكامها، فيعتبر الاشتراك فيها وههنا ليس الاشتراك في المعنى المشروع لأجله، إذ بالإعتاق تحدث قوة شرعية وبالطلاق يرتفع قيد النكاح، فأين من ذاك؟ هذا ما عندي ولعل الله يحدث بعد ذلك أمراً واعترض في التلويح بأنا لا نسلم أن الإعتاق إثبات القوة، بل هو إزالة الملك، ومن ادعى فعليه البيان، ولا يكفي كون المعنى المنقول عنه مراعى في المنقول، بل نقول : لا نقل ههنا، فإن الإعتاق في اللغة لإزلة الملك لا غير وأما إثبات القوة فلا يفهمه إلا الأفراد من الفقهاء، ومعنى الإعتاق يفهمه كل أحد، هذا : قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية ناقلاً عن جدي المولى الشهيد قطب الملة والدين إنه لا نقل، بل في اللغة الإعتاق لإثبات القوة، فإن كل أحد يعلم ويحكم بأن الرقيق ضعيف والعتيق قوي والفقهاء إنما يعرفون كنه هذه القوة لا غير، فقد ظهر فساد قوله : إنّ إثبات القوة لا يعرفه إلا الآحاد من الفقهاء، والدليل على كونه موضوعاً بإزاء هذا المعنى أن العتيق في اللغة القوي والعتق القوة، فإذا جعل أفعالاً يكون إثبات القوة، فإن معنى المجرد محفوظ في أبواب المزيد ومن ادعى النقل في إزالة الملك فعليه البيان إذ الظاهر عدمه لأنه خلاف الأصل، وكذا لا مخلص عن البيان لمن ادعى أنه في اللغة لهذا المعنى فإن الظاهر أيضاً عدمه وإلا لزم الاشتراك، هذا، ثم بقي كلام أورده صدر الشريعة وهو أنَّا نجعل الطلاق مستعاراً لإزالة الملك لا للإعتاق، فينبغي أن يقع العتاق وأجيب بأن الإعتاق تصرف شرعي لا بد له من لفظ يدل عليه حقيقة أو مجازاً، وإزالة الملك يدل عليه مجازاً إطلاقاً للسبب على المسبب، وأما الطلاق المستعار لإزالة الملك، فلا يدل على الإعتاق أصلاً، وأورد أنه يجوز أن يقع العتاق بالدلالة الالتزامية، وأجيب بأن إزالة الملك ليس ملزوماً للعتاق، فإنه يكون بالتبع ولذا لا يعتق بقوله لا ملك لي عليك، بل لا بد من صيغة الإعتاق وليس ههنا إلا إذا قصد بإزالة الملك العتاق، فيلزم التجوز في التجوز وتعقب عليه المطلع على الأسرار الإلهية بأن إزالة الملك لا إلى مالك ملزوم العتاق وأما قوله لا ملك لي إخبار عن عدم ملكه فإن وجد مالك آخر فله وإلا الأصل بخلاف ما نحن فيه وأيضاً بإرادة الإعتاق من الإزالة لا يلزم التجوز في المجاز، بل يجوز أن يكون على طريق الكناية البيانية هذا. الوجه الثاني: أن استعارة القوي للضعيف صحيحة دون العكس، فلا يقال للأسد زيد، بل بالعكس وههنا الإعتاق أقوى في إزالة الملك الطلاق، فلا للعتاق وأورد صاحب التلويح بأن الإعتاق قاصر في إزالة الملك، لأنه يبقى أثره من الولاء، وأيضاً ليس ضرورياً في التشبيه كونه أقوى ، وجوابه أن الإعتاق يجعل الرقبة قوية بعدما كانت ضعيفة بالملك، فله تأثير قوي في الإزالة من الطلاق فإنه لا يزيل ضعف الملك أصلاً، بل يرفع قيد ملك النكاح وهو ملك ضعيف فإزالته إزالة ضعيفة والولاء ليس أثراً للملك، بل لحمة كلحمة النسب، ومن ضروريات العربية أن الاستعارة للمبالغة وهو في استعمال الأشد الأضعف دون العكس هذا والله أعلم بأحكامه.
The Third Discourse on Linguistic Principles: The caused is [named] for the cause and vice versa, like vegetation for rain and vice versa. Perhaps the Shafi'is are not satisfied with this; rather, they do not concede the establishment of this type and accuse induction, especially that the rain is not called sky and vice versa is not a valid proof because there might be another impediment, and a particular example is insufficient to correct a general rule. Let us speak on the subsidiary issue of manumission. Know that their branch that the permission of divorce for manumission based on the relation of causality does not necessitate invalidity for another relation; for it is possible that it is a metaphor due to their sharing in being removals of ownership and necessary dispositions not affected by jest. The answer to this is from two aspects: First: What Imam Fakhr al-Islam stated that sharing in absolute attributes is insufficient; otherwise, sky would be correct for earth, and lion for fox, due to sharing in thingness and animality. Rather, sharing in the meaning as legislated is necessary, and that is not here. For manumission is legislated to establish specific strength, and divorce to remove a restriction; there is no connection between them, so the metaphor is invalid. And what from Imam that manumission is removal of ownership so that it is divided by the division of ownership—the intent is that the disposition issuing from him is this, not that manumission is this. How can that be, while legal terms consider linguistic usages? It is replied: We do not concede that metaphor requires sharing in a description for which it was legislated; rather, it requires sharing in some description. Here, divorce and manumission share in descriptions like necessity, non-effect of jest, and causing prohibition of the private part, etc. This exists in both, so metaphor should be valid between them, and the approval of the Revealer of Divine Secrets appears in this. The answer: This scholar's intent is that sharing here must be in the meaning as legislated, not in any absolute description, because metaphor is based on sharing in the most specific well-known descriptions such that transition is valid. And this description here, i.e., in legal matters, is what is legislated for it. For legal acts are known by their rulings, so sharing in them is considered, and here there is no sharing in the meaning for which it was legislated, since by manumission a legal strength occurs, and by divorce the restriction of marriage is removed. So where is that? This is what I have, and perhaps God will bring about something after this. An objection was raised in al-Talwih: We do not concede that manumission is the establishment of strength; rather, it is the removal of ownership, and whoever claims otherwise must provide proof. It is not sufficient that the transferred meaning is considered in the target meaning; rather, we say there is no transference here, for manumission in language is only removal of ownership. As for the establishment of strength, only a few jurists understand it, while the meaning of manumission is understood by everyone. The Revealer of Divine Secrets said, narrating from his grandfather the martyr Qutb al-Milla wal-Din, that there is no transference; rather, in language manumission is for the establishment of strength, for everyone knows and judges that a slave is weak and a freed person is strong; jurists only know the essence of this strength. Thus, his statement that establishment of strength is known only by a few jurists is shown to be false. The proof that it is placed for this meaning is that al-atiq in language means strong, and al-itiq means strength. When it is made into a verb form, it becomes the establishment of strength, as the meaning of the simple form is preserved in the derived forms. Whoever claims transference to removal of ownership must provide proof, for the apparent is its absence because it is contrary to the original principle. Similarly, there is no escape from proof for whoever claims that it is in language for this meaning, for the apparent is also its absence; otherwise, polysemy would result. Then there remains a statement by Sadr al-Shari'a: We make divorce a metaphor for removal of ownership, not for manumission, so manumission should occur. The answer: Manumission is a legal act that requires a word indicating it literally or metaphorically. Removal of ownership indicates it metaphorically by applying the cause to the effect. As for divorce used metaphorically for removal of ownership, it does not indicate manumission at all. It is objected that manumission could occur by implicational signification. The answer: Removal of ownership is not a necessary concomitant of manumission, for it occurs by dependency; hence, one does not become free by saying 'I have no ownership over you'; rather, a formula of manumission is necessary, and that is not here unless one intends manumission by removal of ownership, which would require metaphor in metaphor. The Revealer of Divine Secrets criticized this: Removal of ownership without a specific owner is a necessary concomitant of manumission. As for his saying 'I have no ownership over you,' it is an informatory statement about his lack of ownership; if there is another owner, it goes to him; otherwise, the original principle applies, unlike our case. Also, by intending manumission from removal, metaphor in metaphor is not necessary; rather, it can be by way of allegorical suggestion (kinaya). The second aspect: The metaphor of the strong for the weak is valid, not vice versa; one does not say 'Zaid is a lion' but the opposite. Here, manumission is stronger in removing ownership than divorce, so [the metaphor] is not for manumission. The author of al-Talwih objected: Manumission is deficient in removing ownership because its effect of clientage remains; also, being stronger is not necessary in simile. The answer: Manumission makes the neck strong after it was weak by ownership, so it has a strong effect in removal compared to divorce, which does not remove the weakness of ownership at all but only lifts the restriction of marriage ownership, which is weak ownership, so its removal is a weak removal. Clientage is not an effect of ownership but a bond like the bond of lineage. It is a necessity of Arabic that metaphor is for exaggeration, which is in using the stronger for the weaker, not the opposite. This is the position, and God knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the word for divorce (talaq) can be used metaphorically for manumission (itq) and vice versa. The discussion begins with a reference to Shafi'i objections about a certain type of majaz, but quickly focuses on a subsidiary issue: the validity of using talaq as a metaphor for itq. The author states that the opponent's argument based on causality does not preclude validity through another relation, suggesting a metaphor based on shared qualities (both remove ownership, are binding, not affected by jest). The answer has two aspects.

First aspect: Imam Fakhr al-Islam argues that sharing in any attribute is insufficient; the metaphor requires sharing in the specific purpose for which the act was legislated. Manumission is for establishing strength, divorce for removing a marriage restriction; no connection exists, so the metaphor fails. A rebuttal claims that sharing in attributes like necessity suffices, and the Revealer of Divine Secrets seems to approve. The response clarifies that the required sharing is in the most specific descriptions that are well-known in legal acts—specifically, the purpose of the legislation. Since the purposes differ, the metaphor is invalid.

A further objection from al-Talwih contests the definition of itq as establishment of strength, arguing it is removal of ownership. The Revealer of Divine Secrets refutes this by citing linguistic evidence: 'atiq means strong, 'itq means strength, so the verb 'a'taqa means to establish strength. This is a Zahir al-Riwayah position supported by a linguistic analysis. The objection that only a few jurists understand this is shown false because common people know that a freed person is strong.

The discussion then moves to Sadr al-Shari'a's suggestion: make talaq a metaphor for removal of ownership, not for itq, so that itq would occur. This is rejected because itq requires a specific formula; using talaq metaphorically for removal does not imply itq. The possibility of implicational signification is also rejected because removal of ownership is not a necessary concomitant of itq (it can be by dependency). The Revealer of Divine Secrets clarifies that removal of ownership to no owner is a necessary concomitant, but using an explicit statement like 'I have no ownership over you' does not effect manumission due to absence of transfer formula. The suggestion of metaphor in metaphor is unnecessary; it can be allegorical suggestion (kinaya).

Second aspect: A stronger entity is used metaphorically for a weaker one, not vice versa. Since itq is stronger in removing ownership than talaq, it cannot be used as a metaphor for talaq. Objections (itq leaves clientage, strength not necessary in simile) are answered: itq makes the neck strong, whereas divorce only lifts a weak restriction; clientage is like lineage, not ownership. The principle of Arabic that metaphor exaggerates requires using the stronger for the weaker.

The overall ruling is that the metaphor between divorce and manumission is invalid in both directions. This dispute shows how Hanafi usul applies conditions for majaz in legal terms.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti'ara): A linguistic mechanism (metaphor) where a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a relation (alaka) between the two meanings.
معنى مشروع (ma'na mashru'): The specific purpose or objective for which a legal act (like divorce or manumission) is legislated in the Shari'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Fakhr al-Islam reject the metaphor of divorce for manumission?
A) Because manumission and divorce do not share any attributes.
B) Because they share in the attribute of removing ownership, which is not a legislative purpose.
C) Because manumission is legislated to establish strength, and divorce to remove a restriction; they have no common legislative purpose.
D) Because divorce is a stronger act than manumission, so the metaphor would be from weaker to stronger.

Key Takeaway
Isti'ara between legal acts is valid only if the acts share the same legislative purpose; the purposes of talaq and itq differ, so the metaphor is invalid.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Ruling on Kinayah Talaq: Ba'in vs Raj'i
Establishes Hanafi view that kinayah talaq is ba'in, rebuts Shafi'i claim of revocability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kinayah talaq and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using linguistic and shari'i reasoning.

Core Idea
A word's literal meaning and the Lawgiver's recognition decide divorce rulings, not just intention.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a foundational usul dispute: do indirect expressions of divorce (kinayah) produce a revocable or irrevocable divorce? The Hanafi school holds that such expressions effect an irrevocable divorce (ba'in), while the Shafi'i school argues they are revocable once intention clarifies the meaning. The debate hinges on how the Lawgiver (shari') defines the categories of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What exactly is in dispute? The revocability of kinayah talaq.
The issue is whether words like 'anti ba'in' result in a raj'i (revocable) or ba'in (irrevocable) divorce.
2
State the Hanafi position
Kinayah talaq is ba'in, not raj'i. Note the exception for intending three divorces.
The Hanafi position is that the husband cannot return during iddah except by new marriage.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its dalil
Shafi'i: once intention specifies the word for divorce, it becomes like explicit talaq, which is raj'i.
The Shafi'i rely on the effect of intention to transform the word into a clear pronouncement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
Linguistic: 'ba'in' literally means irrevocable separation. Context: Quran 2:229 only guarantees revocability for legislated talaq. Analogy: khul' shows the Lawgiver permits immediate separation.
The Hanafi response refutes the idea that intention changes the word's basic meaning.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a husband saying 'anti ba'in' cannot take his wife back during iddah according to Hanafi, but can according to Shafi'i.
This shows how a usul dispute directly affects fiqh practice.

الفائدة الثالثة: كنايات الطلاق نحو أنت بائن، وغيره بوائن عندنا لا يملك الزوج الرجوع إليها إلا بنكاح جديد، إلا أن يراد الإبانة الغليظة بإيقاع الثلاث، فحينئذ لا يملك النكاح الجديد إلا اعتدي بالنص فإن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم طلق أم المؤمنين سودة بهذا اللفظ ثم بعد الاستشفاع راجعها وفي حكم هذا اللفظ استبرئي رحمك، وأنت واحدة، قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية: إن الأحاديث الصحاح المروية في كتب الحديث تدل على أنه لم يطلقها، وإنما أراد التطليق حتى استشفعت ووهبت نوبتها لأم المؤمنين عائشة وندمت لأجل أن تحشر في زمرة الأزواج، فأمسك عن طلاقها، وفصل الأحاديث، ومن شاء فليرجع إليه، إلا أن يراد بالطلاق الإبانة بإيقاع الثلاث في الأحاديث المروية في كتب الحديث، وهو بعيد هذا، ثم إنه لا يحتاج في إثبات كونها رجعيات إلى تعب كثير، فإن اعتدي وأمثالها لا تدل على البينونة أصلاً فلا تكون مثبتة إياها. وكنايات الطلاق رواجع يصح للزوج الرجوع إليها من غير تجديد نكاح عند الشافعي: لأن المراد في هذه الألفاظ إذا تعين صار كالصريح في إفادة الطلاق، وفي الصريح تقع رجعية فكذا هنا. لنا أن البينونة باقية على معناها لا أنها صارت بمعنى الطلاق والاستتار في المراد باعتبار التعلق أي تعلق البينونة بأي شيء فلا يعلم أبائن من الخير أو من النكاح فصارت كناية من هذا الوجه فإذا تعين بالنية في المتعلق أنه النكاح عمل بحقيقة اللفظ وهو البينونة عن النكاح فيقع البائن ولا نسلم أنها بعد تعين المراد صار تصريح الطلاق ومفيداً لمعناه. هذا ولمطلع الأسرار الإلهية قدس سره ههنا تحقيق هو: أن الطلاق كما قد مر عبارة عن رفع قيد النكاح وهو البينونة ثم التصرفات إنما تقع على حسب ما اعتبره الشارع، ألا ترى أنه لا يقع ألف طلاق، وكذا طلاق بائن لا تحل إلا بعد التحليلين من الزوجين، وقد عقب الشارع الرجعة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ الطلاق مرتان فإمساك بمعروف أو تسريح بإحسان ﴾ [البقرة: 229] وهو مجرد رفع قيد النكاح بأي لفظ كان فثبت الرجعة بعد الطلاق بأي لفظ كان ما لم يكن فيه مال لعدم تعقيبه بالرجعة وما كان دون الثلاث، وحينئذ لا يخفى ما في الاستدلال، فإن غاية ما لزم أن مدلول الكنايات البينونة وهذا مسلم، ولا يلزم منه الوقوع كذلك أي على وجه البينونة وعدم صحة المراجعة إلا بعد اعتبار الشارع هذا النوع من التصرف ولم يعتبر كما هدانا إليه الآية الكريمة. وكان قدس سره يبالغ فيه، وقد سمعت مكرراً من لسانه الشريف أن الطلاق البائن الخفيف ليس بشيء عندي سوى ما أبدل به المال هذا. وهذا العبد مع الاعتراف بالقصور في أمثال هذه المعارف يبرز ما تعلق بالخاطر أن حاصل الآية الكريمة أن الطلاق المشروع تطليقة بعد تطليقة، وحكمه الإمساك بالمعروف بالرجعة أو التسريح بإحسان بتركه أو بالطلقة الثالثة على الاختلاف، وعدم حل الأخذ إلا عند النشوز. ولو لم يقيد الطلاق بالمشروع لا نحصر الطلقات في التكرير، ولم يقع الاثنان أو الثلاث دفعة، والمراد بالتكرير أن يكون في طهرين كما بين في الحديث، وحينئذ نقول: لا يلزم تعقب الرجعة إلا في الطلاق المسنون فإنه طلاق واحد رجعي بالاتفاق، وإنما الكلام في أنه: هل يقع طلاق بائن عند الإيقاع وإن كان غير مشروع فلم يلزم، ثم إنه لا بد من التخصيص أيضاً بما لم يكن على مال فيصير حجة ظنية فيصح الزيادة عليه والتخصيص، نعم. بقي مطالبة الدليل على اعتبار الشارع هذا النحو من التصرفات، فأقول وبالله التوفيق أن الله تعالى أباح الخلع وجعله سبباً لوقوع الفرقة في الحال وعدم صحة الرجعة وهو طلاق كما بين في أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى وسيجيء، فقد علم أن في ملك الزوج طلاقاً موجباً للفرقة في الحال أخذ المال عليه، وإذا كان في يده ذلك بالعوض، فكذلك يكون في يده من غير عوض، بل أولى لأن ردّ المعطي نوع دناءة هذا ما عندي والله تعالى أعلم بأحكامه.
The third benefit: Kinayat al-talaq, such as 'you are ba'in' and other [words] ba'in, according to us the husband does not have the right to return to her except with a new marriage, unless the severe separation by effecting three [divorces] is intended, then he does not own the new marriage except by 'iddah according to the text, for the Messenger of Allah ﷺ divorced the Mother of the Believers Sawda with this word, then after intercession he took her back. And in the ruling of this word are 'istabri'i rahimak' and 'you are one'. The author of 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: Authentic hadiths in hadith books indicate that he did not divorce her, but rather intended to divorce until she interceded and gave her turn to the Mother of the Believers Aisha and regretted, so that she would be gathered in the company of the wives, so he refrained from divorcing her. He detailed the hadiths; whoever wishes may refer to him. Unless what is intended by divorce is the separation by effecting three in the hadiths in hadith books, and that is far-fetched. Then, there is no need for much effort in proving that they are revocable, for 'i'taddi' and the like do not indicate irrevocability at all, so they are not establishing it. And kinayat al-talaq are revocable: it is valid for the husband to return to her without renewing the marriage according to al-Shafi'i, because when the intended meaning of these words is specified, it becomes like explicit [words] in effecting divorce, and in the explicit [words] a revocable divorce occurs, so here likewise. For us, irrevocability remains in its meaning, not that it has become in the meaning of divorce; and concealment in the intended [meaning] by way of attachment, i.e., the attachment of irrevocability to something, so it is not known whether it is separation from good or from marriage, so it becomes a kinayah from this aspect. So when it is specified by intention regarding the attachment that it is marriage, one acts according to the literal meaning of the word, which is irrevocability from marriage, so an irrevocable divorce occurs. And we do not concede that after the intended meaning is specified it becomes an explicit pronouncement of divorce and beneficial for its meaning. This, and the author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may his secret be sanctified) has an investigation here: that divorce, as has passed, is an expression for removing the tie of marriage which is irrevocability, then actions occur according to what the Lawgiver has considered. Do you not see that the word 'thousand divorces' does not occur? Likewise, an irrevocable divorce does not become permissible except after two tahlils from the husband? And the Lawgiver connected return with His saying: 'Divorce is twice; then retention in kindness or release with goodness' [2:229], and it is merely removing the tie of marriage by whatever word it is, so return is established after divorce by whatever word it is, as long as there is no property [exchange] for it, due to the lack of its being followed by return, and it being less than three. Then, it is not hidden what is in the deduction, for the utmost that follows is that the signification of kinayat is irrevocability, and this is conceded, and it does not necessitate occurrence likewise, i.e., in the manner of irrevocability and the invalidity of return, except after the Lawgiver has considered this type of action and He did not consider it, as the noble verse guides us. And he (may his secret be sanctified) used to exaggerate in this, and I have heard repeatedly from his noble tongue that the light irrevocable divorce is nothing according to me except that with which property is given. And this servant, while admitting deficiency in such knowledges, presents what occurred to the mind: that the gist of the noble verse is that legislated divorce is one divorce after another, and its ruling is retention with goodness by return or release with goodness by leaving it or by the third divorce, according to difference, and the impermissibility of taking [property] except in case of nushuz. And if divorce were not restricted to the legislated, we would not limit divorces to repetition, and two or three [divorces] would not occur at once. And the meaning of 'repetition' is that it be in two tuhrs, as explained in the hadith. Then we say: The requirement of subsequent return is only in the sunni divorce, for it is a single revocable divorce by agreement. The discussion is only about: does an irrevocable divorce occur upon pronouncement even if it is not legislated? So it is not required. Then, it must be specified also as not involving property, so it becomes a speculative proof, thus addition and specification upon it are valid. Yes. There remains a demand for evidence on the Lawgiver's consideration of this type of actions. So I say, and by God is success: God Almighty permitted khul' and made it a cause for the occurrence of separation immediately and the invalidity of return, and it is a divorce, as explained in the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may God have mercy on him) and will come. Then it was known that in the husband's possession is a divorce that necessitates immediate separation upon taking property for it. And if it is in his hand with compensation, likewise it is in his hand without compensation; rather it is more appropriate, because returning what is given is a kind of baseness. This is what I have, and God Almighty knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute about kinayat al-talaq (indirect expressions of divorce). The author presents the Hanafi position: words like 'anti ba'in' effect an irrevocable divorce (ba'in), so the husband cannot take back his wife except by a new marriage, unless three divorces are intended. He then rebuts the Shafi'i claim that such expressions are revocable (raj'i) because they become like explicit words once the intention is known. The Hanafi argument hinges on the nature of kinayah: the word 'ba'in' literally means 'separated', and it remains a kinayah due to ambiguity in what it is attached to (e.g., from good or from marriage). Even if intention specifies it for marriage, it still means irrevocability, not divorce per se, so it remains ba'in. The author then quotes a hadith about the Prophet divorcing Sawda using such a word, but the commentator (Matali' al-Asrar) argues the hadith shows he did not actually divorce her. The author also uses Quran 2:229 to argue that all divorce is revocable by default unless it is the third or with compensation, and since kinayah talaq is not explicitly recognized as revocable by the Lawgiver, it remains ba'in. Finally, he analogizes from khul' (divorce with compensation) which the Lawgiver made immediately separate. The lesson demonstrates Hanafi usul: using literal meanings, analyzing the Lawgiver's recognition of certain actions, and employing istihsan (in the argument about khul' being more appropriate).

Key Terms
كنايات الطلاق (kināyāt al-ṭalāq): Expressions that indirectly imply divorce, such as 'anti ba'in' (you are separated), which require intention to effect divorce.
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage.
رجعية (raj'iyya): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during iddah without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the key reason that kinayah talaq is ba'in rather than raj'i?
A) The word 'ba'in' literally means irrevocability, and the Lawgiver did not include kinayah in the revocable category.
B) Intention is irrelevant to divorce.
C) The Prophet's practice shows it is revocable.
D) All divorces are irrevocable except the third.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah talaq in the Hanafi school is ba'in unless it is the third divorce, because the word's literal meaning and the Lawgiver's classification prevail over mere intention.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced

The Reality of Kinayah in Divorce
Analyzing whether divorce kinayah are true kinayah or not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi classification of divorce kinayah as kinayah of irrevocable separation and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Divorce kinayah are kinayah of irrevocable separation, not revocable divorce.

The classification of divorce kinayah (e.g., 'you are free') has significant legal consequences. This passage engages with al-Shafi'i's objection that such words, being kinayah of divorce, should result in revocable divorce. The Hanafi response refines the linguistic analysis: these words are kinayah of absolute separation, not of the revocable divorce that explicit words effect. Understanding this distinction is crucial for both usul and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is whether divorce kinayah are true kinayah and what legal effect they produce. Determine the opposing positions.
2
Analyze the linguistic nature
Examine the arguments for and against considering them kinayah. Note the role of intention and known lexical meaning.
3
Trace the Hanafi response
Understand why the Hanafis hold that these words are kinayah of irrevocable separation (bainunah), not of revocable divorce.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: in Hanafi, intended kinayah yields irrevocable divorce; in Shafi'i, revocable divorce.
5
Reflect on the method
Note how linguistic classification drives the ruling and how Hanafi usul uses intention to determine the legal effect.

The fourth benefit: They said that kinayah of divorce is metaphorical, in order to repulse what al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) objected: because they are kinayah of divorce, thus their ruling is its ruling, so they occur as revocable divorces. It was said in its verification that they are not true kinayah because they are used in their real meanings, so they are not kinayah. And in it there is no contradiction between being used in their real meanings and being kinayah, for they are both reality and metaphor. And it was said they are not kinayah because they are not concealed in meaning, for their lexical meanings (which is separation) are known; the uncertainty is only in an external matter of their meanings, which is the object of separation: one does not know whether it is separation from good or from marriage or from something else. So this concealment does not make them kinayah. And in it that kinayah is by virtue of concealing the intended usage, which is present here, even if it is due to uncertainty in the object and even if the lexical meaning is known, like the ambiguous and the specific used in a particular individual, so knowing the lexical meanings does not remove them from being kinayah. Then it refutes these two sayings: if they were not kinayah, they should be explicit truths, thus intention would not be required in them, and the ruling would be by the exact utterance, which no one has said. Also, denying kinayah does not help in answering al-Shafi'i's objection, for he can say that these words are equivalent to explicit divorce, because they are explicit in it or kinayah in it, and on both estimates their meaning is divorce, so they occur as revocable. So does denying kinayah benefit anything? It was said in al-Tahrir that there is a metaphor in the addition (i.e., adding kinayah to divorce), and the correct is that they are really kinayah of absolute irrevocable separation, for the apparent understanding from them is that they are kinayah of divorce, and in its meaning it is not so, otherwise it would occur as revocable, for what occurs by the word 'divorce' is revocable. And this is correct and in the answer and repelling the objection, so reflect.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of whether divorce kinayah (e.g., 'you are free' or 'you are separated') are truly kinayah or not, in response to al-Shafi'i's argument that such words should result in revocable divorce because they are kinayah of divorce. The author presents several opinions: (1) they are not true kinayah because they are used in their real meanings, (2) they are not kinayah because the lexical meaning is known and only the object is uncertain, (3) the correct view is that they are kinayah of irrevocable separation (bainunah kubra). The author concludes that denying kinayah does not refute al-Shafi'i; rather, the proper answer is that these words are kinayah of irrevocable divorce, not revocable. This is a common Hanafi position: kinayah of divorce results in bainunah if intended, not raj'ah. The passage shows the interplay between linguistic classification and legal ruling.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An expression that indirectly indicates a meaning, requiring intention; the lexical meaning is known but the intended application is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) They are not kinayah because the lexical meaning is known.
C) They are kinayah of absolute irrevocable separation.
D) They are kinayah of revocable divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies divorce kinayah as kinayah of irrevocable separation, not revocable divorce, resolving al-Shafi'i's objection.

Why Kinayah Cannot Establish Hudud
Explaining why kinayah cannot establish hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why kinayah creates shubha and state the Hanafi ruling that hudud are not established by kinayah.

Core Idea
Kinayah is weak because it implies shubha; hudud are repelled by doubt.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains why indirect expressions (kinayah) cannot establish hudud punishments. It defines kinayah as having an apparent deficiency and explicit concealment, which creates a shubha (doubt) that repels the fixed penalty. The text gives two examples: confirming a slanderer and using indirect expressions like 'you are not a fornicator' to imply fornication. The Hanafi school holds that such expressions are insufficient for hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept
Recognize that kinayah (indirect expression) is defined as having an apparent deficiency and explicit concealment. This inherent weakness creates a shubha (doubt).
2
Connect to the shubha principle
Recall the Hanafi usul principle: hudud punishments are repelled by shubha (the hadith 'idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat').
3
Apply to tasdiq al-qadif
Analyze the case: one says to a slanderer 'you have told the truth.' This could mean 'your habit is truth' or 'you are truthful in the slander,' creating doubt. Thus no hadd.
4
Apply to ta'rid
Analyze the indirect expression: 'you are not a fornicator' may intend to deny fornication from oneself but imply it of the addressee. The intended meaning is hidden, so it is like non-existence.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal disagrees and may enforce hudud based on such expressions, considering the apparent meaning sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الخامسة) في (الكناية قصور ظاهر و خفاء صريح كما ينادي عليه حده (ففيه شبهة العدم أي عدم ما قصد به فإنه يجوز إن كان قصد غيره من المعنى، وكذا فيه شبهة احتمال آخر فلا يثبت به ما يندرىء بالشبهة وهي الحدود وإلا لزم ثبوته مع منافيه (فلا يحد مصدق القاذف أي القائل للقاذف صدقت من غير ذلك المفعول فإنه يحتمل إرادة أن ديدنك الصدق، كما يحتمل أنك صادق في القذف (ولا المعرض به أي بالقذف والتعريض هو أن يتلفظ بكلام دال علی معنی قصد به معنی آخر كلست بزان فإنه دال على نفي الزنا عن المتكلم والغرض منه النسبة للمخاطب مثلاً بالزنا، وهذه الإرادة أمر خفي في حكم العدم، فلا يعتبر به، وفي هذا خلاف الإمام أحمد بن حنبل.
The fifth benefit: In kinayah there is apparent deficiency and explicit concealment, as its definition indicates. For in it there is a semblance of non-existence, i.e., non-existence of what was intended, because it is possible that a different meaning was intended. Likewise, in it there is a semblance of another probability, so it cannot establish what is repelled by a semblance (shubha), which is the hudud (fixed punishments). Otherwise, it would be established along with what contradicts it. So the one who confirms the slanderer (i.e., who says to the slanderer 'you have told the truth') is not lashed, because it may be intended to mean 'your habit is truth' as well as 'you are truthful in the slander'. Nor is the one who uses indirect expression (ta'rid) in slander punished; and indirect expression is to utter words that indicate a meaning but intend another meaning, like 'you are not a fornicator' — it indicates negation of fornication from the speaker, but the purpose is to attribute fornication to the addressee. This intention is a hidden matter in the category of non-existence, so it is not considered. And there is a difference of opinion with Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal on this.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the inherent weakness (qusur) of kinayah: it has an apparent deficiency (qusur zahir) and explicit concealment (khafa' sarih) because its definition shows that the intended meaning is not certain. This creates a shubha (doubt) similar to non-existence, so hudud penalties—which are repelled by shubha—cannot be applied based on kinayah alone. Two examples: (1) Someone says to a slanderer 'you have told the truth' — this could mean 'your habit is truth' or 'you are truthful in the slander', so it is not clear. (2) Indirect expression (ta'rid) like 'you are not a fornicator' implying the addressee is one. Since the intention is hidden, it is considered legally non-existent. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal disagreed and may enforce penalties. This shows the Hanafi caution in hudud due to shubha.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or semblance that prevents the application of hudud punishments.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that implies a meaning but allows another possibility.
تعريض (ta'rid): A form of kinayah where words indicate one meaning but intend another, often to insinuate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not apply the hadd penalty for qadhf (slander) when a speaker uses kinayah or ta'rid?
A) Because the speaker must explicitly utter the slander.
B) Because kinayah creates a shubha that repels the hadd.
C) Because the Quran does not mention kinayah.
D) Because the listener is not harmed.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah is legally weak for hudud because it creates shubha; therefore, hudud are not applied.

The Waw in Conditional Divorce: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Analyzing whether the waw indicates conjunction or order in conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true basis of the Hanafi disagreement on the waw in conditional divorce and evaluate the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The dispute on the waw is not about its meaning but about the nature of conjunctive attachment.

The waw (و) is a common particle whose meaning might seem straightforward, but its legal implications are profound. In conditional divorce, a single phrase like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' can yield different rulings depending on how we understand the conjunction. Some attribute the difference to whether the waw indicates order or association, but the true cause lies deeper: the mechanism of 'atf (syntactic conjunction) and how the conditional suspends the divorces. This lesson unpacks that methodological dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed formulation
Consider the husband's statement to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced.' Note that the three divorce phrases are joined by waw.
Non-consummated wife: marriage not yet consummated, so only one revocable divorce is possible according to the Quran.
2
State the apparent basis of disagreement
Some scholars thought the difference (one vs. three divorces) stems from the waw's meaning: Abu Hanifa supposedly held it indicates order (tartib), the Sahibayn association (mu'iyyah). Recognize this as a common misconception.
This view was attributed to Abu Hanifa by some, but the author rejects it.
3
Note the author's correction
The author asserts that there is no dispute: the waw is for absolute conjunction (al-jam' al-mutlaq). The real disagreement lies in how conjunction works—specifically, the mechanism of 'atf and the attachment of the condition.
The author establishes that the waw's lexical meaning is agreed upon.
4
Contrast Abu Hanifa's model with the Sahibayn's
Abu Hanifa: in conjunction, the second element attaches via the first, creating ordered attachment. The divorces therefore descend in order. The Sahibayn: attachment is ordered but all share simultaneously; thus divorces descend at once.
Both models accept that attachment (ta'alliq) is ordered; the difference is whether the descending is also ordered.
5
Apply to the non-consummated wife
Because a non-consummated wife can only receive one valid divorce (the first), Abu Hanifa rules that only the first divorce takes effect and the rest are void. The Sahibayn rule that all three occur simultaneously, so she is divorced three times.
The thamarah al-khilaf: one vs. three divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بعد وجود الشرط، والحاصل أن موجب هذا الكلام المعطوف بالواو ذلك ولا دخل فيه لكون الواو للترتيب فلا يرد أن الترتيب إنما جاء لكون الثانية متعلقة بواسطة الأولى، والوساطة جاءت من الواو ، فصار لها دخل وقالا فيتعلقن كلهن دفعة
Appendix on matters of particles upon which revolve fiqh issues and the need for which is severe. Know that their realities are partial connectors by which two things and dependent meanings are linked in observation, so they are not independent in intelligibility, and they are not a pillar in speech except with an addition, for they are not understood independently, nor observed except dependently, as you have learned. They are of types, among them particles of conjunction absolutely. The issue of the waw: is it for conjunction, whether by association or order or the opposite in attachment to what the first is attached, as in conjunction of a singular to a singular or realization in the same matter, as in conjunction of a sentence to a sentence. It was said: for order specifically, and it was transmitted and famous from the Shafi'is and attributed to al-Shafi'i, but Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi strongly criticizes him. And this saying was attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, just as the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) are attributed with association, because of his statement in: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' for a non-consummated wife, she becomes separated by one, i.e., one divorce, and two are nullified according to him, and according to them she becomes separated by three. So it was imagined that this difference in this branch is built upon that difference concerning the waw. According to him, because it is for order, the three divorces attached in order and thus occurred similarly, and since the place is not suitable for occurrence of ordered divorces, she separated by the first only and the rest were nullified. According to them, because the waw is for association, all attached together, so they occurred similarly due to the place's suitability for non-ordered ones. But it is not so; rather there is no dispute that it is for absolute conjunction, so this construction is not correct. Rather, they only differed in this branch because the effect of conjunction according to him is the attachment of the latter (the conjoined) by means of the former (the conjunct), so order in attachment is established, so they descend in order, similarly because the conditional suspended is actualized according to him in the manner it was attached. So the effect of this speech is occurrence in order, and the waw has no role in it, so they remain so after the existence of the condition. The gist is that the effect of this speech conjoined with waw is that, and there is no role for the waw being for order, so it cannot be objected that order only came because the second is attached via the first, and the mediation came from the waw, so it has a role. And they (the two) said: attachment, even if ordered, but all share in attachment, and there is no order in the attached and descent after sharing in attachment, so the divorces descend simultaneously. Order in occurrence would only be necessary if it were in the attached, but it is not, for attachment is ordered only. And his statement 'the conditional suspended is actualized in the manner it is attached' is denied if he means actualization according to the verbal attachment from the perspective of syntax; but if he means actualization according to what the suspended is described by of association and order in existence, it does not benefit, for order here is in the verbal connection itself without order in the existence of the connected, as in the case of delaying the condition, where the three occur by agreement. And they distinguished for the Imam that if in the speech there is a meaning that depends on its beginning on its end, then all here depends on the condition, so they all attach simultaneously.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the meaning of the waw (و) in conditional divorce formulations. The focus is on the statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' for a non-consummated wife. Abu Hanifa rules that only one divorce occurs (the rest are invalid), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule that three divorces occur. Some thought this difference is based on whether the waw indicates order (tartib) or association (mu'iyyah). However, the author argues that there is no dispute that the waw is for absolute conjunction (al-jam' al-mutlaq). Instead, the real difference lies in the nature of 'atf (conjunction) and the attachment of the condition. According to Abu Hanifa, the conjoined items attach in order (the second via the first), so the divorces descend in order, but since the wife is non-consummated, only the first valid divorce takes effect. According to the Sahibayn, attachment is ordered but all share in it, so they occur simultaneously, and since the place is suitable for multiple non-ordered divorces, all three take effect. The passage also mentions a case where the condition is delayed (ta'khir al-shart) where all agree three divorces occur. This shows the complexity of Hanafi usul in deriving rulings from linguistic principles.

Key Terms
واو (waw): A particle of conjunction; its meaning is disputed: absolute conjunction (jam' mutlaq) or specifically order (tartib).
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction; the effect is that the conjoined item attaches via the conjunct.

Key Terms from Source Passage
