The Particle 'Min': Homonymy vs. Reduction in Hanafi Usul
Dispute over the primary meaning of 'min' in Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three contested meanings of the particle 'min' and evaluate the argument for homonymy based on tabadur.

Core Idea
'Min' is a homonym revealed by context, not a single forced meaning.

In Hanafi usul, particles like 'min' carry legal weight — their precise meaning can determine a ruling. This passage examines a classic dispute: does 'min' have one core meaning or multiple distinct meanings? The author argues for homonymy (ishtirak) using immediate understanding (tabadur) as evidence, and critiques attempts to reduce all uses to a single sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
List the three proposed primary meanings of 'min': tab'id (partiality), tabyin (clarification), and ibtida' al-ghaya (beginning of endpoint). Note which group advocates each.
Many jurists (tab'id), Fakhr al-Din (tabyin), majority linguists (ibtida' al-ghaya).
2
Examine the reductionist arguments
Each group tries to explain all usages under its chosen meaning. The author calls this forced (ta'assuf). Analyze the examples given: lease and taking dirhams.
In lease, 'min' marks the start; in 'akhadhtu min al-darahim', it marks partiality.
3
Understand the tabadur argument
The author claims that each meaning is immediately understood in its respective context without need for a paradigm. This points to homonymy.
Tabadur (immediate understanding) prevents treating any usage as metaphorical.
4
Reflect on the common concept objection
A hypothetical objector suggests a common concept; the author refutes it by showing that if there were a common concept, it would also be immediately understood, which it is not.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
How does this dispute affect legal interpretation? For example, in lease contracts, 'min' determines the start date; in sales, it determines partiality.
The Hanafi position accepts context-dependent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة من اختلف فيها فكثير من الفقهاء قالوا : إنها للتبعيض) فقط (و) قال (فخر الدين : للتبيين و قال (جمهور أئمة اللغة : لابتداء الغاية أي الشيء ذي الغاية (زماناً) كان أو مكاناً على الصحيح لا كما زعم البعض أنه لابتداء الغاية المكانية وأرجعوا معانيها واقعة في الاستعمالات (إلى ما ذهبوا إليه، والحق أن التبعيض والتبيين في نحو أجرت من شهر كذا إلى شهر كذا والابتداء في نحو أخذت من الدراهم (تعسف أما الأولان في المثال الأول فلأنه : من البين أن الغرض في الإجارة بهذا الكلام التحديد من ابتداء الشهر، وأما الأخير فلأن الأخذ حتى يتحدد (بل) كلمة من (مشترك بين المعاني (للتبادر) أي لأن الكل يتبادر في مواضعه، فلا احتمال لكونه مجازاً في أحدهما وحقيقة في الآخر، فإما أن يكون موضوعاً بإزاء القدر المشترك أو بإزاء كل والأول باطل، وإلا لتبادر منه فتعين الثاني. فإن قلت: لا احتمال هنا للقدر المشترك، فإن وضع الحروف لمفهومات جزئية ملحوظة بوجه كلي، قلت: أنه لا يضر، فالمراد أن يكون موضوعاً لإفراد المقدر المشترك بوضع واحد أو بإزاء أفراد كل مما ذكرنا من المفهومات الثلاثة بأوضاع والأول باطل وإلا لتبادر الأفراد باعتبار الاشتراك في هذا القدر المشترك، فتعين الثاني فتدبر وهذا أولى مما في «التحرير»، واستقراء مواقعها يفيد أن متعلقها إن تعلق مسافة كسرت وبعت فلابتداء ،الغاية وإن أفاد تناولاً كأخذت وأكلت فلإيصاله إلى بعض مدخوله، ثم ساق الكلام، فإنه يرد عليه أنه لا يلزم للبعض أن يكون متعلقه مفيداً للتناول كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَكَانَتْ مِنَ الْقَنِنِينَ ﴾ [التحريم: ١٢] فتدبر .
Issue: 'min' concerning which there is disagreement. Many jurists said it is only for partiality. Fakhr al-Din said it is for clarification. The majority of linguists said it is for beginning of the endpoint, i.e., a thing having an endpoint, whether temporal or spatial, as the correct view, not as some claimed that it is only for spatial beginning. They refer its meanings occurring in usage to their respective views. The truth is that partiality and clarification in phrases like 'I rented from such month to such month' and beginning in phrases like 'I took from the dirhams' is forced. As for the first two in the first example, it is obvious that the purpose in lease with this speech is delimitation from the beginning of the month. As for the last, it is because taking is delimited. Rather, the word 'min' is shared among the meanings due to immediate understanding (tabadur), because each is immediately understood in its context. So there is no probability that it is metaphorical in one and literal in another. It is either assigned to a common concept or to each individually. The first is invalid, otherwise it would be immediately understood from it, so the second is determined. If you say: there is no probability here of a common concept, because particles are placed for particular concepts considered in a universal way, I say: that does not harm. The intent is that it is assigned to individuals of the supposed common concept by a single assignment, or to individuals of each of the three mentioned concepts by separate assignments. The first is invalid, otherwise individuals would be immediate due to the sharing in this common concept. So the second is determined. Reflect on this. This is superior to what is in al-Tahrir. Induction of its usages shows that if its object is distance, like 'I broke' and 'I sold', then it is for beginning of the endpoint; if it indicates consumption, like 'I took' and 'I ate', then it is for delivering to part of its governed term. Then he continued the discussion, but it is rejected that for partiality, its object must indicate consumption, as in the verse 'And she was of the devout' (Quran 66:12) - so reflect.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a dispute over the primary meaning of the Arabic particle 'min'. Three views are presented: many jurists say it is only for partiality (tab'id); Fakhr al-Din al-Razi says for clarification (tabyin); the majority of linguists say for beginning of a goal (ibtida' al-ghaya), whether temporal or spatial. The author critiques the attempts to force all uses into one meaning, giving examples: in a lease contract, 'min' indicates delimitation (beginning of the month), not partiality or clarification; in 'I took from the dirhams', it indicates partial consumption. The author concludes that 'min' is a homonym (mushtarak) among these meanings, based on immediate understanding (tabadur) in each context. He argues against a common concept, showing that it is assigned to three distinct concepts. He also notes that induction shows a pattern: for distance, it indicates beginning; for consumption, it indicates partiality. He cites Quran 66:12 as a counterexample to a strict rule.

Key Terms
من (min): The particle 'min' is a homonym with meanings: tab'id (partiality), tabyin (clarification), and ibtida' al-ghaya (beginning of endpoint).
التبعيض (al-tab'id): Partiality; indicating a part of a whole, e.g., 'I took from the dirhams' meaning some dirhams.
التبيين (al-tabyin): Clarification; specifying a category, e.g., 'I rented from the month' clarifying that the month is the starting point?
ابتداء الغاية (ibtida' al-ghaya): Beginning of the endpoint; indicating the starting point of a length, e.g., 'I sold from this point'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that 'min' is a homonym (mushtarak)?
A) Consensus of the Companions
B) Immediate understanding (tabadur) in different contexts
C) A hadith from the Prophet
D) Qiyas with other particles

Key Takeaway
The particle 'min' is a homonym whose meaning is determined by context, not by a forced single core meaning.

The Ghayah Dispute: From One to Ten Dirhams
Analyzing different Hanafi rulings on an acknowledgment with a starting and ending amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Zufar, Abu Hanifa, and the Sahibayn on the ghayah in acknowledgments and evaluate the usul principles behind each ruling.

Core Idea
The ghayah (limit) in an acknowledgment may or may not be included, revealing deep usul principles of custom and mental delimitation.

This lesson examines a well-known dispute among early Hanafi scholars regarding an acknowledgment where the debtor says 'I owe from one dirham to ten'. The debate involves Zufar, Abu Hanifa, and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), each with distinct interpretations of whether the starting and ending amounts are included. The analysis reveals how usul principles like custom (urf) and the nature of limits (ghayah) are applied to resolve a seemingly simple phrase. By the end, you will be able to dissect the arguments and understand the Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the three opinions: Zufar (eight dirhams, both limits excluded), Abu Hanifa (nine dirhams, start included, end excluded), Sahibayn (ten dirhams, both included).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and the Sahibayn are the opponents; note the specific reasoning for each (Zufar: literal exclusion; Sahibayn: logical inclusion).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Zufar uses a linguistic analogy (al-Asma'i's challenge); Sahibayn argue that a non-existent cannot be a limit.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that custom includes the start and the endpoint is a mental limit; the rebuttal to Sahibayn emphasizes mental conceptualization.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome is the number of dirhams owed: eight, nine, or ten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع. في: له عليَّ من درهم إلى عشرة قال :زفر يلزم ثمانية لعدم دخول الغايتين) عنده المبدأ والمنتهى وحاجه الأصمعي وقال ما قولك في رجل قال : سني ما بين ستين إلى سبعين، أيكون ابن تسع سنين؟ فتحير زفر هذا إنما يتم عليه لو كان حكم بين ومن واحداً وتحيره ليس دليلاً عليه كما قيل إن تحيره كان لعدم مطابقة الاعتراض بمذهبه، لأن قوله : كان في من وهذا في بين (وعنده) يلزم في الإقرار المذكور (تسعة لدخول المبدأ بالعرف) وعدم دخول المنتهى لأنه غاية مدّ إذ لولاها لما تناول الإقرار العشرة، هذا وقد استدل لدخول المبدأ أن وجود الثاني مستلزم لوجود الأول لما بينهما من التضايف الموجب لوجودهما معاً، ولا وجود للدين إلا بالوجوب في الذمة، ويرد عليه وروداً ظاهراً أن الثاني معروض للثانوية، ولا يلزم من دخوله ووجوده في الذمة وجوده مع هذا العارض حتى يلزم وجود مضايف آخر، بل هذه الأوصاف يعتبرها الذهن ويجعل معنوناتها واقعة في الخارج، فلا يلزم من وجود معنون أحدها وجود معنون الآخر، وأفحش من هذا ما قيل إن وجوب التاسع يستلزم وجوب العاشر أيضاً، كما أن وجوب الثاني يستلزم وجوب الأول عنده، لأن تاسعية التاسع بإزاء ما تحته لا بإزاء ما فوقه حتى يستلزمه فافهم (وعندهما ) يلزم هناك (عشرة، إذ) العشرة، غاية، والمعدوم لا يكون غاية الموجود، وإلا وجد الشيء من دون غاية، فلا بعد من وجوده ووجوده إنما يكون لوجوبه لأن دين (فيجب) العاشر، فتجب عشرة لهذا الصارف عما هو أصل الغاية (قلنا) ليس العاشر غاية في الخارج، بل يكفي التعقل للتحديد) وجعله غاية، والحكم على ما هو محدود في التعقل بلزومه في الذمة فافهم.
Subsidiary issue: Regarding [the acknowledgment] 'I owe from one dirham to ten.' Zufar said: Eight dirhams are necessitated, because according to him the two limits (the starting point and the endpoint) are not included. Al-Asma'i debated him and said: What do you say about a man who says: 'My age is between sixty and seventy' – would he be nine years old? Zufar was perplexed. This [argument] would only hold against him if 'between' and 'from' were the same. And his perplexity is not a proof against him, as it was said that his perplexity was due to the objection not matching his school, because his [Zufar's] statement used 'in' [؟] and this [example] uses 'between'. According to him [Abu Hanifa], in the mentioned acknowledgment, nine are necessitated, because the starting point is included by custom, and the endpoint is not included because it is a limit of extension; otherwise, the acknowledgment would not have covered the ten. This is what he used as evidence for the inclusion of the starting point: that the existence of the second [the ten] entails the existence of the first [the one?] due to the relation of correlation (al-tadaa'if) between them that necessitates their existence together. And there is no existence of debt except by obligation in the liability. It is objected, obviously, that the second is subject to 'secondness', and its inclusion and existence in the liability does not entail its existence with this attribute, so as to necessitate the existence of another correlate. Rather, these attributes are considered by the mind and its references occur in the external world, so the existence of one reference does not necessitate the existence of the other reference. Worse than this is what was said: that the obligation of the ninth entails also the obligation of the tenth, just as the obligation of the second entails the obligation of the first according to him, because the 'ninthness' of the ninth is relative to what is below it, not to what is above it, so as to entail it. Understand. According to the two companions [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], ten are necessitated there. For ten is the limit, and a non-existent cannot be a limit for an existent; otherwise, the thing would exist without a limit. So there is no harm in its existence, and its existence is only due to its obligation, because [the word 'debt' implies obligation]. Thus the tenth is obligated, so ten is obligated, due to this diverting from what is the original [rule of] the limit. We say: The tenth is not a limit in the external world; rather, mental conceptualization suffices for delimitation and making it a limit, and the ruling applies to what is delimited in the mind as binding in the liability. Understand.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This is a dispute among early Hanafi scholars regarding an acknowledgment of debt where the debtor says: 'I owe from one dirham to ten'. The three positions are: (1) Zufar says eight dirhams are owed, because he excludes both the starting point (one) and the endpoint (ten), considering both as limits not included in what is owed. (2) Abu Hanifa says nine are owed: the starting point (one) is included by custom (the common practice in such expressions), but the endpoint (ten) is excluded because it serves as the limit for extension; without it, the acknowledgment would not have included ten. (3) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say ten are owed: they argue that the endpoint (ten) cannot be non-existent because then it would be a limit for something that exists without a limit, which is absurd. Therefore, the ten must be included in the obligation. The author (likely commenting on a text) then responds that the tenth is not a physical limit but a mental one, so it can serve as a limit without being included in the obligation. This reflects deeper usul principles about the nature of ghayah and the interpretation of utterances. The debate also includes a dialogue with al-Asma'i, a linguist, who challenges Zufar's position with an analogy about age. The author criticizes some arguments as fallacious, particularly the idea of correlation (tadaa'if) between numbers. The Hanafi school later favored Abu Hanifa's position (nine) as the relied-upon view (al-mufta bihi) in this scenario.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): An acknowledgment of a right or debt, which is binding in Hanafi law.
غاية (ghāyah): The limit/endpoint in an acknowledgment; debated whether it is included in the obligation.
مبدأ (mabda'): The starting point (one dirham) in the acknowledgment.
منتهى (muntahā): The endpoint (ten dirhams) in the acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the acknowledgment 'I owe from one dirham to ten', how many dirhams does Abu Hanifa hold are owed?
A) Eight
B) Nine
C) Ten
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on the acknowledgment 'from one to ten' is nine dirhams, derived from custom and mental delimitation of the endpoint.

The Iqrar 'From One to Ten': Dispute on Inclusion of Endpoints
Analyzes the iqrar acknowledging a debt from one dirham to ten

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on the iqrar 'from one to ten' and apply the concept of 'urf (custom) to determine inclusion of the starting point.

Core Idea
Custom includes the start; custom excludes the end.

This passage dives into a classic usul al-fiqh dispute about the interpretation of prepositions in an iqrar (acknowledgment). When someone says 'I owe from one dirham to ten', how many dirhams are owed? The answer depends on whether the starting point (one) and the endpoint (ten) are included. Hanafi scholars disagreed, and the reasoning involves deep analysis of language, custom, and logic. This lesson will guide you through the three positions and the arguments behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
First, note the three positions: Zufar says eight dirhams (both ends excluded), Abu Hanifa says nine (start included, end excluded), and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say ten (both ends included).
Remember that the iqrar acknowledges a debt, so the amount must be determined.
2
Understand the linguistic reasoning
Each position is based on how 'min' and 'ila' are interpreted. Zufar treats both as exclusive, Abu Hanifa relies on custom to include the start, and the companions argue that the endpoint must exist to be a valid limit.
Focus on the role of 'urf (custom) in Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Analyze the custom ('urf) argument
Abu Hanifa uses a custom-based argument: in everyday speech, 'from one to ten' includes the starting number but not the ending number (e.g., 'I ran from noon to sunset' includes noon but not sunset).
The text also mentions a correlation argument but refutes it.
4
Evaluate the endpoint (ghayah) reasoning
The companions argue that the end (ten) must be actual to serve as a limit; otherwise, the limit would be non-existent. The Hanafi response is that the endpoint is conceptual, not necessarily physically existing.
This is a subtle but crucial point in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The Hanafi school ultimately adopts Abu Hanifa's view of nine dirhams, because custom ('urf) is a valid source of interpretation in iqrar cases.
This shows how secondary sources (like custom) can override apparent linguistic meaning.

Branch: In 'I owe from one dirham to ten', Zufar said: eight are obligatory due to non-inclusion of both ends according to him, the start and the end. Al-Asma'i argued with him, saying: What do you say about a man who says: my age is between sixty and seventy, would he be nine years old? Zufar was perplexed. This only stands against him if the ruling between 'from' and 'in' was one, and his perplexity is not evidence against him, as it is said that his perplexity was due to the objection not matching his school, because his statement was 'in from' and this is 'in between'. And according to him (Abu Hanifa), in the mentioned iqrar, nine are obligatory due to the inclusion of the start by custom, and the non-inclusion of the end because it is an extended endpoint, otherwise the iqrar would not cover ten. And he has argued for the inclusion of the start that the existence of the second necessitates the existence of the first due to the correlation that necessitates both existing together, and there is no existence of debt except by obligation in the conscience. And it is refuted by an apparent refutation that the second is subject to secondariness, and its entry and existence in the conscience does not entail its existence with this accident until it necessitates the existence of another correlated. Rather, these attributes are considered by the mind and its significations are made to occur externally, so the existence of one signification does not necessitate the existence of the other. And more egregious than this is what is said that the obligation of the ninth entails the obligation of the tenth, just as the obligation of the second entails the obligation of the first according to him, because the ninth-ness of the ninth is in respect to what is below it, not in respect to what is above it, so as to entail it, so understand. And according to them (the two companions), ten are obligatory there, because ten is the endpoint, and the non-existent cannot be an endpoint for the existent, otherwise a thing would be found without an endpoint. So there is no distance from its existence and its existence is only for its obligation because it is a debt, so the tenth becomes obligatory, thus ten are obligatory due to this diversion from what is the original endpoint. We said: the tenth is not an endpoint externally, rather sufficiency of mental conceptualization for determination and making it an endpoint, and ruling on what is limited in conception as being obligatory in the conscience, so understand.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage discusses a famous iqrar case: someone acknowledges 'I owe from one dirham to ten'. The scholars differ: Zufar says eight dirhams (excluding both ends), Abu Hanifa says nine (including the start but excluding the end), and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say ten (including both ends). The text explains the reasoning: Zufar excludes both because 'from' and 'to' each exclude their endpoints. Abu Hanifa includes the start by custom ('urf') but not the end because the end is the limit of extension. The two companions argue that the end (ten) must exist to be a limit, so it is included. The commentator refutes some arguments, emphasizing that the endpoint is conceptual. The Hanafi fatwa follows Abu Hanifa's view (nine dirhams) in this case.

Key Terms
من (min): Preposition meaning 'from', its beginning is included by custom
إلى (ila): Preposition meaning 'to', its endpoint is excluded as a limit
غاية (ghayah): Endpoint or limit; in this context, the terminating number which may be conceptual
عرف (urf): Custom, a secondary source of law in Hanafi usul

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, custom ('urf) can include a starting point in prepositional phrases, but the endpoint is treated as a conceptual limit, not automatically included.

Divorce by 'Kayfa Shita': Delegation of Description or Essence?
Ruling on the husband's statement 'You are divorced as you wish' – a dispute in Hanafi usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Sahibayn positions on delegation of description (tafwid al-wasf) in divorce, and apply the usul principle regarding immediate creative statements (al-insha al-munajjaz).

Core Idea
Delegation of description does not delegate the essence; divorce occurs immediately with the minimum description, per Abu Hanifa.

This lesson explores a nuanced disagreement in Hanafi usul: when a husband says 'You are divorced as you wish,' does the divorce occur immediately or only when the wife wishes? The answer hinges on the usul principle of delegation of description versus delegation of essence, and on the nature of immediate creative statements. You will analyze the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and understand the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly what Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn hold regarding the husband's statement.
Abu Hanifa: one revocable divorce occurs immediately. Sahibayn: divorce only if wife wishes in the session.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Trace his reasoning: delegation of description is subsidiary to existence of described; an immediate creative statement must take effect; the lowest description (revocable) is determined.
Key concept: tafwid al-wasf far'un 'an wujud al-mawsuf.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Trace their reasoning: delegation of a necessary description requires delegation of the essence; otherwise separation of state from possessor occurs.
Key argument: ta'liq al-hal ghayr al-munfakka ta'liq li-dhi al-hal.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The author critiques the Sahibayn's inference and provides an alternative formulation that defends Abu Hanifa's view.
The author denies that attaching a necessary state requires attaching the essence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in rulings between the two positions.
Under Abu Hanifa, divorce occurs immediately and wife chooses only the type in a consummated marriage; under Sahibayn, no divorce unless she wishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع في قول الزوج : أنت طالق كيف شئت، وقع واحدة رجعية بدون المشيئة) من الزوجة (عنده) لكن في المدخولة تفوّض الأحوال الأخر كالبينونة الخفيفة والغليظة على مشيئتها ، وفي غير المدخولة لا يفوّض شيء، لأن المحل قد فات بوقوع الطلاق (ولا يقع عندهما ما لم تشأ في المجلس فإن شاءت في المجلس وقعت وإلا لا، لأن التفويض يتوقف على المجلس (له) أنه طلق وفوّض وصفه إلى مشيئتها و أن تفويض الوصف فرع وجود الموصوف) فيجب أن يقع ولا يقع مجرداً عن أوصافه بل موصوفاً بوصف ما (فتعين الأدنى) وهو الرجعية، وهذا غير واف لأنا لا نسلم أن تفويض الوصف فرع وجود الموصوف بالفعل، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون تفويض الوصف مستلزماً لتفويض الأصل، فلا يقع شيء، فالأولى أن يقرر هكذا إن حال هذا إيقاع الطلاق في الحال مع تفويض الأوصاف إليها، فينبغي أن يقع، لأن الإنشاء المنجز لا يتخلف الحكم عنه وإذا وقع فلا بد أن يقع مع صفة ثبتت له عند وقوعه بلا زيادة أمر ، وهو كونه رجعياً، فيصير رجعياً، والأوصاف الباقية مفوّضة كما كانت إن بقي المحل فتأمل فيه، فإنه إنما يتم لو لم يجعل كلمة كيف مغيرة عن الإيقاع إلى التفويض هذا ولهما أن تعليق الحال الغير المنفكة تعليق لذي الحال وإلا لزم الانفكاك، وههنا أحد الأوصاف لازم للطلاق، وقد فوضت إلى المرأة فيجب أن يفوّض نفس الطلاق أيضاً إليها (أقول) استلزام تعليق الحال اللازمة لتعليق الملزوم (ممنوع) لجواز كون حال أولى عند عدم المشيئة) فيثبت من غير اختيار منه ،ومنها فلا حاجة إلى تعليق الذات، فيه أن هذا الكلام لتفويض جميع الأحوال، وحاصله عدم الوجود قبل المشيئة منها وانتفاء اللازم موجب لانتفاء الملزوم ، فكون البعض أولى غير مضر وغير مثبت إياه، فإن ثبوته مناف لتصرفه، فإن قيل: يخصص تصرفه بما عداه قلت أي دليل على التخصيص، لم لا يبقى تصرفه عاماً وتفويض الأصل أيضاً ولا بأس به فتأمل فيه والمشهور في الاستدلال لهما أن الشيء الغير المحسوس أصله ووصفه سواء، فإذا تعلق الوصف بالمشيئة تعلق الأصل أيضاً، وهو غير تام، لأن مساواة الأصل والوصف في غير المحسوس مما لم يقم عليه دليل .
Branch: Regarding the husband's statement: 'You are divorced as you wish.' One revocable divorce occurs without the wife's wish according to him (Abu Hanifa). However, in the case of a consummated marriage, other states like minor and major irrevocability are delegated to her wish. In the case of an unconsummated marriage, nothing is delegated because the place is gone due to the occurrence of divorce. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not occur unless she wishes in the session; if she wishes in the session, it occurs, otherwise not, because delegation depends on the session. His proof: He divorced and delegated its description to her wish, and delegation of the description is subsidiary to the existence of the described. So it must occur, and it does not occur devoid of descriptions but rather described with some description, so the lowest is determined, which is revocable. And this is insufficient because we do not accept that delegation of the description is subsidiary to the actual existence of the described. Why is it not permissible that delegation of the description necessitates delegation of the essence, so nothing occurs? The better way is to state as follows: This is an immediate execution of divorce with delegation of descriptions to her, so it should occur because an immediate creative statement does not have its ruling postponed. And when it occurs, it must occur with a description that is established for it at its occurrence without additional matter, which is its being revocable. Thus it becomes revocable, and the remaining descriptions are delegated as they were if the place remains. So ponder, for it is only complete if the word 'kayfa' is not made to change from execution to delegation. Their proof is that attaching an inseparable state is attaching the possessor of the state, otherwise separation is necessary. Here, one of the descriptions is necessary for divorce, and it has been delegated to the woman, so the divorce itself must also be delegated to her. I say: The implication of attaching the necessary state requiring the attachment of the necessary condition (malzum) is denied, because it is possible for it to be a first state in the absence of her wish, so it is established without his choice, and thus there is no need to attach the essence. In it, this statement is for delegation of all states, and its gist is non-existence before her wish, and the negation of the necessary requires the negation of the necessary condition. So the fact that some are first is not harmful and does not prove it, because its establishment contradicts his disposal. If it is said: he specifies his disposal to other than it, I say: what evidence is there for specification? Why does his disposal not remain general and also delegation of the essence? And there is no harm in it. So ponder. And the well-known in argumentation for them is that in non-sensory things, the essence and the description are the same, so if the description is attached to the wish, the essence is also attached. But this is incomplete because the equality of essence and description in non-sensory things has no proof.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This branch discusses the ruling when a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced as you wish' (anti taliq kayfa shi't). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, one revocable divorce occurs immediately without needing the wife's wish. However, for a consummated marriage, the other types (like minor irrevocability, major irrevocability) are delegated to her wish. For an unconsummated marriage, nothing is delegated because the place is lost upon divorce. In contrast, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that no divorce occurs unless the wife wishes within the session; if she wishes, it occurs, otherwise not. The debate centers on whether delegation of the description (al-wasf) implies delegation of the essence (al-asl). Abu Hanifa argues that delegation of description is subsidiary to existence of the described, so the lowest description (revocable) is automatically determined. The Sahibayn argue that if a necessary description is delegated, the essence must also be delegated, else there would be separation. The author analyzes the arguments and points out weaknesses in each, ultimately leaving the issue with the phrase 'ponder' (tammal).

Key Terms
تفويض الوصف (tafwid al-wasf): Delegation of the description (type) of divorce to the wife's wish.
الملزوم (al-malzum): The necessary condition; the entity that is described.
الإنشاء المنجز (al-insha al-munajjaz): An immediate creative statement that takes effect at the moment of utterance without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs until she wishes.
B) One revocable divorce occurs immediately.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs immediately.
D) The divorce is entirely delegated to her wish.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, delegation of description (tafwid al-wasf) does not suspend the immediate effect of a creative statement; the divorce occurs with the minimum description, and only further descriptions are delegated.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Divorce with Delegation: 'As You Wish' – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute in Usul
Ruling on husband's statement delegating divorce type to wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between delegation of essence and delegation of description in Hanafi usul and state the practical consequences of the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
Delegating the description does not necessarily delegate the essence; the ikhtilaf turns on whether description can exist without essence.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over the husband's statement 'You are divorced as you wish.' Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce occurs immediately with the default description (revocable), delegating only the remaining descriptions to the wife. The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, argue that since description is inseparable from essence in non-sensory matters, the delegation must include the essence itself, suspending the divorce on the wife's choice. This dispute illustrates a fundamental question in usul al-fiqh: the relationship between essence and description in legal causes and objects.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: one revocable divorce occurs immediately without the wife's choice, and in a consummated marriage further descriptions are delegated to her. In an unconsummated marriage, no delegation remains because the subject (mahall) is already extinguished by the divorce.
The immediate occurrence is based on the principle that al-insha' al-munjaz (immediate act) produces its effect immediately.
2
Identify the opponent's view (the two companions)
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad: no divorce occurs unless the wife chooses in the same session; if she chooses, it occurs, otherwise not. The delegation (tafwid) depends on the session and encompasses both description and essence.
They argue that describing an imperceptible thing necessarily includes the thing itself.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the proof of the two companions: linking a non-separable state (like revocability) to the wife's volition requires linking the possessor of that state (the divorce itself). If the description cannot be separated, delegating it implies delegating the essence.
They use a qiyas-like argument: if the state is inseparable, then delegation of state = delegation of essence.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and commentator's refinement
Abu Hanifa's original proof is insufficient because it assumes description cannot exist without actual existence. The commentator suggests a refined formulation: the statement is an immediate pronouncement with delegation of descriptions; since the pronouncement is immediate, divorce occurs with the established description (revocable), and other descriptions are delegated if the subject remains. The commentator then challenges the two companions' proof by rejecting the entailment: it is possible that some state (revocable) becomes primary without choice, so there is no need to delegate the essence. However, the commentator ultimately leans toward the two companions' view as stronger.
The commentator's phrase 'I say' (أقول) introduces his own analysis and rejection of the entailment argument, but notes that the famous proof for the two companions (equality of essence and description in imperceptibles) is incomplete.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): under Abu Hanifa, divorce occurs immediately; under the two companions, it is suspended on the wife's choice in the session. The commentator supports the two companions' view, and later Hanafi fatwa relies on their position.
The relying (mufta bihi) position in the school is that no divorce occurs until she chooses in the session.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فرع) في قول الزوج : أنت طالق كيف شئت، وقع واحدة رجعية بدون المشيئة) من الزوجة (عنده) لكن في المدخولة تفوّض الأحوال الأخر كالبينونة الخفيفة والغليظة على مشيئتها ، وفي غير المدخولة لا يفوّض شيء، لأن المحل قد فات بوقوع الطلاق (ولا يقع عندهما ما لم تشأ في المجلس فإن شاءت في المجلس وقعت وإلا لا، لأن التفويض يتوقف على المجلس (له) أنه طلق وفوّض وصفه إلى مشيئتها و أن تفويض الوصف فرع وجود الموصوف) فيجب أن يقع ولا يقع مجرداً عن أوصافه بل موصوفاً بوصف ما (فتعين الأدنى) وهو الرجعية، وهذا غير واف لأنا لا نسلم أن تفويض الوصف فرع وجود الموصوف بالفعل، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون تفويض الوصف مستلزماً لتفويض الأصل، فلا يقع شيء، فالأولى أن يقرر هكذا إن حال هذا إيقاع الطلاق في الحال مع تفويض الأوصاف إليها، فينبغي أن يقع، لأن الإنشاء المنجز لا يتخلف الحكم عنه وإذا وقع فلا بد أن يقع مع صفة ثبتت له عند وقوعه بلا زيادة أمر ، وهو كونه رجعياً، فيصير رجعياً، والأوصاف الباقية مفوّضة كما كانت إن بقي المحل فتأمل فيه، فإنه إنما يتم لو لم يجعل كلمة كيف مغيرة عن الإيقاع إلى التفويض هذا ولهما أن تعليق الحال الغير المنفكة تعليق لذي الحال وإلا لزم الانفكاك، وههنا أحد الأوصاف لازم للطلاق، وقد فوضت إلى المرأة فيجب أن يفوّض نفس الطلاق أيضاً إليها (أقول) استلزام تعليق الحال اللازمة لتعليق الملزوم (ممنوع) لجواز كون حال أولى عند عدم المشيئة) فيثبت من غير اختيار منه ،ومنها فلا حاجة إلى تعليق الذات، فيه أن هذا الكلام لتفويض جميع الأحوال، وحاصله عدم الوجود قبل المشيئة منها وانتفاء اللازم موجب لانتفاء الملزوم ، فكون البعض أولى غير مضر وغير مثبت إياه، فإن ثبوته مناف لتصرفه، فإن قيل: يخصص تصرفه بما عداه قلت أي دليل على التخصيص، لم لا يبقى تصرفه عاماً وتفويض الأصل أيضاً ولا بأس به فتأمل فيه والمشهور في الاستدلال لهما أن الشيء الغير المحسوس أصله ووصفه سواء، فإذا تعلق الوصف بالمشيئة تعلق الأصل أيضاً، وهو غير تام، لأن مساواة الأصل والوصف في غير المحسوس مما لم يقم عليه دليل .
(Branch) Regarding the husband's statement: 'You are divorced as you wish,' one revocable divorce occurs without the wife's volition according to him (Abu Hanifa). However, in a consummated marriage, other states like minor and major irrevocability are delegated to her volition. In an unconsummated marriage, nothing is delegated because the subject (marriage) has passed with the occurrence of divorce. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not occur unless she wishes in the session; if she wishes in the session it occurs, otherwise not, because delegation depends on the session. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: He divorced and delegated its description to her volition, and delegation of description is subsidiary to existence of the described. So it must occur, and it does not occur stripped of its descriptions but with some description, so the lowest (revocable) is determined. This is insufficient because we do not accept that delegation of description is subsidiary to actual existence of the described; why is it not possible that delegation of description entails delegation of the essence, so nothing occurs? So it is better to formulate thus: This is an immediate pronouncement of divorce with delegation of descriptions to her, so it must occur, because the immediate act of creation (insha) does not fail to produce its legal effect. When it occurs, it must occur with a description that is established at the time of occurrence without additional act, which is its being revocable. So it becomes revocable, and the remaining descriptions are delegated as they were if the subject remains. Reflect on it, for it is only completed if the word 'how' is not considered as changing from pronouncement to delegation. This is their (the two companions') view: That linking a non-separable state is linking the possessor of that state, otherwise separation would occur. Here one of the descriptions is necessary for divorce, and it is delegated to the woman, so the divorce itself must also be delegated to her. (I say) The entailment of linking the necessary state to linking the entailed (the essence) is rejected, because it is possible that some state becomes primary when there is no volition, so it is established without her choice; thus there is no need to link the essence. In it: This speech is for delegating all states, and its conclusion is non-existence before her volition, and non-existence of the necessary entails non-existence of the entailed. So some state being primary does not harm nor establish it, for its establishment contradicts his act. If it is said: He specifies his act to other than it, I say: What evidence for specification? Why not leave his act general and also delegate the essence? There is no harm in that. Reflect on it. The famous proof for them (the two) is that for an imperceptible thing, its essence and description are equal; so if the description is tied to volition, the essence is also tied. This is incomplete because equality of essence and description in imperceptible things is not supported by evidence.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the husband's statement 'You are divorced as you wish' (أنت طالق كيف شئت). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that one revocable divorce immediately occurs without the wife's choice, because the delegation is only over the description (type of divorce), not the essence of divorce. The divorce is effective immediately, and since the description is delegated but the essence has occurred, the default (lowest) description—revocability—applies. If the marriage is consummated, further states like irrevocability are delegated to her choice; if unconsummated, no further delegation is possible because the subject (marriage) is gone. The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, hold that nothing occurs unless the wife chooses in the session, because delegating the description necessarily entails delegating the essence. They argue that linking an inseparable state (like revocability) to her volition implies linking the divorce itself. The commentator (likely 'Allamah 'Abd al-'Ali Bahr al-'Ulum, author of Fawatih al-Rahamut) presents Abu Hanifa's argument first, then responds to it, supporting the two companions' view. The final relied-upon position in the Hanafi school follows the two companions: no divorce occurs until she chooses in the session.

Key Terms
تفويض الوصف (tafwid al-wasf): Delegation of the description or type of divorce to the wife's choice.
الإنشاء المنجز (al-insha' al-munjaz): An immediate legal act whose effect is immediate, not suspended on a condition.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce, where the husband retains the right to take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No divorce occurs until the wife wishes.
B) One revocable divorce occurs immediately, and no further delegation is possible.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs immediately.
D) The wife has full choice over the type of divorce in the session.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'أنت طالق كيف شئت' turns on whether description can be delegated without delegating essence; the relied-upon position follows the two companions that no divorce occurs until the wife chooses in the session.

Temporal Adverbs in Divorce: Linguistic Rule and Legal Effect
Rulings on temporal adverbs in divorce statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic analysis of temporal adverbs (qabl, ba'd, ma') to determine the number of divorces that occur in different divorce statements, distinguishing between consummated and unconsummated marriages, and evaluate the objection to the necessity of both terms in the relation.

Core Idea
A 'before' relation demands both terms exist—so a future divorce after the marriage ends is impossible.

This passage examines how divorce statements using temporal adverbs like 'before', 'after', and 'with' are interpreted in Hanafi law. The key is the linguistic principle that these adverbs substitute for verbs and require a subject. The ruling changes depending on whether the adverb is attached to a noun or a pronoun, and whether the wife is consummated or not. We will unpack the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic rule of adverbs (zarf)
Adverbs substitute for verbs and need a subject (the thing described). Identify whether the adverb is attached to a noun or a pronoun, as this determines what it describes.
The subject of the adverb is the entity that is described by the temporal relation.
2
Distinguish noun vs. pronoun attachment
When added to an explicit noun, the adverb describes what follows that noun. When added to a pronoun, the adverb describes what precedes the pronoun (the referent).
This is the base linguistic rule; exceptions exist but are resolved by the principle that the adverb requires a subject.
3
Apply to 'one before one' (explicit noun) for unconsummated wife
The first divorce occurs immediately. The second is described as after the first, so it would be in the future. But the marriage ends with the first, so the second cannot occur. Result: only one divorce takes effect.
The adverb 'before' requires both events to exist; the future event cannot exist because the marriage is dissolved.
4
Apply to 'one before it one' (pronoun) for unconsummated wife
The pronoun 'it' refers to the first divorce. The second divorce is described as before the first, placing it in the past. Pronouncement in the past is effective as if pronounced now. Result: two divorces occur.
Because 'before the present' is past, and the law considers past pronouncement as present.
5
Analogize to 'with' and 'after'
'With' indicates simultaneity, so two divorces occur at the same moment (for unconsummated). 'After' with explicit noun gives two (second in past), with pronoun gives one (second in future, impossible).
The same logic applies: the temporal relation determines feasibility of the second divorce.
6
Note the exception for consummated wife
For a consummated wife, she can receive multiple divorces even if ordered. Therefore, in all forms, two divorces occur because the second is possible in the present or even in the future (if the marriage continues).
The capacity for multiple divorces avoids the impossibility issue.
7
Address the objection about 'before' not requiring existence
Some might argue that 'before' does not require the later thing to exist (e.g., today before non-existent tomorrow). The Hanafi response: 'before' is a relation (nisba) between two relata; the relation requires both to exist when asserted. In divorce, the second divorce must be a real event; the analogy fails.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of the relational nature of temporal priority in legal contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل الظروف
مسألة: (قبل وبعد ومع متقابلات فالأولان متضايفان والثالث مضاد مشهوري لهما (وإذا أضيفت كل منها (إلى) اسم (ظاهر فصفات لما قبلها و إذا أضيفت إلى ضمير فلما بعدها أي فصفات لما بعدها هذا منقوض بنحو : جاءني رجل قبل زيد غلامه، وجاء رجل غلامه قبله والتحقيق أن هذه الظروف لنيابتها عن الفعل تقتضي فاعلاً هو الموصوف بهذه الظروف، فهو قد يكون ظاهراً بعدها فهي صفات لما بعدها، وقد يكون مضمراً راجعاً إلى اسم قبلها فهي صفات لما قبلها (فلزم واحدة في طالق واحدة قبل واحدة لغير مدخولة) لأن الأولى وقعت في الحال مقارنة لإيقاعه، وقد وصفها بالقبلية على الأخرى، فلا بد من وقوعها بعدها في المستقبل فيلغو لفوات المحل (و) لزم (ثنتان في : طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة) لأن الأولى وقعت في الحال متصفة بقبلية الأخرى عليها فتقع في الماضي، لأن قبل الحال ماض، والإيقاع في الماضي إيقاع في الحال (كمع) كما يقع ثنتان في مع واحدة ومعها واحدة، لأنه إيقاع للاثنين دفعة في الحال بعكس بعد فإنه يقع في طالق واحدة بعد واحدة ثنتان، لأنه أوقع الواحدة في الحال، ووصفها بالبعدية عن واحدة أخرى فلا بد أن تقع هذه الواحدة الأخرى قبل الأولى في الماضي، والإيقاع فيه إيقاع في الحال ويقع واحدة في طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة، لأنه أوقع الأولى في الحال متصفة ببعدية الأخرى عنها، فهي في المستقبل، ويلغو لفوات المحل، وهذا بخلاف المدخولة فثنتان واقعتان عليها (مطلقاً) في الصور كلها، لأنها قابلة للطلقات الكثيرة ولو مرتبة وما قيل إن كون الشيء قبل غيره لا يقتضي وجود غيره) فإن اليوم متقدم على الغد المعدوم، فينبغي أن لا يقع في المدخولة بقوله: طالق واحدة قبل واحدة، لأنه إنما أوقع واحدة موصوفة بالقبلية على أخرى، ولا يلزم منه وقوع أخرى (فمدفوع بأن القبلية نسبة بين القبل والبعد (وتحققها فرع تحقق المنتسبين) وهوبديهي وإنكاره مكابرة.
Issues of Adverbs
Question: 'Before' and 'after' and 'with' are opposites; the first two are correlatives, and the third is a common opposite to them. When each of them is added to an explicit noun, they are attributes of what precedes them; when added to a pronoun, they are attributes of what follows them. This is contradicted by examples like: 'A man came to me before Zayd's servant' and 'A man whose servant came before him came to me.' The verification is that these adverbs, because they substitute for the verb, require a subject that is described by these adverbs. That subject may be explicit after them, so they are attributes of what follows; or it may be implicit referring to a noun before them, so they are attributes of what precedes. Thus, in 'You are divorced one (divorce) before one (divorce)' for an unconsummated wife, one divorce is necessitated because the first occurred immediately concurrent with his pronouncement, and he described it as before another, so that other must occur after it in the future, but it becomes ineffective due to loss of the subject. And in 'You are divorced one before it one (i.e., with the pronoun)', two divorces are necessitated because the first occurred immediately described as preceding another; so that other occurs in the past, because 'before the present' is past, and pronouncement in the past is pronouncement in the present. Similarly with 'with': two occur in 'with one' and 'with it one' because it is pronouncement of two simultaneously in the present. In contrast, with 'after': in 'You are divorced one after one', two occur because he pronounced one in the present and described it as after another; so that other must occur before the first in the past, and pronouncement in the past is pronouncement in the present. And in 'You are divorced one after it one', one occurs because he pronounced the first in the present described as after another, so that other is in the future, and it becomes ineffective due to loss of subject. This is unlike the consummated wife: two divorces occur in all cases absolutely because she can receive multiple divorces even if ordered. What is said that 'a thing being before another does not necessitate the existence of the other'—for example, today precedes non-existent tomorrow—so it should not occur in the consummated wife with his statement 'You are divorced one before one' because he only pronounced one described as before another, and another is not necessitated—this is refuted because 'before' is a relation between the prior and the posterior, and its realization is subsidiary to the realization of the two related things, which is self-evident and denial is obstinacy.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on divorce statements that use temporal adverbs like 'before' (qabl), 'after' (ba'd), and 'with' (ma'). The author first clarifies the linguistic rule: these adverbs, when added to a noun, typically describe what comes after them, but when added to a pronoun, they describe what comes before. The exceptions are discussed. Then the legal application: for an unconsummated wife, the statements 'You are divorced one before one' causes only one divorce because the second is impossible in the future. 'You are divorced one before it one' (with pronoun) causes two because the second is placed in the past. 'With' causes two simultaneously. 'After' causes two in the first form and one in the second. For a consummated wife, since she can receive multiple divorces, two occur in all scenarios. The objection that 'before' does not entail existence is refuted: the relation requires both relata to exist.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): An adverb of time or place that substitutes for a verb and requires a subject.
قبل (qabl): Before; a relative term indicating priority in time, requiring existence of both related entities.
بعد (ba'd): After; opposite of qabl, also a correlative.
مع (ma'): With; indicating simultaneity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One divorce occurs.
B) Two divorces occur.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) Three divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the effect of temporal adverbs in divorce depends on whether the adverb is with a noun or pronoun, and whether the wife is consummated, with the core linguistic principle that the adverb requires its subject to exist.

Synonymy as a Linguistic Reality and Its Usul Implications
Dispute on the reality of synonymy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on synonymy (taraduf) and apply the principle 'al-taraduf khilaf al-asl' to resolve ambiguity in a legal text.

Core Idea
Synonymy is proven by induction but is exceptional, so ambiguous terms default to non-synonymous meanings.

The reality of synonymy (taraduf) in language is disputed. Hanafis affirm it by necessary inductive knowledge, yet treat it as contrary to the default principle (khilaf al-asl). This lesson unpacks the argument and its practical consequence for usul al-fiqh: when a word could be a synonym or a metaphor, the metaphorical meaning is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the passage addresses a dispute (ikhtilaf) about whether two words can have identical meanings (synonymy). The Hanafi school affirms it; unnamed opponents deny it.
2
Analyze the opponents' argument
The opponents claim synonymy would be useless because one word already defines the meaning. Identify this as a claim of absence of benefit (nafy al-fa’idah).
This is a logical argument: if two words mean the same, the second adds nothing new.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi counter
The Hanafi response rejects that benefit is limited to definition. Note two sub-responses: (a) rhetorical benefits like assonance and polarity, (b) metrical benefits in poetry. Understand that induction (istiqra’) proves many such examples.
Examples from the Quran and poetry are cited as evidence.
4
Note the qualifying principle
Despite affirming synonymy, the author states it is 'contrary to the original principle' (khilaf al-asl). This means the default assumption is that each word has a unique meaning. Understand the methodological impact.
This is crucial for usul: when a term is ambiguous between synonymy and another meaning (e.g., metaphor), the non-synonymous interpretation prevails.
5
Derive the usul rule
Formulate the rule: if a word is possibly a synonym or possibly figurative/metaphorical, the latter is preferred because synonymy is rare. This affects interpretation of legal texts where two words may appear synonymous.
This is an application of the principle that what is rare (nadir) is not a basis for default interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (الترادف واقع) في اللغة (بالضرورة الاستقرائية كالتأكيد أي كما أن التأكيد واقع بالضرورة (خلافاً لقوم لا يعبأ بهم (قالوا) لو وقع لوقع من غير فائدة واللازم باطل، أما الملازمة فلأن الواحد قد حصل به التعريف للمعنى فالآخر أيضاً لتعريف هذا المعنى، و(لا فائدة في تعريف المعرف (قلنا غاية ما لزم انتفاء تعريف واحد بعد تعريف الآخر، ولا يلزم منه انتفاء الفائدة مطلقاً، إذ لا ينتفي التعريف أي تعريف كل (بدلاً على أن فائدته في المحسنات البديعية (لا تخفى على المتتبع ولا نسلم أيضاً أن الفائدة منحصرة في تعريف المعنى، وتلك المحسنات كالسجع) في قولك : ما أبعد ما فات وما أقرب ما هو آت ولو كان لفظ انقضى فقط مرفوعاً لهذ المعنى لفات السجع (وكالمجانسة، كقولك: اشتريت البر وأنفقته في البر) ولو أقيم لفظ القمح مقام البرّ فاتت ومنه قول العارف الكامل الشيخ ابن الفارض الحموي قدس الله سره : فطوفان نوح عـنـد نـوحـي كـأدمـعـي وإيقاد نيران الخليل كلوعتي ومنه ظهر فائدة أخرى من المحافظة على الوزن (وكالقلب نحو) قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَّكَ فكير[؟] دونه في فعظم[؟] (نعم) هو ) أي الترادف (على خلاف الأصل حتى إذا تردد لفظ) بينه وبين غيره كالمجاز فيحمل على غيره لقلته بالنسبة إلى أغياره.
Issue: (Synonymy is real) in language (by necessary inductive knowledge, like emphasis—just as emphasis is necessarily real. (Contrary to a people who are not worth considering. (They said) if it occurred, it would occur without benefit, and the consequent is false. As for the correlation, because one word has already defined the meaning, the other also defines that meaning, and (there is no benefit in defining what is already defined. (We replied) the utmost that follows is the uselessness of one definition after another, but it does not negate benefit absolutely, since definition is not negated, rather the benefit is in rhetorical embellishments, (which are not hidden from the observer. And we do not concede that benefit is restricted to defining the meaning. Those embellishments are like assonance) in your saying: 'How distant is what has passed, and how near is what is coming.' If only the word 'antaqaḍa' were used for this meaning, assonance would be missed. (And like polarity, as in your saying: 'I bought wheat and spent it on piety')—if the word 'qamḥ' were used instead of 'burr', it would be lost. And among it is the saying of the perfect knower, Shaykh Ibn al-Fariḍ al-Hamawi, may Allah sanctify his secret: 'The flood of Noah at my mourning like my tears, and the kindling of the fires of al-Khalil as my burning.' From it another benefit appears: maintaining meter. (And like tashbīh [؟]) towards His statement: 'And your Lord magnify' [Surat al-Muddaththir: 3]—its likeness is in [؟]. (Yes, it) i.e., synonymy (is contrary to the original principle, so if a word is ambiguous) between it and another such as metaphor, it is interpreted as other than it due to its rarity relative to its counterparts.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) about whether synonymy (taraduf) actually exists in language. The author asserts that it is a linguistic reality proven by induction (istiqra')—empirical observation of many examples. This is analogous to the reality of emphasis (ta'kid) in grammar. Opponents argue that synonymy would be useless because if two words have the same meaning, the second adds nothing. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, the benefit is not merely definition but includes rhetorical beauties like assonance (saj') and polarity (mujanasah). Examples from poetry and the Quran (verse 74:3) illustrate these benefits. Second, even if one definition becomes redundant, there are other benefits like metrical preservation in poetry. However, the author also notes that synonymy is 'contrary to the original principle' (khilaf al-asl), meaning the default is that each word has a unique meaning. Therefore, if a word is ambiguous between a synonym and a metaphorical meaning, the non-synonymous meaning is preferred due to its rarity. This reflects the Hanafi principle that language operates on the basis of literal meaning unless there is evidence otherwise.

Key Terms
الترادف (al-tarāduf): The phenomenon of two or more words having identical meanings from every aspect
السجع (al-saj‘): Assonance: the use of rhyming or rhythmically similar phrases, a rhetorical benefit of synonymy
المجانسة (al-mujānasah): Punning or wordplay using words with multiple meanings, another rhetorical benefit of synonymy
على خلاف الأصل (‘alā khilāf al-aṣl): Contrary to the original/default principle; here it means synonymy is an exception, not the norm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for the existence of synonymy?
A) Logical necessity
B) Necessary knowledge through induction
C) Consensus of grammarians
D) Quranic prohibition of multiple synonyms

Key Takeaway
Synonymy is a linguistic reality but is contrary to the default principle; thus, when a term is ambiguous, the non-synonymous interpretation is preferred due to rarity.

Substituting Synonyms in Composition: A Usul Dispute
Disputes the permissibility of replacing synonyms in syntactic contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on substituting synonyms in composition, analyze the Hanafi proof using the prayer example, and apply the concept of 'awāriḍ to determine when substitution is permissible.

Core Idea
Synonymy does not guarantee substitutability; accidental linguistic features can block composition.

This lesson delves into a key usul al-fiqh dispute: whether synonyms can be freely substituted in syntactic constructions. While some scholars argue for absolute permissibility, Hanafis hold that accidental features ('awāriḍ) like customary usage can prohibit substitution even within the same language. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to recognize when linguistic rules intersect with legal custom (urf).

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
Identify the three views on substituting synonyms in composition: absolute permissibility (Ibn al-Hajib), permissibility only within one language (al-Baydawi), and the Hanafi view that it is not always permissible.
2
Examine the key proof
The opponents argue using the example of substituting Persian 'Khuda-yi Akbar' for Arabic 'Allahu Akbar' in prayer. They claim meaning alone justifies substitution.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Follow the three Hanafi counter-arguments: (a) they accept linguistic permissibility but legal prohibition is separate; (b) mixing languages may be restricted; (c) even within one language, customary usage restricts certain collocations (e.g., 'salla ʿalayhi' vs. 'daʿā ʿalayhi').
4
Recognize the role of accidental attributes
Understand that the Hanafi position emphasizes that the correctness of composition depends on accidental features of the words, not just meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (يجوز إقامة كل مقام الآخر في حال التعداد اتفاقاً إما في حال (التركيب) مع العامل أو المعمول أو غيرهما من المتعلقات (فلا يجب) الجواز بل قد يمتنع هذا (وهو الحق وقيل يجب) الجواز ولا يمتنع أصلاً ( وعليه) الشيخ ابن الحاجب ، وقيل) يجب الجواز (إن كانا من لغة واحدة وإن كانا من لغتين يمتنع واختاره البيضاوي في المنهاج الحاصل أن المذاهب ثلاثة : جواز الإقامة مطلقاً وجوازها إن كانا من لغة فقط، وعدم إطرادها وإن كانا لغة المختار لنا أن صحة الضم والتركيب مع الغير من العوارض الطارئة على اللفظ (واتحاد المعنى لا يستلزم الاتفاق فيها فصح تركيب لفظ مع آخر من غير صحة تركيب آخر بمعناه معه من وهو  المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية (واستدل) بأنه لو صح القيام (الصح[؟] خداي أكبر عند افتتاح الصلاة واللازم باطل (وأجيب) أولاً بأن الحنفية يلتزمونه أي جوازه عند افتتاح الصلاة فبطلان التالي ممنوع (و) ثانياً (بأن المنع شرعي إن سلم المنع فإن الشرع لم يصحح اللافتتاح به والنزاع في الصحة لغة و) ثالثاً بأن اختلاط اللغتين لعله ممنوع لغة إلا بالتعريب فلا يلزم من امتناع صحة خداي أكبر (المنع في اللغة الواحدة وهذا غير واف، فإنه من قبيل المؤاخذات اللفظية، لأن له أن يقول لو صح لصح الله أعظم، فلا جواب إلا الأول منا والثاني مما عدانا أصحاب إطراد الجواز (قالوا: المعنى واحد وهو المقتضى لجواز الإقامة (ولا) حجر في التركيب لغة) إذ هو لإفادة المعنى، واللفظان فيها سواءان ومانعوه في لغتين فقط قالوا: لا حجر في التركيب إلا من لغتين (قلنا) قولكم لا حجر في التركيب (ممنوع) ألا ترى أنه يقال صلى عليه دون دعا عليه (خصوصاً) إذا كانا من (لغتين فإن الحجر فيه ظاهر ثم إن أصحاب الإطراد إن أرادوا الجواز بالنظر إلى نفس اللفظ دون الأمور العارضة لتم الاستدلال فإن وحدة المعنى مقتض ولا استنكاف في التركيب بالنظر إلى نفسها، وإن كان يمتنع بالنظر إلى ما ضم أو أمر آخر، ولا يتوجه الدليل المذكور لإبطال هذا، ويؤيده تجويزهم من لغتين مع أنه نقل الاتفاق على امتناع ضم اللغات المختلفة من غير تعريب، لكن النزاع على هذا يصير لفظياً فتدبر .
Issue: (It is permissible to substitute one for the other in the case of enumeration by agreement, but in the case of (composition) with an operative or operand or other dependencies, (then it is not necessary) that it is permissible; rather it may be forbidden. (This is the truth, and it was said that it must be) permissible and never forbidden, (and upon this is Shaykh Ibn al-Hajib. And it was said) that it must be permissible (if they are from one language, but if from two languages it is forbidden, and this was chosen by al-Baydawi in al-Minhaj. In sum, there are three schools: absolute permissibility, permissibility only if from one language, and non-universality even if one language. Our chosen view is that the correctness of conjunction and composition with others are accidental attributes that attach to the word, (and unity of meaning does not entail agreement in these, so the composition of one word with another is correct while the composition of its synonym with it is not correct. The [PAGE_237] third article on linguistic principles: (They argued) that if it were correct (the utterance) 'الصح[؟] God is Great' at the beginning of prayer, the consequent would be false. (It was answered) first: The Hanafis commit to its permissibility at the beginning of prayer, so the falsehood of the consequent is denied. (And) second: (The prohibition is legal if it is admitted, for the Law did not validate opening with that, and the dispute is about linguistic correctness. And) third: Mixing two languages may be forbidden linguistically unless Arabized, so the impermissibility of 'God is Great' in Persian does not imply (impermissibility in one language. But this is not sufficient, for it is a verbal criticism, because they could say: if it were correct, then 'Allah is Greatest' would be correct. So there is no answer except the first from us and the second from our opponents, the holders of universal permissibility. (They said: meaning is one, and that requires permissibility of substitution, (and there is no restriction in composition linguistically) since it is for conveying meaning, and the two words are equal in it. Their opponents restrict it to two languages only, saying: there is no restriction except from two languages. (We said: your statement 'no restriction in composition' (is denied). Do you not see that one says 'sallā ʿalayhi' (he prayed upon him) but not 'daʿā ʿalayhi' (he called upon him)?” (Especially) if they are from (two languages, for the restriction is apparent. Then, if the holders of universal permissibility intend permissibility with respect to the word itself without accidental matters, then the proof is established, for unity of meaning is a requirement and there is no aversion in composition with respect to itself, even if it is forbidden with respect to what it is conjoined with or another matter. And the mentioned proof does not refute this. This is supported by their permitting from two languages despite the transmitted agreement on the prohibition of combining different languages without Arabization. But on this the dispute becomes verbal, so reflect.)
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage addresses a technical usul issue: can a word be replaced by its synonym in a syntactic construction (tarkib)? The author outlines three positions: (1) absolute permissibility (Ibn al-Hajib), (2) permissibility only within the same language (al-Baydawi), and (3) the Hanafi view that it is not always permissible and may be forbidden due to accidental features. The proof revolves around the example of opening the prayer with 'Khuda-yi Akbar' (Persian) instead of 'Allahu Akbar' (Arabic). Opponents argue that since the meaning is the same, substitution should be allowed. The Hanafi response is multi-layered: first, they accept that it is permissible in principle, but the legal prohibition is another matter. Second, mixing languages may be restricted. Third, even within the same language, not all synonyms can be freely substituted; composition depends on customary usage (isti'mal), not just meaning. For example, 'salla 'alayhi' is used but not 'da'a 'alayhi' to mean 'pray for him'. Thus, the permissibility of substitution is a linguistic question of syntactical compatibility, not mere semantic equivalence. The author concludes that if one considers only the word itself, substitution is possible, but with accidental factors (like the operator), it may be blocked. This shows the Hanafi concern for actual linguistic practice over abstract meaning.

Key Terms
التركيب (al-tarkīb): Syntactic composition where words are combined with an operator (ʿāmil) or operand (maʿmūl)
العوارض الطارئة (al-ʿawāriḍ al-ṭāri'a): Accidental features that attach to a word, like customary usage in specific constructions

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because language restricts certain collocations by custom
C) Because the words are not true synonyms
D) Because the Prophet ﷺ forbade it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the permissibility of substituting synonyms in composition is not guaranteed by meaning alone; accidental linguistic features and customary usage play a decisive role.

No Synonymy Between Complete Definition and Defined
Proves that a full definition is not synonymous with the defined term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition for synonymy (identity from every aspect and singularity) to determine whether a complete definition and its defined term are synonymous.

Core Idea
A complete definition is not synonymous with the defined because it is compound and detailed, while the defined is singular and summary.

This lesson examines a subtle dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: Is a complete logical definition (ḥadd tāmm) synonymous with the term it defines? Some scholars argue yes, but the Hanafi school, with its emphasis on precision, says no. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyse definitions in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi view (no synonymy) and the opposing view (synonymy). Note that both agree synonymy requires conceptual identity from every aspect.
The opposing school is not named; they are 'some people' who see definition as mere word substitution.
2
Extract the Hanafi proof
The defined term conveys a single, summarized concept (unitary image), while the definition conveys multiple distinct details. This difference prevents identity from every aspect.
Key phrase: 'al-maḥdūd yadullu ʿalā al-ṣūra al-waḥdāniyya' vs 'al-ḥadd yadullu ʿalā ʿiddat ashyā''.
3
Apply the condition of singularity
Synonymy in Hanafi usage is restricted to singular words (mufrad). A definition is compound (murakkab), so it cannot be a synonym.
This is a key methodological point: definitions are not mere word substitutes.
4
Analyze the verbal nature of the dispute
The text notes that the dispute may reduce to a verbal one if parties define 'taraduf' differently—some condition singularity, others don't.
The author sides with the meticulous scholars (ahl al-tadqīq) who require singularity.
5
Conclude with thamarah al-khilaf
In usul, if a definition were synonymous with the defined, one could replace the term with the definition in legal reasoning. Hanafis reject this because of the structural difference.
This affects how definitions are used in istinbāṭ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (لا ترادف بين الحد التام والمحدود خلافاً لقوم قالوا: ما الحد إلا تبديل لفظ بلفظ أجلى منه والمفاد واحد وهو المعنى من الترادف لنا أن المحدود يدل على الصورة الوحدانية) أي على أمر واحد مجمل معلوم بالصورة الوحدانية (بخلاف الحد) فإنه يدل على عدة أشياء معلومة بصورة على حدة مفصلة فلا) (اتحاد بينهما من كل وجه) بل الفرق بالإجمال والتفصيل ولا بد في الترادف من الاتحاد من كل وجه، ومن هنا خرج الجواب عن دليل الخصم بمنع كون التحديد تبديل لفظ بلفظ أجلى (وما) ذكر في «التحرير») مع الإشارة إلى التمريض بكلمة اللهم إلا أن النزاع لفظي يرجع إلى اشتراط الإفراد وعدمه فيه) فمن شرط فيه الإفراد ينفي الترادف بينهما، فإن الحد مركب وليس بمفرد، ومن لم يشترط قال بالترادف (فممنوع) فإن الفريقين بعد الاتفاق على أن المرادفة يجب فيها الاتحاد بحسب المعنى من كل وجه اختلفوا في أنه هل يتحقق بين الحد والمحدود أم لا، فمذهب الذين رأوا الأمر في بادىء النظر قالوا : بذلك الاتحاد وأهل التدقيق قالوا : لا ،اتحاد كذا في الحاشية»، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أنه قد مر أن المترادفين من أقسام المفرد والقائل بالترادف بين الحد والمحدود إن سلم هذا لا يتأتى منه هذا القول، وإن كان المعنى واحداً من كل وجه أيضاً، وإلا آل النزاع إلى اللفظ البتة، فإن النافين نفوه بمعنى والمثبتين أثبتوه بمعنى آخر، إلا أن يقال إنهم ما رأوا الحد مركباً، وهو بعيد عن العقلاء، أو يقال النزاع في نفس اتحاد المفهومين هل يتحدان من كل وجه أو لا، وإطلاق الترادف مسامحة هذا والله أعلم بمراد عباده.
Issue: (There is no synonymy between the complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm) and the defined (al-maḥdūd), contrary to some people who said: 'Definition is nothing but substituting a word for a clearer word, and the outcome is one, which is the meaning of synonymy.' (Our proof: the defined indicates the unitary image) i.e., one single thing, summarized, known by a unitary image, (unlike the definition) for it indicates several things, each known in a separate detailed image. So (there is no identity between them from every aspect), rather the difference is between summary and detail, and synonymy requires identity from every aspect. From this comes the answer to the opponent's proof by denying that definition is replacing a word with a clearer one. (And what was mentioned in 'al-Tahrir') with a hint of objection by the phrase 'unless the dispute is verbal, referring to the condition of being singular or not.' So if one conditions singularity, he denies synonymy between them, because the definition is compound and not singular; but if one does not condition it, he asserts synonymy. (This is denied.) For both parties agree that synonymy requires conceptual identity from every aspect, yet they differ on whether this obtains between definition and defined. The view of those who see the matter in the first glance is that there is identity, while the meticulous scholars say there is no identity, as in the gloss. And you are not unaware that it has passed that synonyms are among the categories of the singular. The one who asserts synonymy between definition and defined, if he concedes this, cannot consistently hold his statement, even if the meaning is one from every aspect. Otherwise, the dispute becomes purely verbal. For the deniers deny it by one meaning and the affirmers affirm it by another meaning, unless it is said that they did not consider the definition as compound, which is far from the rational, or it is said the dispute is about the very identity of the two concepts—whether they are identical from every aspect or not—and the use of 'synonymy' is a loose expression. And Allah knows best the intentions of His servants.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This third mas'alah addresses whether a complete logical definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm, consisting of genus and differentia) is synonymous with the term it defines (al-maḥdūd). Some scholars argue that they are synonyms because definition merely replaces a term with a clearer equivalent, conveying the same meaning. The Hanafi position, grounded in precision, denies this. The reason: the defined term denotes a single, simple concept (in a unitary image), whereas the definition spells out multiple components in detail. Synonymy requires complete conceptual identity from every aspect (min kull wajh), which is not present here due to the summary/detail distinction. The text also notes that if we condition synonymy on the words being singular (mufrad), then a compound definition cannot be synonymous. The dispute may reduce to a verbal one if parties define 'taraduf' differently. The author sides with the meticulous scholars (ahl al-tadqīq) who deny identity, noting that even if the meaning is the same in content, the mode of presentation (ijmāl vs. tafṣīl) prevents synonymy. This illustrates the Hanafi commitment to conceptual precision in usul definitions.

Key Terms
الحد التام (al-ḥadd al-tāmm): A complete definition comprising genus and differentia, giving a detailed conceptual image
المحدود (al-maḥdūd): The term being defined, indicating a simple summarized meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the definition has more letters than the defined
B) Because the defined is singular and the definition is composite
C) Because the definition is always in Arabic and the defined may be foreign
D) Because no two words can be synonyms

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a complete definition is not synonymous with the defined term because synonymy requires singularity and identity from every aspect, which a compound definition lacks.
