The Permissibility of Non-Arabic in Prayer: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Usul dispute on mixing languages in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on using non-Arabic phrases in ritual prayer and apply the usul principle of linguistic permissibility (itṭirād al-jawāz) to analogous cases.

Core Idea
If the meaning is one, language cannot restrict worship—unless the prohibition is legal, not linguistic.

This lesson explores a classic usul dispute: can a worshipper replace the Arabic 'Allahu Akbar' with the Persian 'Khuday Akbar' when opening the prayer? The Hanafi school argues that since the meaning is identical, there is no inherent linguistic barrier. Their opponents (likely Shāfiʿīs) counter that mixing languages without Arabization is prohibited. We will unpack the Hanafi response—distinguishing linguistic from legal prohibition—and see how this dispute illustrates the principle that composition is a vehicle for meaning, not an end in itself.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the exact ruling under dispute: the permissibility of uttering a non-Arabic phrase (Persion 'Khuday Akbar') during takbīr al-ifitāḥ.
Both sides agree that 'Allahu Akbar' is the standard, but the question is whether a synonym in another language suffices.
2
Identify the Hanafi Position and Its Proof
The Hanafīs hold it is permissible because the meaning is the same. Their key claim: 'lā ḥajara fī al-tarkīb lughatan' (no restriction in composition linguistically); composition is merely a vehicle for meaning.
This is based on the principle 'itṭirād al-jawāz' – consistent permissibility when meaning is preserved.
3
Identify the Opponent’s Position and Proof
The opponents argue that mixing two languages in a single utterance is forbidden unless Arabized. They cite a purported consensus (ijmāʿ) on this point.
The opponent's proof is essentially: 'mixing different languages without taʿrīb is prohibited'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafīs offer three rebuttals: (1) they affirm permissibility directly; (2) the prohibition is legal (sharʿī), not linguistic, so the linguistic validity remains; (3) the opponent's claim of consensus is not absolute. They further argue that even if there is restriction in two languages, it does not apply to a single language.
The third response is deemed insufficient by the author, leading to a refining of the argument: the dispute becomes verbal.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilāf
Practical consequence: Hanafis permit prayer with 'Khuday Akbar'; opponents require repeating takbīr in Arabic, potentially affecting the prayer’s validity.
The debate ultimately centers on whether the restriction is linguistic or legal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية (واستدل) بأنه لو صح القيام (الصح خداي أكبر عند افتتاح الصلاة واللازم باطل (وأجيب) أولاً بأن الحنفية يلتزمونه أي جوازه عند افتتاح الصلاة فبطلان التالي ممنوع (و) ثانياً (بأن المنع شرعي إن سلم المنع فإن الشرع لم يصحح اللافتتاح به والنزاع في الصحة لغة و) ثالثاً بأن اختلاط اللغتين لعله ممنوع لغة إلا بالتعريب فلا يلزم من امتناع صحة خداي أكبر (المنع في اللغة الواحدة وهذا غير واف، فإنه من قبيل المؤاخذات اللفظية، لأن له أن يقول لو صح لصح الله أعظم، فلا جواب إلا الأول منا والثاني مما عدانا أصحاب إطراد الجواز (قالوا: المعنى واحد وهو المقتضى لجواز الإقامة (ولا) حجر في التركيب لغة) إذ هو لإفادة المعنى، واللفظان فيها سواءان ومانعوه في لغتين فقط قالوا: لا حجر في التركيب إلا من لغتين (قلنا) قولكم لا حجر في التركيب (ممنوع) ألا ترى أنه يقال صلى عليه دون دعا عليه (خصوصاً) إذا كانا من (لغتين فإن الحجر فيه ظاهر ثم إن أصحاب الإطراد إن أرادوا الجواز بالنظر إلى نفس اللفظ دون الأمور العارضة لتم الاستدلال فإن وحدة المعنى مقتض ولا استنكاف في التركيب بالنظر إلى نفسها، وإن كان يمتنع بالنظر إلى ما ضم أو أمر آخر، ولا يتوجه الدليل المذكور لإبطال هذا، ويؤيده تجويزهم من لغتين مع أنه نقل الاتفاق على امتناع ضم اللغات المختلفة من غير تعريب، لكن النزاع على هذا يصير لفظياً فتدبر .
The third article on linguistic principles. (And they argued) that if it were valid to say 'Khuday Akbar' at the opening of prayer, then the necessary consequence is false. (And it was answered) firstly that the Hanafis affirm it, i.e., its permissibility at the opening of prayer, so the falsity of the consequent is denied. Secondly, (that the prohibition is legal if granted, for the law did not validate opening with it, but the dispute is about linguistic validity). Thirdly, that mixing two languages is perhaps linguistically forbidden unless by Arabization, so the impermissibility of 'Khuday Akbar' does not necessitate prohibition in a single language. And this is insufficient, for it is a verbal objection. Since he could say: if it were valid, 'Allah A'zam' would be valid. So there is no answer except the first from us and the second from those who assert permissibility. (They said: the meaning is one, which necessitates permissibility of utterance. And there is no restriction in composition linguistically, for it is to convey meaning, and both words are equivalent. And those who prohibit only in two languages said: no restriction in composition except from two languages. (We said): your statement 'no restriction in composition' is denied. Do you not see that one says 'prayed upon him' but not 'called upon him'? Especially when they are from two languages, the restriction is apparent. Then if the proponents of permissibility intend permissibility with respect to the word itself without accidental matters, then the proof is complete, for unity of meaning is a requisite and there is no aversion in composition with respect to itself, even if it is prohibited with respect to what is attached or another matter. And the mentioned proof does not refute this. And it is supported by their permitting from two languages, although it is reported that there is consensus on the prohibition of mixing different languages without Arabization. But the dispute on this becomes verbal, so reflect.)

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul dispute) about the permissibility of saying 'Khuday Akbar' (Persian for 'Allah is Great') at the opening of prayer. The Hanafi position is that it is permissible because the meaning is the same. The opponents (likely Shafi'is) argue that mixing Arabic and Persian is not allowed. The Hanafis respond with three points: (1) they affirm permissibility; (2) the prohibition is legal, not linguistic; (3) mixing two languages is not inherently forbidden. The debate also addresses the issue of composition in two languages. The author concludes that if the proponents of permissibility focus on the word itself, the proof stands, but restrictions may arise from other considerations. The conflict reduces to a verbal dispute.

Key Terms
إطراد الجواز (itṭirād al-jawāz): The principle of consistent permissibility; proponents of permissibility argue that if meaning is one, then the same ruling applies regardless of language.
تعريب (ta'rīb): Arabization; the process of adapting a foreign word into Arabic morphology and phonology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi position in this lesson, what is the primary reason for permitting 'Khuday Akbar' in the opening of prayer?
A) Because the Hanafis believe Arabic has no special status in worship.
B) Because the meaning is the same as 'Allahu Akbar' and there is no inherent linguistic restriction.
C) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) explicitly allowed Persian in prayer.
D) Because the consensus (ijma) permits it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that language is a neutral vehicle for meaning permits non-Arabic phrases in worship as long as the meaning is preserved, unless a clear legal prohibition exists.

Inclusion of the Non-Existent in Legal Address: Hanafi vs. Hanbali
Hanafi rebuttal of Hanbali arguments for direct address of non-existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the inclusion of the non-existent in the legal address and evaluate the Hanbali arguments and Hanafi rebuttals.

Core Idea
The non-existent are bound by Shariah's generality, not by direct address.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: whether individuals not alive at the time of revelation (al-ma'dum) are directly addressed by Quranic and prophetic addresses. The Hanbalis argue they are, citing scholarly practice and the necessity of the Prophet's mission. The Hanafis offer a nuanced rebuttal, affirming the rulings' applicability to all generations but through the generality of Shariah rather than direct oral address. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school justifies the eternal relevance of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and Hanafi position
The dispute is about whether al-ma'dum (those not present at the time of revelation) are included in the direct legal address (khitab). The Hanafi position: they are not directly addressed, but the rulings apply to them via the generality of Shariah.
2
Analyze the first Hanbali argument and Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanbalis argue that scholars consistently use Quranic verses to argue with later generations, proving that the address includes them. The Hanafis respond that this practice does not require direct address; it relies on the generality of Shariah, which applies to all mukallafun.
3
Analyze the second Hanbali argument and Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanbalis claim that without direct address to later generations, the Prophet would not be sent to them. The Hanafis rebut: the Prophet's sending is realized by establishing evidence (dalil) that the rulings for later generations are the same as for the first.
4
Examine the third argument and rebuttal
The Hanbalis also argue that because internal speech (kalam nafsi) includes the non-existent, the verbal speech (kalam lafzi) must correspond. The Hanafis deny the necessity of full correspondence, distinguishing between immediate connection (ta'liq munajjaz) and conditional connection (ta'liq mu'allaq).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: Hanbalis view the Quran and Sunnah as directly addressing every generation, while Hanafis see them as addressing the first generation and applying to later ones via evidential extension. The Hanafi position is defended by logical distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الحنابلة (قالوا: (أولاً) لو لم يكن المعدوم مشمول الخطاب لما صح الاحتجاج به على شمول الأحكام إياه ولم يزل العلماء يحتجون به على من هو في أعصارهم) وكان معدوماً زمن الخطاب (وذلك) أي الاحتجاج المذكور منهم (إجماع على العموم قلنا يجوز أن لا يكون الاحتجاج لأجل دخولهم في الخطاب إرادة بل ذلك لعلمهم بعموم الشريعة) لكل مكلف موجود من زمن الوحي إلى يوم القيامة (وهو لا يتوقف على عموم الخطاب الشفاهي، و) قالوا (ثانياً : لو لم يكن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم (مخاطباً لهم لم يكن مرسلاً إليهم إذ لا تبليغ إليهم إلا بهذه العمومات ولا إرسال إلا بتبليغ أحكام الله تعالى (قلنا) عدم التبليغ إلا بهذه العمومات (ممنوع بل الخطاب (للبعض (شفاهاً وهم الموجودون زمن الخطاب (وللباقي بنصب الدليل على أن حكمهم كحكمهم وبه يتحقق الإرسال قيل : النظم القرآني يحاذي الكلام النفسي وهذا أي الكلام النفسي (يعم المعدوم) كما تقدم فلزم تناول اللفظي أيضاً للمعدوم، وإلا بطل التحاذي قلنا : المحاذاة ليس) واجباً من كل وجه ضرورة الفرق بين التعلق منجزاً كما في الخطاب الشفاهي (والتعليق) كما في الكلام النفسي، وإذا كان فرق بالتعلق والتعليق فيجوز الافتراق بدخول المعدوم وعدمه هذا.
The third discourse on linguistic principles: The Hanbalis said: (Firstly) If the non-existent were not included in the address, then arguing with it for the inclusion of rulings upon them would not be valid, yet scholars have continuously argued with it against those who were in their own times and were non-existent at the time of the address. (And that) i.e., the aforementioned argument from them (is a consensus on generality. We say: It is possible that the argument is not because they are included in the address intentionally, rather it is due to their knowledge of the generality of the Shariah) for every mukallaf existing from the time of revelation until the Day of Resurrection (and it does not depend on the generality of the oral address. And) They said (Secondly): If the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) were not addressing them, he would not have been sent to them, because there is no conveyance to them except through these generalities, and no sending except by conveying the rulings of Allah. (We say): The lack of conveyance except through these generalities (is denied, rather the address is to some orally (those present at the time of the address) and to the rest by establishing evidence that their ruling is like their ruling, and by that the sending is realized. It was said: The Qur'anic text corresponds to the internal speech, and this internal speech (includes the non-existent) as previously mentioned, so the verbal speech must also encompass the non-existent, otherwise the correspondence would be invalid. We say: The correspondence is not) necessary from every aspect due to the difference between immediate connection as in oral address (and conditional connection) as in internal speech. And if there is a difference in connection and conditionality, then it is permissible for them to diverge regarding the inclusion of the non-existent and its non-inclusion. That is it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a usul dispute about whether individuals who are not present at the time of revelation (المعدوم) are included in the legal address (الخطاب). The Hanbalis argue that if they were not included, scholars could not use Quranic verses to argue against later generations, but they do so, indicating consensus that the address is general. The Hanafis respond that the scholars' argument may stem from the generality of Shariah itself, not from direct address. The Hanbalis also argue that the Prophet's mission would fail without general address, but Hanafis say address to those present suffices; later generations learn through evidence that their rulings are the same. The Hanafis refute the correspondence between verbal and internal speech by noting differences in modality. The passage follows the author's dialectical style: present opponent's proof, then Hanafi rebuttal.

Key Terms
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): Those who were not existent at the time of the revelation or address.
الخطاب (al-khitab): The divine or prophetic address containing legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do the Hanafis explain the scholars' use of Quranic verses to argue with those not present at revelation?
A) They say the verses directly address all humans at all times.
B) They say it is due to the generality of Shariah, not the direct address.
C) They deny that scholars ever used verses that way.
D) They say it is only for those present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the non-existent are included in the Shariah's general rulings, not in the direct oral address, thereby preserving the universal applicability of Islamic law without requiring direct address to all generations.

Muslim Qisas for Dhimmi: Interpreting Umum and Khusus
Analysis of a usul dispute between Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i on qisas for a dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on qisas for a dhimmi, applying the usul principles of umum (generality) and takhsis (specification) to the Quranic verses cited.

Core Idea
General Quranic texts cannot be overridden by individual statements or weak hadith.

This passage examines a classical dispute between Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i on whether a Muslim may be executed for killing a dhimmi. The disagreement hinges on how to interpret the general verses of qisas in light of a contextual verse about Paradise. We'll walk through the proofs and counter-proofs, and see how the Hanafi school uses usul principles to defend its position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
State the exact fiqh question: Does qisas apply to a Muslim who kills a dhimmi?
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
2
Extract the Hanafi position and its primary dalil
From the passage, find the Hanafi view (no qisas) and the Quranic verses they rely on—especially the general verses and the contextual verse about Paradise.
Look for the verse 'Ashab al-janna hum al-fa'izun' (al-Baqarah 2: not exact but context).
3
Extract the Shafi'i position and its primary dalil
Identify the Shafi'i view (qisas applies) and the verses they use (al-Baqarah 2:178, 179; al-Ma'idah 5:45). Understand their claim that these verses are general and unqualified.
The passage states al-Shafi'i holds that the general verses are not opposed by the contextual verse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
See how the Hanafis argue that the contextual verse (about Paradise) specifies (yukhassis) the general qisas verses. Also note the weakness of the hadith and Ali's saying.
The author mentions the hadith of Ibn al-Baylamani is weak, and Ali's authentic statement is 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.'
5
Determine the tarjih and its usul basis
Conclude that the Hanafi position is preferred because general Quranic texts (umum) cannot be opposed by individual statements (aqwal) unless the specification is clear and strong, and the contextual verse provides that specification.
This illustrates the usul rule: al-amm yufidu al-wujub, but can be specified by a dalil that is qati' or strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلم بالذمي لعموم آيات القصاص من غير معارضتها هذه الآية وتلك الآيات، مثل قوله تعالى : {الحُرُّ بِالْحُرِّ} [البقرة: ۱۷۸] وقوله تعالى: {وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَاةٌ يَا أُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ} [البقرة: ١٧٩] وقوله : {النَّفْسَ بِالنَّفْسِ} [المائدة: ٤٥] (أو) أن عمومه بعدما خصص (يعم الدارين) من الأحكام (كما ذهب إليه الإمام الشافعي: فلا يقتل المسلم بالذمي عنده المعارضة الآيات الدالة على وجوب القصاص مع هذه الآية ولا حجية مع قيام المعارضة (والظاهر مع) الإمام أبي حنيفة لقوله تعالى في سياقها {أَصْحَابُ الْجَنَّةِ هُمُ الْفَائِزُونَ} ولا شك أن المراد الفوز الأخروي ولأن كون صاحب الجنة أو صاحب النار مما لا يدرك، فإنه موقوف على الخاتمة، وذلك مما لا يدرك أصلاً، فلا يدخل تحت حكم القاضي أنه من أهل الجنة، فلا يقتل بمن هو من أهل النار ، وإرادة الكافر ظاهراً من أهل النار والمؤمن ظاهراً من أهل الجنة الفائزون ( ولحديث ابن البيلماني) بالباء الموحدة واللام المفتوحتين بينهما ياء ساكنة من التابعين ذكره ابن حبان في الثقات وضعفه الدارقطني كذا في «التيسير» قتل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مسلماً بمعاهد وقال : أنا أحق بوفاء ذمته رواه أبو داود وعبد الرزاق والدارقطني عن ابن البيلماني عن ابن عمر مرفوعاً ، كذا في «التيسير» (ولقول) أمير المؤمنين (علي رضي الله تعالى عنه : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا، قال الشيخ ابن الهمام في فتح القدير: لم يجده المخرجون بهذا اللفظ، وروى الشافعي من طريق الإمام محمد بسند فيه أبو الجنوب : من كان له ذمة فدمه كدمنا وديته كديتنا، وقال أبو الجنوب : ضعيف وفي التيسير رواه الدارقطني أيضاً بسند فيه أبو الجنوب، ثم إن قول أمير المؤمنين يحتمل أن يكون وجه الشبه نفس حرمة الدم لا وجوب القصاص، فلا يصلح حجة، والذي ورد في الصحاح من قول أمير المؤمنين : لا يقتل مسلم بكافر، وهذا لو لم يخص دل على عدم اقتصاص المسلم بالذمي لكن الحق قول الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، فإن النصوص القرآنية العامة لا يعارضها قول أحد، كما لا يخفى .
The Muslim [is not killed] for the dhimmi due to the generality of the verses of qisas without these being opposed by this verse and those verses, such as His saying: 'The free for the free' (al-Baqarah 2:178) and His saying: 'And there is life for you in qisas, O people of understanding' (al-Baqarah 2:179) and His saying: 'The soul for the soul' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). (Or) that its generality after specification (applies to both realms) of rulings (as Imam al-Shafi'i held: so a Muslim is not killed for a dhimmi according to him due to the opposition of the verses indicating the obligation of qisas with this verse, and there is no authority with the existence of opposition). (And the apparent is with) Imam Abu Hanifa due to His saying in its context: 'The companions of Paradise are the successful' — and there is no doubt that the intended meaning is the otherworldly success, and because being a companion of Paradise or a companion of Hellfire is not known, for it depends on the final end, and that is not known at all, so it does not fall under the judge's ruling that he is among the people of Paradise, so he is not killed in retaliation for one who is among the people of Hellfire. And the apparent meaning is that the disbeliever is from the people of Hellfire and the apparent believer is from the people of Paradise who are successful. (And due to the hadith of Ibn al-Baylamani) — with the unified bā' and opened lām between them a silent yā', from the Followers, mentioned by Ibn Hibban in al-Thiqat and weakened by al-Daraqutni, as in al-Taysir — 'The Messenger of Allah killed a Muslim for a protected person (mu'ahid) and said: I am most entitled to fulfill his covenant.' Narrated by Abu Dawud, Abd al-Razzaq, and al-Daraqutni from Ibn al-Baylamani from Ibn Umar marfu'an, as in al-Taysir. (And due to the saying of) the Commander of the Faithful (Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: 'They only paid the jizya so that their blood would be like our blood and their wealth like our wealth.' Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said in Fath al-Qadir: 'The researchers did not find it with this wording.' And al-Shafi'i narrated via Imam Muhammad with a chain containing Abu al-Janub: 'Whoever has a covenant, his blood is like our blood and his blood-money is like our blood-money.' And Abu al-Janub said: 'Weak.' And in al-Taysir, al-Daraqutni also narrated it with a chain containing Abu al-Janub. Then the saying of the Commander of the Faithful may be interpreted as the similarity being the sanctity of blood itself, not the obligation of qisas, so it is not valid as a proof. And what is authentically reported from the Commander of the Faithful is: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' And if this is not specified, it indicates the non-retaliation of a Muslim for a dhimmi. But the truth is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, for general Quranic texts are not opposed by anyone's statement, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether a Muslim may be executed in qisas for killing a dhimmi (a non-Muslim under Islamic protection). The author presents al-Shafi'i's position first: he holds that the general verses of qisas (e.g., al-Baqarah 2:178, 179; al-Ma'idah 5:45) apply equally to Muslims and dhimmis, so a Muslim can be killed for a dhimmi. However, the author clarifies that al-Shafi'i's view is based on the idea that the general verses are not opposed by the specific verse about Paradise (which could differentiate the value of life). The author then presents the Hanafi position supported by several proofs: (1) The Quranic context mentions 'the companions of Paradise are the successful,' which indicates an ultimate otherworldly distinction; since final destiny is unknowable in this world, a judge cannot deem a Muslim to be of Hellfire, so a Muslim's life is not equated with a dhimmi's. (2) A hadith from Ibn al-Baylamani (though weak) states that the Prophet killed a Muslim for a mu'ahid (protected person), but this is weakened. (3) A saying of Ali (though not authentically preserved in that wording) suggests blood equality, but the author notes that the authentic report from Ali is 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' The author concludes that the truth is with Abu Hanifa because general Quranic texts cannot be opposed by individual statements.

Key Terms
عموم (ʿumūm): Generality of a text; here referring to the general applicability of qisas verses to all individuals.
خصوص (khuṣūṣ): Specificity; here the specific context or cause of a verse that may restrict its generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why is a Muslim not killed in qisas for a dhimmi?
A) Because the Quranic verses on qisas are abrogated.
B) Because the Muslim's life is considered more valuable due to his ultimate destiny in Paradise.
C) Because the dhimmi's life is not protected under Islamic law.
D) Because a hadith explicitly states that a Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position prevails because general Quranic texts (umum) cannot be opposed by individual statements, and the contextual verse provides a valid specification (takhsis).

The Generality of Mafhum al-Mukhalafa: Hanafi Critique of Ghazali
Arguing that mafhum al-mukhalafa has no generality because it is based on silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that mafhum al-mukhalafa cannot have generality because it is derived from silence.

Core Idea
Mafhum al-mukhalafa does not have generality because it is derived from silence, not from a word.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute between Hanafis and Imam al-Ghazali regarding whether the opposite meaning inferred from a conditional (mafhum al-mukhalafa) has generality (umum) over all unmentioned cases. The author analyzes Ghazali's denial of generality and critiques it, revealing that the dispute may be verbal depending on the definition of 'aam'. By working through this lesson, you will learn why Hanafis reject the generality of mafhum and how this affects practical legal derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Pinpoint the exact question under dispute: Does mafhum al-mukhalafa have generality (عموم) over all cases not covered by the uttered text?
The passage states the issue as whether the concept has generality for all that is not uttered.
2
Understand Ghazali's position
Recognize that Ghazali denies generality because he restricts 'aam' to words that encompass in the place of utterance; mafhum is from silence, so it cannot be general.
Some say the dispute is verbal, depending on the definition of 'aam'.
3
Grasp the Hanafi position
Hanafis negate mafhum entirely; if conceded, they still deny generality because generality is a property of words, and silence (sukut) is not a word.
The original principle (al-asl) of non-existence governs unmentioned cases, not the concept.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author offers two objections: (1) Attributing the ruling to the principle is a denial of the concept if it is conceded; (2) Sometimes the concept is existential, so it cannot be attributed to non-existence.
These critiques aim to show that Ghazali's attribution to the principle is inconsistent.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
In deriving rulings, Hanafis do not rely on mafhum al-mukhalafa; they require explicit textual evidence or other proofs for the unmentioned case.
This impacts fields like zakat, where a conditional statement does not automatically imply the opposite ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية مسألة ورد لمفهوم المخالفة عند قائليه عموم لجميع ما وراء المنطوق (خلافاً للغزالي) الإمام حجة الإسلام ( فقيل) : النزاع (لفظي يعود إلى أن العام هل هو ما استغرق في محل النطق) وبه يقول الإمام حجة الإسلام، فنفي العموم عنه (أو) ما استغرق في الجملة سواء كان في محل النطق أو غيره، كما يقول به الجمهور فأثبتوا العموم إذ لا خلاف لأحد من قائلي المفهوم (في ثبوت نقيض الحكم لا في محل النطق عموماً بل الخلاف إنما هو في إطلاق لفظ العام عليه، هذا بأن كلامه لا يساعده والظاهر من كلامه أن يبنى على عدم كونه لفظاً (و) قال (في «التحرير»: جاز أن يقول الإمام الغزالي بثبوت النقيض للمسكوت على العموم، وينسبه إلى الأصل لا إلى المفهوم) بأن لا يكون للفظ دلالة على ثبوت الحكم فيما وراء المنطوق لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، فيبقى المسكوت على ما كان قبل، فينتفي الحكم لعدم مقتضيه فلا يكون من العموم في شيء إذ لا بد فيه من دلالة وهذا كطريق (الحنفية) النافين للمفهوم بعينه (أقول أولاً : الكلام بعد تسليم المفهوم وهذا بالحقيقة إنكار له (و) أقول (ثانياً النسبة) أي نسبة ثبوت النقيض في المسكوت (عموماً إلى الأصل لا يصح، إذ ربما يكون المفهوم وجودياً) فلا يمكن إسناده إلى الأصل وهذا أيضاً لا يصح عموماً، فإن بعض الوجوديات أيضاً ينسب إلى الأصل، لكن لا يضر المورود والإيرادان لا يتوجهان إليه أصلاً، فإنه من أين علم أن هذا الحبر الإمام تكلم بعد تسليم المفهوم وعبارته المنقولة في التحرير من المستصفى النزاع عائد إلى أن العموم من عوارض الألفاظ خاصة أم لا، فإن من يقول بالمفهوم قد يظن للمفهوم عموماً ويتمسك به وفيه نظر، لأن العام لفظ تتشابه دلالته بالإضافة إلى المسميات والتمسك بالمفهوم ليس تمسكاً بلفظ، بل بسكوت فإذا قال في سائمة الغنم زكاة فنفي الزكاة عن المعلوفة ليس بلفظ حتى يعم أو يخص، ويجوز أن يكون حاصله أن القائل بالمفهوم يظنه عاماً ويتمسك به وفيه نظر فإنه يصح عندنا إذ ليس اللفظ هناك دالاً عليه كما زعموا بل هو تمسك بالسكوت، فإن المسكوت يبقى على ما كان والأصل في الأحكام العدم، فيلزم انتفاء الحكم، فلا يكون عاماً ولا يصلح للتمسك به، ثم ليس المقصود أن لكل كلام مفهوماً عاماً ينسبه إلى الأصل، بل
The third article on linguistic principles. Issue: It was asserted that the opposite meaning (mafhum al-mukhalafa) according to its proponents has generality for all that is not uttered (contrary to al-Ghazali, the Imam, Proof of Islam). So it was said: The dispute is verbal, returning to whether the general is what encompasses in the place of utterance – and this is the view of the Imam, Proof of Islam, so he negates generality from it – or what encompasses in the sentence, whether in the place of utterance or elsewhere, as the majority say, so they affirm generality. For there is no disagreement among any of the proponents of the concept (in affirming the contrary ruling not in the place of utterance generally, but rather the dispute is only in applying the term 'general' to it. This because his statement does not support it, and the apparent from his statement is that it is based on its not being a word. And he said in 'al-Tahrir': It is possible that Imam al-Ghazali says with affirming the contrary for the unmentioned on a general basis, and attributes it to the principle not to the concept, namely that the word has no indication of affirming the ruling regarding what is beyond the uttered, neither negation nor affirmation, so the unmentioned remains as it was before, so the ruling is negated due to the absence of its required factor, so it is not from generality in anything, for there must be indication in it. And this is exactly the way of the Hanafis who negate the concept. I say firstly: The discussion is after conceding the concept, and this is in fact a denial of it. And I say secondly: The attribution, i.e., the attribution of affirming the contrary in the unmentioned on a general basis to the principle, is not correct, because sometimes the concept is existential, so it cannot be attributed to the principle. And this also is not correct generally, for some existential things are also attributed to the principle. But it does not harm the objection, and the two objections do not target him at all, for from where does he know that this learned Imam spoke after conceding the concept? And his quoted statement in al-Tahrir from al-Mustasfa: The dispute returns to whether generality is a property of words specifically or not. For one who affirms the concept may suppose that the concept has generality and adheres to it, but it is debatable, because the general is a word whose indication is similar regarding the named items, and adhering to the concept is not adherence to a word but rather to silence. So if one says 'In grazing sheep there is zakat,' the negation of zakat from the fed sheep is not a word such that it is general or specific. And it is possible that the gist is that one who affirms the concept thinks it is general and adheres to it, but it is debatable, for it is valid with us, as the word is not indicative of it as they claim, but rather it is adherence to silence. For the unmentioned remains as it was, and the principle in rulings is non-existence, so the ruling is necessarily negated. Thus it is not general nor suitable for adherence. Then, the intended meaning is not that every speech has a general concept that is attributed to the principle, but rather...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a major usul dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Ghazali regarding mafhum al-mukhalafa (the opposite meaning of a conditional or qualifying statement). Ghazali is reported to have denied that the concept has generality over all unmentioned cases. The author first notes that some say the dispute is merely verbal: it depends on the definition of 'عام' (general). Ghazali restricts 'عام' to the place of utterance, while the majority (including some concept proponents) extend it to any encompassing indication. The author then cites al-Tahrir that Ghazali might only attribute the opposite ruling to the original principle (al-asl) of non-existence, not to the concept itself, which is exactly what the Hanafis who reject the concept do. The author critiques this by pointing out that if one concedes the concept, attributing the ruling to the principle denies the concept. He also notes that sometimes the concept is existential (e.g., commanding something implies its opposite is not commanded), so it cannot be attributed to the original non-existence. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the general is a property of words, and since mafhum al-mukhalafa is derived from silence (sukut), it is not a word and therefore cannot be general. The discussion is incomplete, indicating continuation.

Key Terms
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafa): The opposite meaning inferred from a conditional or qualifying statement, e.g., 'If grazing, then zakat' implies no zakat if not grazing.
عام (aam): General; a word that encompasses all its individuals, e.g., 'all men'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis argue that mafhum al-mukhalafa cannot be considered general?
A) Because it is derived from silence, not from a word
B) Because it only applies to existential rulings
C) Because it is always specific to the place of utterance
D) Because the Prophet never used it

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-mukhalafa does not have generality because it is derived from silence, not from a word, and generality is a property of words alone.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Dispute Over Generality of Mafhum
Analyzes whether mafhum can be considered general (amm) and nature of dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nature of the dispute over whether mafhum is a general term and explain the Hanafi position that mafhum is a rational necessity not subject to partial specification.

Core Idea
Mafhum is a rational necessity, not a general expression; it does not accept partial specification.

In this lesson, we unpack a key usul dispute: can the implied meaning (mafhum) derived from a statement be treated as a general term ('amm) that accepts partial specification (tajzi)? The Hanafi school argues that mafhum is a rational necessity (lazim 'aqli), not a linguistic expression intended by the speaker, so it does not have the properties of a general term. The majority of usul scholars, however, treat mafhum as a general expression that can be partially specified. This dispute has practical implications for legal derivation, especially when interpreting silences in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
First, recognize the two opposing views on mafhum: the Hanafi position considers it a rational necessity; the majority considers it a general term that can accept partial specification. Note how the author frames the dispute as meaningful (ma'nawi) rather than purely verbal.
The dispute is not merely about terminology but about the nature of mafhum as a source of indication.
2
Understand the Hanafi Proof
Examine the Hanafi example: the statement 'I do not eat' implies a concept of 'the eaten'. The Hanafis argue that this concept is a necessary rational consequence, not a generality intended by the speaker. The indication is by silence (sukut), not by wording.
This is analogous to how rational necessities like existence follow from a cause.
3
Examine the Majority Counter-Argument
The majority responds that if the indication to the mafhum is by convention (wad'), the speaker must intend it, so it should be treated like other general expressions. Also, the uniformity of indication across individuals makes it 'amm.
The objection raised in the text is that al-Ghazali's own words deny this interpretation, pointing to his focus on wording versus silence.
4
Analyze the Author's Resolution
The author offers his own analysis: the real dispute is whether the mafhum's indication to its individuals is uniform (like 'amm) or varied. He argues that if the indication is conventional, it must be uniform, making mafhum general. External variation does not affect generality.
He concludes that denying conventional indication effectively denies mafhum altogether, which contradicts the premise of the discussion.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis reject partial specification (tajzi) of a mafhum; the majority allow it. This affects how rulings derived from mafhum can be narrowed or applied to subcategories.
For example, if a mafhum implies no qisas for unintentional killing, can it be partially specified to exclude quasi-intentional? Hanafis would require a separate evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصود أن المفهوم لو كان ثبت بالسكوت لا بدلالة اللفظ فاندفع الثاني أيضاً، وعلى هذا فالنزاع معنوي مبني على خلاف آخر معنوي فتدبر (وقيل) ليس النزاع لفظياً (بل النزاع في أن العموم ملحوظ المتكلم فيقبل التجزي) والخصوص في الإرادة (أولاً) ملحوظ للمتكلم (بل) هو (لازم) عقلي) كالمأكول في لا آكل عند الحنفية فلا يقبله وهو مراد) الإمام (الغزالي) قدس سره، فالنزاع في العموم القابل للتجزي فأثبته الجمهور، وأنكره هذا البحر القمقام قدس سره، وإن تذكرت تحقيق ما قد سلف يعينك على فهم هذا (وأورد) عليه أن كلامه لا يتحمل هذا التوجيه حيث قال في ردّهم أي رد القائلين بعموم المفهوم لأن العام لفظ تتشابه دلالته، والتمسك بالمفهوم ليس تمسكاً باللفظ بل بسكوت فإن ظاهره أن المناط أن المعاني لا تتصف بالعموم لا كونه ملحوظ ،المتكلم وأيضاً يرد عليه أن كون المفهوم غير ملحوظ للمتكلم غير معقول على تقدير القول به، فإذا كان دلالة اللفظ عليه بالوضع كان المتكلم ملاحظاً له مستعملاً للفظ فيه فالعموم فيه لو كان قابلاً للتجزي والخصوص كما في سائر الألفاظ العامة وأيضاً الحكم على الشيء من غير اتصاف ما يغايره بنقيضه معقول، فلا يكون المفهوم لازماً عقلياً، ولو حرر كلام القائل بأن العموم استغراق يقصد من اللفظ ولا دلالة هنا للفظ، بل قد يفهم بالسكوت عن الحكم عليه انتفاء الحكم كما تفهم اللوازم العقلية لآل إلى ما في «التحرير»، ولا يرد عليه شيء (أقول) ليس النزاع كما زعموا بل النزاع في أن المفهوم هل تتشابه (دلالته على الأفراد فيكون عاماً فإن تشابه الدلالة معتبر فيه أو تتفاوت) الدلالة عليها (فلا يكون) عاماً والفحوى يجوز أن يتفاوت) في الانفهام فإن قولك في القتل العمد قود دلالته على عدمه في الخطأ تفاوت دلالته على عدمه في شبه (العمد فإنها في الأول أظهر دون الثاني (فافهم) وفيه نظر فإن الدلالة على المفهوم وضعي، ولا شك أن تساوي نسبة الأفراد إليه من لوازمه، فلا يمكن كون الدلالة على أفراد المسكوت متفاوتة، وإن كان التفاوت من خارج فلا يضر العموم، كما أن دلالة العام على سبب نزوله أقوى منها على ما سواه، فإن قيل : المقصود أن ليس دلالته عليه بالوضع فلا يتشابه قلت: هذا بالحقيقة إنكار للمفهوم، وقد كان على زعمه الكلام بعد التسليم فتدبر
The point is that if the concept (mafhum) were established by silence and not by the indication of the wording, then the second [objection] is also repelled. Based on this, the dispute is meaningful, built upon another meaningful disagreement, so ponder. (It is said) the dispute is not verbal, rather the dispute is about whether generality is intended by the speaker so that it accepts partial specification, or is it not intended by the speaker, but rather is a necessary rational consequence, like 'the eaten' in 'I do not eat' according to the Hanafis. Thus it does not accept [partial specification], and this is what Imam al-Ghazali intended. So the dispute is about generality that accepts partial specification: the majority affirmed it, and this great sea denied it. If you recall the investigation that preceded, it will help you understand this. (And it is objected) that his statement does not bear this interpretation because he said in refuting them, i.e., refuting those who affirm the generality of the concept: because a general term is a word whose indication is uniform, and clinging to the concept is not clinging to a word but to silence. So his apparent meaning is that the criterion is that meanings are not described with generality, not that it is intended by the speaker. Also, it is objected to him that the concept not being intended by the speaker is unintelligible on the supposition of affirming it. For if the indication of the wording to it is by convention, then the speaker intends it and uses the word for it. So generality in it, if it were to accept partial specification and particularity, would be as in other general expressions. Also, judging a thing without its opposite being described by its opposite is intelligible, so the concept is not a necessary rational consequence. If one refined the statement of the proponent that generality is comprehensiveness intended from the wording, and there is no indication here from the wording, rather it may be understood from silence about the ruling on it that the ruling is negated, similar to how rational necessities are understood, it would lead to what is in al-Tahrir, and nothing is objected to it. (I say) the dispute is not as they claim, rather the dispute is about whether the concept's indication to individuals is uniform, so it would be general—for uniformity of indication is considered—or its indication to them varies, so it would not be general. And the implication may vary in understanding: for your saying in premeditated murder, qisas, its indication to its absence in error varies from its indication to its absence in quasi-intentional killing—it is clearer in the first than the second. So understand. There is consideration in it, for the indication to the concept is conventional, and there is no doubt that the equal relation of individuals to it is among its necessities. So it is impossible for the indication to the individuals of the unspoken to vary. If the variation is external, it does not harm generality, just as the general term's indication to its occasion of revelation is stronger than to others. If it is said: the point is that its indication is not by convention, so it is not uniform. I say: this is in reality a denial of the concept, and according to his claim, speech would be after acceptance, so ponder.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about whether the concept (mafhum) can be considered a general term ('amm). The author begins by clarifying that the dispute is meaningful (ma'nawi), not merely verbal (lafzi). He presents two positions: (1) that generality is intended by the speaker, so the mafhum can accept partial specification (tajzi); (2) that the mafhum is a rational necessity (lazim 'aqli) not intended by the speaker, as in 'I do not eat' where the concept of 'the eaten' is not independently intended. The author attributes the first view to the majority and the second to Imam al-Ghazali. He then reports an objection to this classification, arguing that al-Ghazali's own words in rebutting those who affirm the generality of mafhum do not support this interpretation; al-Ghazali said that a general term is a word with uniform indication, but the mafhum is not a word. The objection also points out that if the mafhum is linguistically indicated, the speaker must intend it, so it should accept partial specification. The author then gives his own analysis: the real dispute is whether the mafhum's indication to its individuals is uniform (like a general term) or varied. He argues that if the indication is by convention (wad'), it must be uniform, so the mafhum is actually general. External variation does not harm generality. He concludes that the objection that the indication is not by convention effectively denies the mafhum altogether, which contradicts the premise of the discussion. The Hanafi position, as indicated by the reference to 'I do not eat', is that the mafhum is a rational necessity and not a general linguistic expression, thus it does not accept partial specification. This dispute has practical implications for how concepts are used in legal derivation.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The implied meaning derived from a statement, understood by context or silence (as opposed to mantuq).
العموم (al-'umum): Generality; a term covers all individuals of its category.
التجزي (al-tajzi): Partial specification; accepting that some individuals of the general term can be excluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, why does the concept (mafhum) not accept partial specification (tajzi)?
A) Because mafhum is indicated by a word, not by silence.
B) Because mafhum is a rational necessity (lazim 'aqli) not intended by the speaker.
C) Because mafhum has uniform indication like a general term.
D) Because the speaker never intends any generality in mafhum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes mafhum as a rational necessity rather than a general expression, which precludes partial specification—a key difference from the majority approach in usul al-fiqh.
